Divine Attributes

The First Proof: God as Creator and Willer of All Actions
Razi presents the first proof that God is the Willer of all actions based on His creation without coercion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first of Razi's two proofs that God is the Willer of all actions, based on His role as Creator without coercion.

Core Idea
God's creation of all actions without coercion proves He wills them.

Razi begins his defense of the Ash'arite position by establishing a foundational principle: God creates all actions of His servants. Since creation without coercion implies will, it follows necessarily that God wills every action, whether good or evil. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The passage is concise; each word carries weight.
2
Identify the key premises
Find the two premises: God is the Creator of all actions, and whoever creates without coercion is the willer of that thing.
The first premise is established earlier; Razi assumes it here.
3
Consider the conclusion
How does Razi conclude that God is the Willer of all actions? Reflect on why this conclusion is necessary.
Think about what 'without coercion' implies about will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا وجهان
الحجة الأولى : قد دللنا على أنه تعالى خالق أفعال العباد وكل من خلق شيئا لا على سبيل الإكراه والإلجاء، فهو مريد لذلك الشيء، فوجب القطع بأنه تعالى مريد لجميع أفعال العباد.
الحجة الثانية : أنه تعالى علم من أبي جهل أنه لا يؤمن. وقد بينا أن العلم بعدم الإيمان مضاد لوجود الإيمان. وعند قيام أحد الضدين كان الضد الثاني ممتنع الوجود بذاته. فإذن إيمان أبي جهل ممتنع الحصول مع ذلك العلم. وكل المعلومات معلوم لله تعالى. فالله تعالى لا بد وأن يكون عالما بامتناع وجود هذا يدل على أنه سبحانه وتعالى يمتنع أن يكون مريدا لصدور الإيمان عن الكافر.
We have two aspects. The first proof: We have demonstrated that He, exalted, is the Creator of the actions of the servants, and whoever creates a thing not by way of coercion and compulsion, then He is the willer of that thing. So it is necessarily conclusive that He, exalted, is the Willer of all the actions of the servants. The second proof: He, exalted, knew of Abu Jahl that he would not believe. And we have explained that knowledge of the non-existence of belief is contrary to the existence of belief. And when one of the two contraries stands, the second contrary is impossible in its essence. Thus, the faith of Abu Jahl is impossible of occurrence together with that knowledge. And all known things are known to God, exalted. So God, exalted, must necessarily be knowing of the impossibility of the existence of this, and that indicates that He, glorified and exalted, is unable to will the occurrence of faith from the disbeliever.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage contains two proofs (hujjatān) for the Ash'arite doctrine. The first relies on God's being the creator of all actions without compulsion. Since He creates all actions of His servants voluntarily (not under coercion), He must be the willer of those actions. The second proof uses the example of Abu Jahl: God knew he would not believe. Knowledge of non-existence contradicts existence. Therefore, with that knowledge, Abu Jahl's faith is impossible. God knows this impossibility. This knowledge of impossibility implies that God cannot will something impossible; hence He does not will faith from the disbeliever. The conclusion is that God wills all actions, even disbelief, in accordance with His knowledge.

Key Terms
خالق
أفعال العباد
إكراه
إلجاء
مراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will is only for good actions.
Correction: The proof shows God's will covers all actions, including evil, because He creates them without coercion.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the first proof that God is the Willer of all actions?
A) God's knowledge of future events.
B) God's creation of actions without compulsion.
C) God's command to do good.
D) God's pleasure with the actions.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of all actions without coercion establishes His will over all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God wills every action you do—including your sins—how does that change the way you view your own accountability? Does it make you more desperate for His mercy, or does it tempt you to excuse yourself? This week, commit to one specific sin you will repent from, fully aware that God willed it but also wills your return to Him.
?
The proof says God creates all actions without coercion. Reflect on a time you felt you acted freely but later saw God’s hand in it. How does this understanding affect your trust in His plan for your life? Write down one area where you struggle to submit to His will, and pray for the certainty that He wills what is best.

The Second Proof: Knowledge of Non-Belief Implies Impossibility
Razi presents the second proof using the example of Abu Jahl to show that God's knowledge of non-belief makes belief impossible, thus God does not will it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that God is the Willer of all actions, using the example of Abu Jahl to show that God's knowledge makes belief impossible.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of disbelief makes belief impossible, so He cannot will it.

Razi's second proof uses a concrete example: Abu Jahl. God knew he would never believe. Razi argues that such knowledge is contrary to the existence of belief, rendering belief impossible. Since God knows all things, He knows this impossibility, and thus cannot will what is impossible. This reinforces that God's will encompasses all actions, including disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the example of Abu Jahl and the logical steps.
This proof is longer; follow the argument carefully.
2
Identify the key premises
Find the premises: God's knowledge of Abu Jahl's disbelief, and that knowledge of non-existence is contrary to existence.
Contraries cannot coexist.
3
Understand the implication
Why does God's knowledge make Abu Jahl's faith impossible? Because with one contrary (knowledge of non-faith), the other (faith) cannot exist.
The impossibility is inherent in the situation.
4
Draw the conclusion
God knows this impossibility, so He cannot will the impossible. Thus He does not will faith from the disbeliever.
This supports the Ash'arite view that God wills all actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا وجهان
الحجة الأولى : قد دللنا على أنه تعالى خالق أفعال العباد وكل من خلق شيئا لا على سبيل الإكراه والإلجاء، فهو مريد لذلك الشيء، فوجب القطع بأنه تعالى مريد لجميع أفعال العباد.
الحجة الثانية : أنه تعالى علم من أبي جهل أنه لا يؤمن. وقد بينا أن العلم بعدم الإيمان مضاد لوجود الإيمان. وعند قيام أحد الضدين كان الضد الثاني ممتنع الوجود بذاته. فإذن إيمان أبي جهل ممتنع الحصول مع ذلك العلم. وكل المعلومات معلوم لله تعالى. فالله تعالى لا بد وأن يكون عالما بامتناع وجود هذا يدل على أنه سبحانه وتعالى يمتنع أن يكون مريدا لصدور الإيمان عن الكافر.
We have two aspects. The first proof: We have demonstrated that He, exalted, is the Creator of the actions of the servants, and whoever creates a thing not by way of coercion and compulsion, then He is the willer of that thing. So it is necessarily conclusive that He, exalted, is the Willer of all the actions of the servants. The second proof: He, exalted, knew of Abu Jahl that he would not believe. And we have explained that knowledge of the non-existence of belief is contrary to the existence of belief. And when one of the two contraries stands, the second contrary is impossible in its essence. Thus, the faith of Abu Jahl is impossible of occurrence together with that knowledge. And all known things are known to God, exalted. So God, exalted, must necessarily be knowing of the impossibility of the existence of this, and that indicates that He, glorified and exalted, is unable to will the occurrence of faith from the disbeliever.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage contains two proofs (hujjatān) for the Ash'arite doctrine. The first relies on God's being the creator of all actions without compulsion. Since He creates all actions of His servants voluntarily (not under coercion), He must be the willer of those actions. The second proof uses the example of Abu Jahl: God knew he would not believe. Knowledge of non-existence contradicts existence. Therefore, with that knowledge, Abu Jahl's faith is impossible. God knows this impossibility. This knowledge of impossibility implies that God cannot will something impossible; hence He does not will faith from the disbeliever. The conclusion is that God wills all actions, even disbelief, in accordance with His knowledge.

Key Terms
علم
أبي جهل
إيمان
ضد
امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will is separate from His knowledge, so He could will belief even if He knows it won't happen.
Correction: Razi shows that God's knowledge makes the opposite impossible, so His will aligns with what is possible.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that Abu Jahl's faith is impossible?
A) Because Abu Jahl was inherently evil.
B) Because God's knowledge of his disbelief is contrary to belief.
C) Because God did not create the ability to believe.
D) Because Abu Jahl refused to believe.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of disbelief makes belief impossible, showing He does not will the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's knowledge of your future disbelief would make it impossible for you to believe, do you feel a sense of helplessness or a deeper reliance on His mercy? How does this truth change the way you approach your faith daily?
?
Razi argues that God does not will the impossible, including the faith of Abu Jahl. Who in your life do you assume will never believe? Does this lesson make you question whether you should still pray for them, or does it free you from false hope? What step will you take today to align your du'as with divine will?
?
If your own faith depends on God's will and not your own effort, what does that mean for your moments of doubt? Have you ever considered that your very desire to believe is a gift from God, not a product of your own choice? How does this lesson move you to thank Allah for His gift of iman?

The First Three Mu'tazilite Objections and Responses
First three objections: command implies will, willing evil is foolish, obedience is fulfilling will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three Mu'tazilite objections against God's universal will and the Ash'arite responses to each.

Core Idea
Command does not imply will; evil is not inherently bad; obedience is to command, not will.

This lesson introduces the debate between the Mu'tazila and Ash'aris over God's will for disbelief. The Mu'tazila present logical objections, claiming that willing evil is absurd and that command implies will. The Ash'ari response clarifies key distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the gist.
Notice that the objections are numbered as 'shubha'.
2
Identify each objection and its answer
There are three objections (shubah) and three answers (ajwiba). Match each objection with its corresponding answer in the text.
The answers start with 'Wa an al-shubha...'
3
Focus on the distinction between command and will
The first answer denies that command entails will. This is the core of the dispute. Understand why the Mu'tazila assumed connection and the Ash'ari rejection.
This distinction is fundamental to Ash'ari theology.
4
Reflect on the principle of moral judgments
The second answer states that evil is defined by revelation, not reason. This is a major Ash'ari principle. Think about its implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة فقد احتجوا على قولهم بوجوه : الشبهة الأولى : إنه تعالى أمر الكافر بالإيمان وكل من أمر بشيء فهو مريد لوجود المأمور به فيلزم أن يكون الله تعالى أراد الإيمان من الكافر. الشبهة الثانية : إرادة الكفر والفسق سفه، والسفه لا يليق بالحكيم. وأيضا : إن منه الكفر والفسق ، ثم إنه يعاقب عليهما كان ذلك غاية السفاهة. الشبهة الثالثة : الطاعة عبارة عن تحصيل مراد المطاع فلو أراد الله تعالى الكفر من الكافرين لكان الكافر مطيعا لله تعالى بكفره وذلك باطل بالاتفاق. الشبهة الرابعة : الرضا بقضاء الله تعالى واجب بالإجماع، فلو كان الكفر بقضاء الله تعالى لوجب الرضا بالكفر، لكن هذا باطل لأن الرضا بالكفر كفر. الشبهة الخامسة : لو أراد الله تعالى الكفر من الكافر، وخلق الكفر في الكافر، لكان تكليف الكافر بالإيمان تكليف بما لا يطاق. وهذا باطل بالعقل والسمع. أما العقل : فلأن كل إنسان يجد بالضرورة من نفسه أنه إن أراد الطاعة، يمكنه فعلها فكيف يقال : إنه غير متمكن من الطاعة، أنه يجد بالضرورة ثبوت هذه مع المكنة؟ أما السمع : فقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَمَاذَا عَلَيْهِمْ لَو ءَامَنُوا بِالله ا [النساء : ٣٩] وفا هم عَنِ التذكرة مُعرِضِينَ ﴾ [المدثر : ٤٩] وكذا القول في جملة الآيات الدالة على أن الإنسان غير عاجز عن الإتيان بالطاعات. [؟] الشبهة السادسة : التمسك بقوله تعالى : ﴿وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الكفر [الزمر : ٧] وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلّمًا لِلْعِبَادِ الله [غافر: ٣١] وبقوله يَهْدِي مَن يَشَاءُ [البقرة : ١٤٢] . والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : لا نسلم أن الأمر بالشيء يستدعي إرادة المأمور به فإن النزاع ما وقع إلا فيه. وعن الشبهة الثانية إنا سنقيم الدلالة القاطعة - إن شاء الله تعالى - على أن القبيح لا يقبح إلا بالشرع، وعلى هذا الأصل فإنه لا يقبح من الله تعالى شيء. وعن الشبهة الثالثة : إن الطاعة موافقة للأمر لا موافقة للإرادة. وعن الشبهة الرابعة : القضاء صفة الله ونحن راضون بقضاء الله تعالى. أي راضون بصفة الله تعالى، وأما الكفر فليس هو نفس قضاء الله تعالى، بل هو مقتضي قضاء الله تعالى ولم يدل الدليل على أنه واجب الرضى بكل شيء قضى الله تعالى به. وعن الشبهة الخامسة : المعارضة بالوجوه الستة التي ذكرناها في خلق الأفعال. وعن الشبهة السادسة : إن الرضا والمحبة ترك الاعتراض، والكفر والمعاصي وإن كانا بإرادة الله تعالى لكنهما ليستا بمحبة الله تعالى ولا برضاه.
As for the Mu'tazila, they have argued for their view with arguments: The first doubt: He, exalted, commanded the disbeliever to believe, and whoever commands something is a willer of the existence of the commanded thing, so it follows that God, exalted, willed faith from the disbeliever. The second doubt: Willing disbelief and immorality is foolishness, and foolishness does not befit the Wise. Also: if He creates disbelief and immorality, then He punishes for them, that would be extreme foolishness. The third doubt: Obedience is the attainment of the will of the obeyed, so if God, exalted, willed disbelief from the disbelievers, then the disbeliever would be obedient to God, exalted, by his disbelief, and that is false by consensus. The fourth doubt: Being pleased with the decree of God, exalted, is obligatory by consensus, so if disbelief were by the decree of God, exalted, then being pleased with disbelief would be obligatory, but this is false because being pleased with disbelief is disbelief. The fifth doubt: If God, exalted, willed disbelief from the disbeliever, and created disbelief in the disbeliever, then charging the disbeliever with faith would be a charge of what cannot be borne. And this is false by reason and revelation. As for reason: because every person necessarily finds from himself that if he wills obedience, he can do it, so how can it be said he is not able to obey, while he necessarily finds the existence of this ability? As for revelation: His statement, exalted: 'And what harm would it do them if they believed in Allah' [4:39] and 'They are from the reminder turning away' [74:49], and likewise the statement concerning the many verses indicating that man is not incapable of performing acts of obedience. [؟] The sixth doubt: Adherence to His statement, exalted: 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' [39:7], and His statement, exalted: 'And Allah does not intend injustice for the servants' [40:31], and His statement: 'He guides whom He wills' [2:142]. And the answer concerning the first doubt: We do not concede that commanding something necessitates the will of the commanded thing, for the dispute is only about that. Concerning the second doubt: We shall soon establish a decisive proof, if God wills, that the bad is not bad except by revelation, and on this principle, nothing is bad from God, exalted. Concerning the third doubt: Obedience is conformity to the command, not conformity to the will. Concerning the fourth doubt: Decree is an attribute of God, and we are pleased with the decree of God, exalted; that is, we are pleased with the attribute of God, exalted. As for disbelief, it is not itself the decree of God, exalted, but rather that which is necessitated by the decree of God, exalted, and the proof has not indicated that it is obligatory to be pleased with everything that God decreed. Concerning the fifth doubt: The counter-argument is by the six aspects we mentioned in the creation of actions. Concerning the sixth doubt: Approval and love are refraining from objection, and disbelief and sins, even if they are by the will of God, exalted, they are not by the love of God, exalted, nor by His approval.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the six Mu'tazilite objections (shubah) and the Ash'arite responses (ajwiba). The objections are: (1) Command implies will; (2) Willing evil is foolishness; (3) Obedience is fulfilling the will of the obeyed; (4) Being pleased with God's decree requires being pleased with disbelief; (5) If God wills disbelief, commanding faith is impossible; (6) Verses show God does not desire disbelief. The answers: (1) Command does not entail will—this is the very point of dispute. (2) Evil is defined by revelation, not inherent; hence nothing is bad for God. (3) Obedience is to command, not to will. (4) We are pleased with God's attribute of decree (qada'), not with the decreed things like disbelief. (5) Counter-argument by the six points previously mentioned in the issue of creation of actions. (6) God's approval (rida) and love (mahabbah) are distinct from His will; He may will what He does not love.

Key Terms
الشبهة
الأمر
الطاعة
السفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commanding something always implies willing it.
Correction: The Ash'ari response explicitly denies this: 'لا نسلم أن الأمر بالشيء يستدعي إرادة المأمور به' (We do not concede that commanding something necessitates the will of the commanded thing).

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'arite response to the objection that commanding something implies willing it?
A) They agree, but say God's will is different from human will.
B) They deny that command necessarily entails will.
C) They argue that God's command is only for good, not evil.
D) They say God's will is unknowable.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arite response hinges on denying that command entails will and that moral judgments require revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where your obedience to God conflicts with your inner desires, do you truly believe that obedience is measured by command, not by your will? How does that conviction affect your daily choices?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought commanding implies willing. Have you ever felt bitter or confused when God commands something you struggle with, thinking He must not truly want you to succeed? How does the Ash'ari response free you from that despair?
?
If moral evil is not inherent but determined by revelation, does that change how you view the trials and suffering in your life? Can you accept that what seems evil to you may not be so in God's decree, and surrender your judgment to His command?

The Final Three Objections and the Distinction Between Will and Approval
Last three objections: pleased with decree, impossible command, Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite responses to objections about being pleased with decree, impossible commands, and Quranic verses, and the key distinction between divine will and approval.

Core Idea
God may will what He does not approve; His decree is His attribute, not the decreed things.

The Mu'tazila further argue that if God decrees disbelief, we must be pleased with it, and that commanding faith is then impossible. The Ash'ari response distinguishes between God's decree as an attribute and the decreed things, and between His will and His approval.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage for objections 4-6 and their answers. Then read the translation.
Note the corrupted sequence '[؟]' in the sixth objection - it's a textual issue.
2
Analyze the fourth objection and answer
The objection: if disbelief is by decree, we must be pleased with it. The answer distinguishes between decree (attribute) and decreed (thing). Pay attention to the term 'qada'.'
3
Examine the fifth objection and answer
The objection: if God wills and creates disbelief, then commanding faith is impossible (taklif ma la yutaq). The answer refers to six aspects previously discussed in 'creation of actions'.
The answer is brief; it counters by prior arguments.
4
Understand the sixth objection and answer
The objection cites Quranic verses that God does not approve or intend injustice. The answer clarifies that approval (rida) and love (mahabbah) are distinct from will (irada); God may will what He does not love.

Source Text

وأما المعتزلة فقد احتجوا على قولهم بوجوه : الشبهة الأولى : إنه تعالى أمر الكافر بالإيمان وكل من أمر بشيء فهو مريد لوجود المأمور به فيلزم أن يكون الله تعالى أراد الإيمان من الكافر. الشبهة الثانية : إرادة الكفر والفسق سفه، والسفه لا يليق بالحكيم. وأيضا : إن منه الكفر والفسق ، ثم إنه يعاقب عليهما كان ذلك غاية السفاهة. الشبهة الثالثة : الطاعة عبارة عن تحصيل مراد المطاع فلو أراد الله تعالى الكفر من الكافرين لكان الكافر مطيعا لله تعالى بكفره وذلك باطل بالاتفاق. الشبهة الرابعة : الرضا بقضاء الله تعالى واجب بالإجماع، فلو كان الكفر بقضاء الله تعالى لوجب الرضا بالكفر، لكن هذا باطل لأن الرضا بالكفر كفر. الشبهة الخامسة : لو أراد الله تعالى الكفر من الكافر، وخلق الكفر في الكافر، لكان تكليف الكافر بالإيمان تكليف بما لا يطاق. وهذا باطل بالعقل والسمع. أما العقل : فلأن كل إنسان يجد بالضرورة من نفسه أنه إن أراد الطاعة، يمكنه فعلها فكيف يقال : إنه غير متمكن من الطاعة، أنه يجد بالضرورة ثبوت هذه مع المكنة؟ أما السمع : فقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَمَاذَا عَلَيْهِمْ لَو ءَامَنُوا بِالله ا [النساء : ٣٩] وفا هم عَنِ التذكرة مُعرِضِينَ ﴾ [المدثر : ٤٩] وكذا القول في جملة الآيات الدالة على أن الإنسان غير عاجز عن الإتيان بالطاعات. [؟] الشبهة السادسة : التمسك بقوله تعالى : ﴿وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الكفر [الزمر : ٧] وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلّمًا لِلْعِبَادِ الله [غافر: ٣١] وبقوله يَهْدِي مَن يَشَاءُ [البقرة : ١٤٢] . والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : لا نسلم أن الأمر بالشيء يستدعي إرادة المأمور به فإن النزاع ما وقع إلا فيه. وعن الشبهة الثانية إنا سنقيم الدلالة القاطعة - إن شاء الله تعالى - على أن القبيح لا يقبح إلا بالشرع، وعلى هذا الأصل فإنه لا يقبح من الله تعالى شيء. وعن الشبهة الثالثة : إن الطاعة موافقة للأمر لا موافقة للإرادة. وعن الشبهة الرابعة : القضاء صفة الله ونحن راضون بقضاء الله تعالى. أي راضون بصفة الله تعالى، وأما الكفر فليس هو نفس قضاء الله تعالى، بل هو مقتضي قضاء الله تعالى ولم يدل الدليل على أنه واجب الرضى بكل شيء قضى الله تعالى به. وعن الشبهة الخامسة : المعارضة بالوجوه الستة التي ذكرناها في خلق الأفعال. وعن الشبهة السادسة : إن الرضا والمحبة ترك الاعتراض، والكفر والمعاصي وإن كانا بإرادة الله تعالى لكنهما ليستا بمحبة الله تعالى ولا برضاه.
As for the Mu'tazila, they have argued for their view with arguments: The first doubt: He, exalted, commanded the disbeliever to believe, and whoever commands something is a willer of the existence of the commanded thing, so it follows that God, exalted, willed faith from the disbeliever. The second doubt: Willing disbelief and immorality is foolishness, and foolishness does not befit the Wise. Also: if He creates disbelief and immorality, then He punishes for them, that would be extreme foolishness. The third doubt: Obedience is the attainment of the will of the obeyed, so if God, exalted, willed disbelief from the disbelievers, then the disbeliever would be obedient to God, exalted, by his disbelief, and that is false by consensus. The fourth doubt: Being pleased with the decree of God, exalted, is obligatory by consensus, so if disbelief were by the decree of God, exalted, then being pleased with disbelief would be obligatory, but this is false because being pleased with disbelief is disbelief. The fifth doubt: If God, exalted, willed disbelief from the disbeliever, and created disbelief in the disbeliever, then charging the disbeliever with faith would be a charge of what cannot be borne. And this is false by reason and revelation. As for reason: because every person necessarily finds from himself that if he wills obedience, he can do it, so how can it be said he is not able to obey, while he necessarily finds the existence of this ability? As for revelation: His statement, exalted: 'And what harm would it do them if they believed in Allah' [4:39] and 'They are from the reminder turning away' [74:49], and likewise the statement concerning the many verses indicating that man is not incapable of performing acts of obedience. [؟] The sixth doubt: Adherence to His statement, exalted: 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' [39:7], and His statement, exalted: 'And Allah does not intend injustice for the servants' [40:31], and His statement: 'He guides whom He wills' [2:142]. And the answer concerning the first doubt: We do not concede that commanding something necessitates the will of the commanded thing, for the dispute is only about that. Concerning the second doubt: We shall soon establish a decisive proof, if God wills, that the bad is not bad except by revelation, and on this principle, nothing is bad from God, exalted. Concerning the third doubt: Obedience is conformity to the command, not conformity to the will. Concerning the fourth doubt: Decree is an attribute of God, and we are pleased with the decree of God, exalted; that is, we are pleased with the attribute of God, exalted. As for disbelief, it is not itself the decree of God, exalted, but rather that which is necessitated by the decree of God, exalted, and the proof has not indicated that it is obligatory to be pleased with everything that God decreed. Concerning the fifth doubt: The counter-argument is by the six aspects we mentioned in the creation of actions. Concerning the sixth doubt: Approval and love are refraining from objection, and disbelief and sins, even if they are by the will of God, exalted, they are not by the love of God, exalted, nor by His approval.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the six Mu'tazilite objections (shubah) and the Ash'arite responses (ajwiba). The objections are: (1) Command implies will; (2) Willing evil is foolishness; (3) Obedience is fulfilling the will of the obeyed; (4) Being pleased with God's decree requires being pleased with disbelief; (5) If God wills disbelief, commanding faith is impossible; (6) Verses show God does not desire disbelief. The answers: (1) Command does not entail will—this is the very point of dispute. (2) Evil is defined by revelation, not inherent; hence nothing is bad for God. (3) Obedience is to command, not to will. (4) We are pleased with God's attribute of decree (qada'), not with the decreed things like disbelief. (5) Counter-argument by the six points previously mentioned in the issue of creation of actions. (6) God's approval (rida) and love (mahabbah) are distinct from His will; He may will what He does not love.

Key Terms
الرضا
القضاء
المحبة
التكليف بما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will and His approval are identical.
Correction: The answer to the sixth objection explicitly distinguishes: 'الكفر والمعاصي وإن كانا بإرادة الله تعالى لكنهما ليستا بمحبة الله تعالى ولا برضاه' (disbelief and sins, even if they are by the will of God, are not by His love or approval).

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'arite response to the sixth objection, what is the relationship between God's will and His approval?
A) They are identical; what He wills He approves.
B) He may will something but not approve it.
C) He only wills what He approves.
D) Approval is a subset of will.

Key Takeaway
God's will is universal, but His approval is only for good; thus He may will disbelief without approving it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a calamity—like illness or loss—do you find yourself resenting God's decree, or can you genuinely be pleased with His decree as an attribute while still hating what happened? What would change in your heart if you truly internalized this distinction?
?
Have you ever been tempted to think that because God wills something to happen, He must approve of it—perhaps justifying a sin as 'part of God's plan'? How does the lesson's distinction between will and approval challenge that assumption, and what step will you take today to align your approval with God's?
?
If God's approval is only for good, and He does not approve of disbelief or injustice, then where do you stand? Are you living in a way that seeks His approval, or merely assuming His will covers everything? What is one concrete change you must make to pursue what He loves, not just what happens?

The First Proof Against Causation in Divine Actions
Razi states the Mu'tazila position and gives the first proof against divine causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof that Allah's actions are not caused by any purpose or benefit external to Himself.

Core Idea
If Allah acted for a benefit, He would either need it or be indifferent—both impossible.

Imam Razi begins the twenty-sixth question by presenting the Mu'tazila claim that Allah's actions are based on benefits for servants, then rejects it with five proofs. This lesson focuses on the first proof, which exposes a fundamental dilemma: any action for a benefit implies either need or lack of motive, both of which are impossible for Allah. We will work through the proof, a potential objection, and Razi's response.

Guided Path
1
Context and Opponent View
Read the opening lines where Razi states the twenty-sixth question and describes the Mu'tazila position that Allah's actions are based on interests of servants. Understand that this is the view he rejects.
The Mu'tazila and later jurists held that Allah acts for the benefit of creation.
2
The First Proof's Dilemma
Follow the proof: If an agent acts for a benefit, either that benefit is more preferable for the agent or it is not. If more preferable, the agent needs it (deficiency). If equal, there is no reason to choose. Both are impossible for Allah.
The key terms: 'أولوية' (preference) and 'نقص' (deficiency).
3
Consider the Objection
Read the potential objection: it says that even if benefit is equal for Allah, it is still preferable for the servant, so that preference could motivate the action.
Objectors argue that the benefit is for the servant, not for Allah, so no deficiency in Allah.
4
Razi's Reply
Now examine Razi's response: He forces the same dilemma from Allah's perspective regarding the benefit to the servant. Either benefiting the servant is equal or not equal for Allah, leading back to the original problem.
Razi shows that the objection does not escape the dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السادسة والعشرون في أنه لا يجوز أن تكون أفعال الله تعالى وأحكامه معللة بعلة البتة اتفقت المعتزلة: على أن أفعال الله تعالى وأحكامه معللة برعاية مصالح العباد وهو اختيار أكثر المتأخرين من الفقهاء. وهذا عندنا باطل. ويدل عليه وجوه خمسة: الحجة الأولى: إن كل من فعل فعلا لأجل تحصيل مصلحة أو لدفع مفسدة، فإن كان تحصيل تلك المصلحة أولى له من عدم تحصيلها، كان ذلك الفاعل قد استفاد بذلك الفعل تحصيل تلك الأولوية. وكل من كان كذلك، كان ناقصا بذاته، مستكملا بغيره وهو في حق الله تعالى محال وإن تحصيلها وعدم تحصيلها بالنسبة إليه سيان فمع الاستواء لا يحصل الرجحان، فامتنع الترجيح. لايقال: حصولها [؟] ولها حصولها بالنسبة إليه، وإن كانا على التساوي، إلا أن حصولها أولى للعبد من عدم حصولها له، فلأجل الأولوية العائدة إلى العبد ترجح الوجود على العدم لأنا نقول: تحصيل مصلحة العبد وعدم تحصيلها له إما أن يكونا متساويين بالنسبة إلى الله تعالى أو لا يستويان. وحينئذ يعود التقسيم المذكور.
The twenty-sixth question: On the matter that it is not permissible that the actions of God Almighty and His rulings be based on any cause at all. The Mu'tazila have agreed that the actions of God Almighty and His rulings are based on regard for the interests of the servants, and this is the choice of most of the later jurists. This, according to us, is false. And there are five proofs for it: The first proof: Whoever does an action for the sake of obtaining a benefit or repelling an evil, if obtaining that benefit is more preferable for him than not obtaining it, then that agent has, by that action, obtained that preference. And whoever is like that is deficient in himself, perfecting himself through another, and this is impossible with respect to God Almighty. If obtaining and not obtaining are equal for him, then with equality no preponderance occurs, so preponderance is impossible. It should not be said: Its obtaining and [؟] its obtaining relative to Him, even if they are equal, but its obtaining is more preferable for the servant than its not obtaining for him, so due to the preference returning to the servant, existence is preferred over non-existence. For we say: Obtaining the benefit for the servant and not obtaining it for him are either equal with respect to God Almighty or not equal. Then the aforementioned division returns.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage states the twenty-sixth question and its first proof. The author declares the Mu'tazila position that Allah's actions are based on benefits for servants, then rejects it. The first proof argues: if an agent acts for a benefit, either that benefit is more preferable for him or not. If it is more preferable, he needs it, implying deficiency — impossible for Allah. If it is equal, there is no preponderance to prompt action. A potential objection: the benefit is for the servant, so preference is for the servant. The reply: from Allah's perspective, benefiting the servant is either equal or not; if equal, the same problem; if not equal, then Allah prefers the servant's benefit, which again implies need and deficiency.

Key Terms
أفعال الله
معللة
مصالح العباد
أولوية
نقص
ترجیح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this proof denies any wisdom in Allah's actions, implying they are arbitrary.
Correction: The proof only denies that Allah's actions are caused by an external purpose or need. It does not deny that actions have wisdom, but affirms that the wisdom is not a motivating cause.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof's conclusion about Allah acting for a benefit?
A) Allah's actions are based on benefit for servants
B) If an agent acts for benefit, it implies deficiency or impossibility of preference
C) The benefit to the servant provides a reason for Allah to act
D) Allah needs nothing, so He never acts

Key Takeaway
Allah's actions cannot be caused by any purpose external to Him, because acting for a benefit would imply need or indifference, both impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that your acts of worship are somehow beneficial to Allah? How does this proof reveal the arrogance of such a thought, and what should your worship actually be based on?
?
Consider a time when you obeyed Allah primarily to gain a worldly benefit or avoid a harm. Does that make your obedience a transaction? How does this passage call you to purify your intention so that you worship Allah solely because He is worthy, not for any return?
?
If Allah does not act for any benefit external to Himself, then His commands and prohibitions are also not for His benefit. Will you now submit to His rulings even when you see no personal advantage, trusting that His wisdom is perfect while your need is real?

Objections to Necessitarian Divine Wisdom
Razi presents objections to the necessitarian view of divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key objections Razi raises against the view that divine wisdom necessitates reward and prevents evil, and why these objections are considered difficult.

Core Idea
Divine wisdom cannot necessitate action without compromising God's power and free will.

Razi presents a series of objections against a specific understanding of divine wisdom (al-hikma) that treats it as a necessitating attribute. He argues that if wisdom necessitates certain actions like rewarding the deserving, it would imply God cannot do otherwise, which undermines His power. Additionally, if actions depend on a motive, determinism results, and the attribute of self-sufficiency alone does not rule out evil. This passage challenges students to think critically about how divine attributes interact.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi structures his objections.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The passage contains two distinct lines of objection: one about the necessity of reward compromising power, and another about motive leading to determinism. Find these.
There is also a third objection later in the passage.
3
Understand the consequence for divine power
The first argument shows that if wisdom necessitates reward, God would not be able to omit it, which detracts from His being all-powerful (qādir). Reflect on why this is problematic.
4
Analyze the second objection
The final part argues that God's self-sufficiency (ghaniyy) does not logically preclude evil, so it cannot be used as evidence that God does not do evil. Consider the challenge this poses.
Razi himself admits this objection is difficult.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالذات، ممتنع العدم. وإذا كان ترك القبيح أمرا واجبا بالذات، كان إيصال الثواب إلى المستحق أمرا واجبا، وجوبا بالذات لأن تركه لما كان قبيحا ممتنعا بالذات كان فعله فعلا واجبا بالذات. فحينئذ يلزم أن تكون ذاته تعالى موجبة لحصول الثواب ووصوله إلى المستحق، وأن لا يكون قادرا على الترك أصلا فإثبات الحكمة على هذا الوجه يقدح في كونه قادرا، إلا أن الحكمة مفرعة على كونه قادرا والفرع إذا استلزم فساد الأصل كان باطلا، فالقول بالحكمة يجب أن يكون باطلا على هذا القول.
وأيضا : إذا كان الفعل موقوفا على الداعي لزم الجبر، وإذا لزم الجبر كان الله تعالى فاعلا لجميع أفعال العباد بواسطة خلق القدرة والداعي الموجب لها، وإذا كان كذلك، امتنع أن يقال : إنه تعالى لا يفعل هذه الأفعال.
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن تقولوا : إن كونه تعالى غنيا مع كونه عالما بكونه غنيا، لا ينافي فعل القبيح ولم يكن بين حصول هذا الفعل وحصول ذلك الوصف منافاة ولا معاندة أصلا، فحينئذ يتعذر الاستدلال بذلك الوصف على أنه تعالى لا يفعل القبيح، لأن كل ما لم يكن فيه امتناع لم يلزم من فرض وجوده محال ولا فساد.
وهذا سؤال صعب على ما ذكروه. والله أعلم بالصواب.
By essence, impossible of non-existence. And if refraining from evil is an obligatory matter by essence, then delivering reward to the one who deserves it is an obligatory matter, obligatory by essence, because its omission being evil and impossible by essence makes its doing an obligatory act by essence. Then it follows that His essence, exalted, necessitates the occurrence of reward and its delivery to the one who deserves it, and that He is not able to omit it at all. So affirming wisdom (al-hikma) in this manner detracts from His being powerful, except that wisdom is derived from His being powerful, and if the derived leads to corruption of the root, it is false; so the statement about wisdom must be false according to this view.
And also: if the action depends on the motive (al-da'ī), then determinism (al-jabr) follows, and if determinism follows, then God, exalted, is the doer of all actions of servants through creating the ability and the motive that necessitates them, and if that is so, it is impossible to say that He, exalted, does not do these actions.
As for the second division: which is that you say: His being self-sufficient (ghaniyy) together with His being knowing of His being self-sufficient does not negate doing evil, and between the occurrence of this action and the occurrence of that description there is no opposition or contradiction at all; then it becomes impossible to use that description as evidence that He, exalted, does not do evil, because for anything in which there is no impossibility, no absurdity or corruption follows from supposing its existence.
And this is a difficult objection according to what they mentioned. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents objections to a specific understanding of divine wisdom (al-hikma). The author first sets up a scenario: if refraining from evil is necessary by essence, then granting reward to the deserving becomes equally necessary. This would imply that God's essence necessitates the reward and He cannot withhold it, which compromises His power (qudra). Since wisdom is a derivative of power, if the derived concept undermines the root, the concept is invalid. The author then adds a second argument: if actions depend on a motive (al-da'ī), this necessitates determinism (al-jabr), making God the true agent of all human actions through creating the motive and ability, thus contradicting the claim that God does not do certain actions. Finally, the author introduces a second objection (al-qism al-thānī): one could argue that God's self-sufficiency and knowledge thereof do not preclude evil, so the attribute of self-sufficiency cannot be used to prove that God does not do evil, since no logical impossibility arises from supposing He does evil. The passage concludes by acknowledging the difficulty of this objection.

Key Terms
الحكمة
الجبر
القبيح
الداعي
الوجوب بالذات
الاستدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage denies that God is wise altogether.
Correction: The passage only refutes a necessitarian understanding of wisdom that would compromise divine power and free will, not wisdom itself.

Checkpoint
What is one consequence mentioned in the passage of holding that God's wisdom necessitates the delivery of reward?
A) It proves that God is all-powerful.
B) It leads to the conclusion that God cannot omit the reward, thus detracting from His power.
C) It establishes that reward is purely voluntary.
D) It shows that God is free from any obligations.

Key Takeaway
Razi's objections highlight that divine wisdom must be understood without compromising God's absolute power and free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's wisdom, do you secretly believe that God is bound to act in a certain way? How does that belief affect your trust in His absolute power and freedom?
?
Have you ever felt that God must reward you because of your good deeds, as if He owes you? How does Razi's objection challenge that assumption?
?
In moments of suffering or injustice, have you ever doubted God's wisdom because you thought He should have acted differently? How does this lesson reframe the relationship between God's wisdom and His absolute power?

Razi's Refutation of Necessity in Divine Actions
Razi presents two objections and a counterobjection on divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two arguments against the claim that divine wisdom necessitates reward, and the objection that self-sufficiency does not preclude evil.

Core Idea
Divine wisdom cannot necessitate actions without contradicting God's power and freedom.

In this passage, Imam Razi critically examines the view that divine wisdom obligates God to reward the deserving. He presents two arguments showing that such necessity would undermine His power and lead to compulsion (jabr). He then considers a counterargument based on God's self-sufficiency, but leaves the matter as a difficult question. This lesson will guide you through each part of his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get a sense of Razi's overall argument. Note the three distinct sections: two arguments and a second division.
2
Analyze the first argument
Identify the chain: if abandoning evil is necessary by essence, then rewarding the deserving is also necessary, making God's act necessary and thus conflicting with His power. Razi says this invalidates the concept of wisdom as usually understood.
Notice the phrase 'فإثبات الحكمة على هذا الوجه يقدح في كونه قادرا'.
3
Analyze the second argument
Razi argues that if action depends on motive (الداعي), then compulsion (الجبر) follows, meaning God does all actions through creating ability and motive. This makes God the doer of all human acts, contradicting the idea that He does not do evil.
Focus on 'إذا كان الفعل موقوفا على الداعي لزم الجبر'.
4
Understand the second division
Razi presents a counterargument: God's self-sufficiency and knowledge of it do not logically preclude evil acts; thus one cannot use these attributes to prove God never does evil. He acknowledges this as a difficult question.
The phrase 'وهذا سؤال صعب' shows Razi's humility.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how Razi uses logical entailment to challenge the notion of necessary divine actions. Ask yourself: does this passage completely refute divine wisdom, or merely a particular understanding of it?
This connects to the learning objective.

بالذات، ممتنع العدم.
كتاب الأربعين في أصول الدين
وإذا كان ترك القبيح أمرا واجبا بالذات، كان إيصال الثواب إلى المستحق أمرا واجبا، وجوبا بالذات لأن تركه لما كان قبيحا ممتنعا بالذات كان فعله فعلا واجبا بالذات. فحينئذ يلزم أن تكون ذاته تعالى موجبة لحصول الثواب ووصوله إلى المستحق، وأن لا يكون قادرا على الترك أصلا فإثبات الحكمة على هذا الوجه يقدح في كونه قادرا ، إلا أن الحكمة مفرعة على كونه قادرا والفرع إذا استلزم فساد الأصل كان باطلا، فالقول بالحكمة يجب أن يكون باطلا على هذا القول.
،
وأيضا : إذا كان الفعل موقوفا على الداعي لزم الجبر، وإذا لزم الجبر كان الله تعالى فاعلا لجميع أفعال العباد بواسطة خلق القدرة والداعي الموجب لها، وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع أن يقال : إنه تعالى لا يفعل هذه الأفعال.
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن تقولوا : إن كونه تعالى غنيا مع كونه عالما بكونه غنيا، لا ينافي فعل القبيح ولم يكن بين حصول هذا الفعل وحصول ذلك الوصف منافاة ولا معاندة أصلا، فحينئذ يتعذر الاستدلال بذلك الوصف على أنه تعالى لا يفعل القبيح، لأن كل ما لم يكن فيه امتناع لم يلزم من فرض وجوده محال ولا فساد.
وهذا سؤال صعب على ما ذكروه. والله أعلم بالصواب.
By essence, non-existence is impossible. The Book of the Forty in the Principles of Religion. And if abandoning evil is an obligatory matter by essence, then delivering reward to the deserving one is an obligatory matter, an obligation by essence, because its abandonment, since it is evil and impossible by essence, its performance becomes an act obligatory by essence. So then it follows that His Essence (Exalted is He) necessitates the occurrence of reward and its reaching the deserving one, and that He is not capable of abandoning at all. So establishing wisdom in this manner impugns His being capable. However, wisdom is derived from His being capable, and if a branch necessitates the corruption of the root, it is invalid. So the statement about wisdom must be false according to this opinion. And also: if the action depends on the motive, then compulsion (al-jabr) follows. And if compulsion follows, then God (Exalted is He) is the doer of all actions of the servants by means of creating the ability (al-qudra) and the motive that necessitates it. And if it is so, it becomes impossible to say that He (Exalted) does not do these actions. As for the second division: it is that you say: that His being (Exalted) Self-sufficient along with His being knowing of His being Self-sufficient does not contradict the doing of evil, and there is no opposition or conflict between the occurrence of this act and the occurrence of that description at all. So then it becomes impossible to use that description as evidence that He (Exalted) does not do evil, because anything that does not have impossibility, its supposition does not entail impossibility or corruption. And this is a difficult question according to what they mentioned. And Allah knows best the truth.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents a series of arguments concerning divine wisdom and its implications. First, it argues that if abandoning evil is necessary by essence (بالذات), then rewarding the deserving must also be necessary by essence, which would mean God must reward and cannot refrain—this undermines His power. Since wisdom is derived from power, this reasoning invalidates the concept of wisdom as usually understood. A second argument: if actions depend on motive, then compulsion (الجبر) follows, making God the actual doer of all human actions, which contradicts the idea that He does not commit evil. The author then presents a 'second division'—an objection: God's self-sufficiency and knowledge of it do not necessarily preclude evil acts, because no logical contradiction exists; thus, one cannot use these attributes to prove God never does evil. The passage closes by noting this is a difficult question, leaving the matter to Allah's knowledge. The commentary follows the author's order: first objection, second objection, a counterpoint, and a concluding remark.

Key Terms
بالذات
القبيح
الجبر
الحكمة
الداعي
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine wisdom obligates God to act in certain ways.
Correction: Razi shows this leads to contradictions with His power and free will, so wisdom must be understood differently.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, if abandoning evil is obligatory by essence, what follows about God's action of rewarding?
A) He must reward necessarily.
B) He may reward or not.
C) He cannot reward.
D) Reward is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the notion that divine wisdom necessitates specific actions by showing it would conflict with God's absolute power and freedom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that if God's actions were necessitated by wisdom, He would no longer be truly powerful. When you face difficult circumstances where God's actions seem inexplicable, do you assume He must act a certain way out of wisdom? How would your trust in God deepen if you truly believed His wisdom does not constrain His absolute freedom?
?
The second counterargument suggests that God's self-sufficiency does not logically prevent Him from doing evil. Yet we believe He does not. What does this teach you about relying on logical proofs for God's attributes versus accepting revelation? Where in your faith are you tempted to over-rely on human reasoning about God?
?
If God's actions depend on a motive, Razi says this leads to compulsion. How do you reconcile your own sense of free will with the belief that God's will is sovereign? Is there an area in your life where you blame God for your choices because you think He compelled them? What would it mean for you to take full responsibility while trusting His overall plan?

Three Ancient Views on the Soul as Fire, Air, and Water
Imam Razi presents and critiques three ancient opinions that the soul is a body: fire, air, water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three ancient philosophical views identifying the soul with fire, air, or water, and the logical error that invalidates their arguments.

Core Idea
Shared properties do not prove identity – a fundamental logical fallacy in ancient soul theories.

Imam Razi presents three historical attempts to identify the soul with a physical element: fire, air, and water. Each philosopher argued from observable similarities between the element and the soul’s functions. However, Razi demonstrates that their reasoning rests on a logical flaw: attributing common properties does not establish that two things are the same. This lesson examines these views and their critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow: the definitive opening, three views, and concluding critique.
Notice the names: 'Aqlu Tarjas' (likely corrupted), Diogenes, Thales of Miletus.
2
Identify each view's argument
For each philosopher, find the property they claim is shared between the element and the soul. Note their reasoning.
Fire: illumination/movement. Air: breath/shapelessness. Water: growth.
3
Understand Razi's logical critique
Razi says all three arguments are weak because they use two affirmative premises in the second figure of syllogism. This form yields no necessary conclusion.
Different essences can share attributes; shared attribute does not imply identical essence.
4
Reflect on the lesson for theology
Recognize why sound logical reasoning is crucial in defending correct beliefs about the soul and God.
This critique protects against hasty identifications in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القطع بأن ما هو المشار إليه بقولي أنا مغاير لجميع الأعضاء الظاهرة والباطنة. وأما القول الثاني. وهو أن الإنسان عبارة عن جسم موجود في داخل هذا الهيكل فهذا القول فيه وجوه. أحدها : قول (أقلو) طرجس). وهو أنه عبارة عن الأجزاء النارية السارية في هذا الهيكل قال : لأن خاصية النار الإشراق والحركة والنفس خاصيتها الإدراك والحركة الاختيارية والإدراك من جنس الإشراق فثبت أن النفس عبارة عن النار. قالوا : ويتأيد هذا بما يقول الأطباء : إن مدير البدن هو الحرارة الغريزية. وثانيها : قول (ديوجانس) وهو أن النفس هو الهواء قال : (وذلك لأنه متى كان النفس مترددا، كانت الحياة باقية. وإذا انقطع النفس زالت الحياة، فوجب أن تكون النفس عبارة عن التنفس، وهو الهواء المستنشق المتردد في مخالق البدن، وأيضا من خواص الهواء: أنه لا لون له ويدخل في المنافذ الضيقة، ويقبل الأشكال المختلفة، والجسم الذي يكون فيه مستقلا بذاته كالزق المنفوخ. والنفس بهذه الصفات فثبت أن النفس هو الهواء. وثالثها: قول ( ثاليس الملطي قال : ( إن النفس هو الماء، لأن الماء سبب لحصول النشوء والنمو، والنفس أيضا كذلك ( فكان النفس هو الماء. واعلم أن دلائل هذه الأقوال الثلاثة كلها ضعيفة لأنها أقيسة مركبة من الموجبتين في الشكل الثاني، وذلك غير منتج ؛ لأنه لا يمنع في العقل استواء الماهيات المختلفة في بعض الصفات.
The certainty that what is pointed to by my saying 'I' is distinct from all external and internal organs. As for the second opinion, which is that man is a body existing inside this physical structure, this opinion has aspects. First: the saying of (Aqlu Tarjas): that it (the soul) is the fiery parts that permeate this physical structure. He said: because the property of fire is illumination and movement, and the property of the soul is perception and voluntary movement, and perception is of the genus of illumination, so it is established that the soul is fire. They said: and this is supported by what the physicians say: the director of the body is the innate heat. Second: the saying of Diogenes: that the soul is air. He said: because when the breath is moving back and forth, life remains, and when the breath is cut off, life ceases, so it is necessary that the soul be the breath, which is the inhaled air moving in the passages of the body. Also, from the properties of air: it has no color, enters narrow openings, and accepts different shapes, and the body that is self-subsisting like an inflated bag. And the soul has these qualities, so it is established that the soul is air. Third: the saying of Thales of Miletus: that the soul is water, because water is the cause of growth and development, and the soul is also like that. So the soul is water. Know that the proofs of these three sayings are all weak because they are syllogisms composed of two affirmative premises in the second figure, and that yields no conclusion; because reason does not prevent different essences from sharing some attributes.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents the first three of ten aspects under the second opinion that the soul is a body. It begins with a definitive statement (likely the author's own view or a conclusion from the previous opinion) that the referent of 'I' is distinct from all visible and hidden organs. Then it introduces the second opinion: that man is a body inside the physical frame, and this opinion has several aspects. The first aspect is attributed to (Aqlu Tarjas), who said the soul is the fiery particles permeating the body, arguing that fire's properties (illumination and movement) match the soul's properties (perception and voluntary movement). This is supported by the medical concept of innate heat. The second aspect is from Diogenes, who said the soul is air. His proof: life continues as long as breath moves, and ceases when breath stops; thus the soul must be air. He also notes similarities: air has no color, enters narrow passages, and takes shapes like an inflated bag, and the soul has these qualities. The third aspect is from Thales of Miletus, who said the soul is water because water causes growth and so does the soul. After presenting these three, the author (Imam Razi) critiques them collectively: their proofs are weak because they are syllogisms with two affirmative premises in the second figure, which is invalid since overlapping predicates do not establish identity. Different essences can share attributes.

Key Terms
النفس (nafs/soul)
الهيكل (haykal/physical structure)
الأجزاء النارية (fiery parts)
الحرارة الغريزية (innate heat)
التنفس (breath)
الشكل الثاني (second figure of syllogism)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share a property, they must be identical in essence.
Correction: Different essences can share attributes (e.g., fire and soul both have movement), so shared properties do not prove identity.

Checkpoint
What logical flaw does Imam Razi identify in the proofs of the first three views on the soul?
A) They use circular reasoning.
B) They contain contradictory premises.
C) They are composed of two affirmative premises in the second figure, which yields no conclusion.
D) They rely on empirical observation that cannot be verified.

Key Takeaway
The ancient element theories of the soul fail because they rely on the invalid second-figure syllogism, where shared predicates do not entail shared essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you refer to your own 'I', do you instinctively identify it with your body, your breath, or your emotions? How does Razi's logical critique challenge you to separate your essence from these physical attributes?
?
Think of a belief you hold about yourself—perhaps that you are your thoughts or your career. Could that belief be a second-figure fallacy? What would it mean for you if your 'I' is distinct from all of that?
?
Razi shows that three ancient thinkers mistook shared properties for identity. Where in your own life have you wrongly assumed that two things are the same because they share a quality? How can you guard against this fallacy in your faith and self-understanding?

The Soul as Subtle Bodies Permeating the Body
Razi presents the sixth view: the soul as subtle bodies that permeate the physical structure, with a detailed analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth view on the soul as subtle bodies that permeate the physical structure, and how this view explains life and death.

Core Idea
The soul is a subtle body that permeates the dense body like fire in coal.

Razi now presents the sixth view: that the soul is not the dense body but a separate, subtle body that enters and permeates it. This view uses powerful analogies from nature, such as rosewater in leaves and fire in coal, to explain how life arises from the union of subtle and dense bodies. The passage also clarifies how death occurs when the physical frame loses its receptivity to the subtle soul-body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the analogy of permeation.
The analogies are central to the argument.
2
Identify the core claim and the analogy
The claim is that souls are subtle bodies. The analogies illustrate how they permeate the physical structure.
3
Understand the mechanism of life and death
Focus on the conditions for life (receptivity) and death (separation). See how this view differs from identifying soul with the dense body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسادسها : أن الأجسام مختلفة في ماهياتها. وذلك لأن جسم الأرض كثيف، وأنه البتة لا ينقلب لطيفا وجسم النار لطيف وأنه لا ينقلب البتة كثيفا. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الأنفس أجسام لطيفة لذواتها. وتلك الأجسام إذا شاركت هذا الهيكل وسرت فيه - سريان ماء الورد في ورق الورد، وسريان النار في الفحم، وسريان دهن السمسم في جرم السمسم - صار هذا الهيكل حيا، بسبب المشاركة والذوبان والانحلال. والتبدل لا يتطرق إلى تلك الأجسام اللطيفة الحية، وإنما يتطرق إلى هذا الهيكل وما دامت الأعضاء والأخلاط قابلة لسريان تلك الأجسام اللطيفة الحية لذواتها فيها، بقي هذا الهيكل حيا، وإذا أخرجت الأعضاء والأخلاط عن القابلية انفصلت تلك الأجسام اللطيفة عنها وذلك هو الموت.
And the sixth of them: that bodies differ in their quiddities. Because the body of earth is dense, and it absolutely does not become subtle, and the body of fire is subtle, and it absolutely does not become dense. If this is established, then we say: the souls are subtle bodies in themselves. And those bodies, when they share this physical structure and permeate it – like the permeation of rosewater in rose leaves, and like the permeation of fire in coal, and like the permeation of sesame oil in the substance of sesame – this physical structure becomes alive, due to participation, dissolution, and decomposition. And change does not affect those subtle living bodies, but rather affects this physical structure. And as long as the organs and humors are receptive to the permeation of those subtle living bodies in themselves, this physical structure remains alive. And when the organs and humors are rendered unreceptive, those subtle bodies separate from them, and that is death.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth view: that the soul is a subtle body distinct from the dense physical body. The argument begins by noting that bodies differ in essence: earth is dense and never becomes subtle, while fire is subtle and never becomes dense. From this, the proponent concludes that souls are inherently subtle bodies. When these subtle bodies enter and permeate the physical frame (like rosewater in leaves, fire in coal, sesame oil in seeds), the body becomes alive through their participation and dissolution. Importantly, change does not affect the subtle living bodies themselves, but only the physical body. Life continues as long as the organs and humors are receptive to their permeation. When receptivity is lost, the subtle bodies separate, which is death. This view distinguishes the soul from the physical body while still considering it a body (jism) of a subtle kind.

Key Terms
ماهيات (quiddities)
أجسام لطيفة (subtle bodies)
سريان (permeation)
المشاركة والذوبان والانحلال (participation, dissolution, decomposition)
الموت (death)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is the same as the dense physical body.
Correction: This view argues that the soul is a separate, subtle body that permeates the dense body, not identical to it.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth view, what happens to the subtle bodies at death?
A) They also decompose and die.
B) They separate from the physical structure when it loses receptivity.
C) They become denser and stay inside.
D) They transmute into another element.

Key Takeaway
The sixth view holds that the soul is a subtle, imperishable body that gives life to the dense physical structure through permeation, and death occurs when the body loses receptivity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that life persists as long as the organs and humors are receptive to the soul's permeation. In what ways might your own 'organs and humors'—your heart, mind, and limbs—be unreceptive to the soul's subtle presence due to negligence or sin? What one step can you take today to increase your receptivity?
?
The analogy of fire in coal suggests that the soul is always present but only manifests when the coal is ready. Is there an area in your life where you are stifling the 'fire' of your soul because you are unwilling to let it burn away impurities? What would it cost you to let it ignite fully?
?
If the subtle bodies themselves are not subject to change, and only the physical structure changes, then your essence (soul) remains untouched by age and decay. How does this truth confront your fear of death or your attachment to the physical? What changes in your daily priorities would reflect that your true self is this imperishable subtle body?

The Soul as Balanced Temperament
Razi presents the seventh view: soul as balanced temperament.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh view that identifies the soul with the balanced temperament (mizaj) arising from the mixture of elements and humors.

Core Idea
The soul is the balanced proportion of elements and humors.

Imam Razi continues his survey of opinions on the nature of the soul. The seventh view defines the soul as the temperament (mizaj) – the balanced quality that emerges when the four elements and four humors mix in due proportion. Read carefully how this view equates the soul with life itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic sentence describing the seventh view. Notice the key terms: المزاج, اعتدال, الأركان, الأخلاط.
The definition is given explicitly: النفس عبارة عن المزاج.
2
Understand the definition chain
The view says soul = temperament = balanced proportion. Then it explains that when elements and humors mix, a balanced quality arises, and that balance is the soul and life.
3
Reflect on the equation with life
Note that this view equates the soul with life itself, making the soul a byproduct of physical mixture.
This is a reductionist view that Razi does not endorse.
4
Consider the philosophical implications
Think about whether a simple physical balance can account for consciousness and identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسابعها : أن النفس عبارة عن المزاج الذي هو عبارة عن اعتدال الأركان والأخلاط، وذلك لأن الأركان والأخلاط إذا امتزج بعضها ببعض حصلت هناك كيفية معتدلة متوسطة - وهي المزاج - وذلك الاعتدال عبارة عن النفس، وعن الحياة. وثامنها : أن النفس عبارة عن الأجسام اللطيفة المتكونة في جانب البطن الأيسر من القلب النافذة في الشرايين النابتة منه إلى جملة أجزاء البدن.
And the seventh of them: that the soul is the temperament, which is the balanced proportion of the elements and humors. That is because when the elements and humors mix with each other, a balanced moderate quality arises – which is the temperament – and that balance is the soul, and is life. And the eighth of them: that the soul is the subtle bodies generated in the left cavity of the heart, passing through the arteries that sprout from it to all parts of the body.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents two more views. The seventh view identifies the soul with the temperament (mizaj), defined as the balanced proportion of the four elements (arkan) and the four humors (akhlat). When these mix, a moderate quality emerges, and that balance is both the soul and life. The eighth view says the soul consists of subtle bodies formed in the left cavity of the heart, which travel through the arteries to all bodily parts. This reflects a physiological theory akin to the 'animal spirit' concept.

Key Terms
المزاج (temperament)
اعتدال الأركان والأخلاط (balance of elements and humors)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the soul is identical to the physical mixture itself, not the balance.
Correction: The view specifies the soul is the balanced quality (kayfiyya mu'tadila), not the mixture of components.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh view, what is the soul?
A) The mixture of elements and humors.
B) The balanced quality resulting from the mixture.
C) The elements themselves.
D) The humors themselves.

Key Takeaway
The seventh view identifies the soul with the balanced temperament that results from a proper mixture of elements and humors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul were merely a balanced mixture of physical elements, what would happen to your sense of moral responsibility? Does this view make you uncomfortable? Why? Commit to seeking the truth about your soul beyond this limited perspective.
?
This passage presents the soul as a balance that arises from mixing elements and humors. In your own life, are you seeking balance in your actions and thoughts? Or are you imbalanced in ways that affect your spiritual well-being? What one step can you take today to restore balance?
?
Razi lists this view without endorsing it. Have you ever accepted a belief about yourself without critically examining it? What is one belief about your own soul that you hold but have not questioned? Are you willing to challenge it?

The Soul as Cardiac Subtle Bodies
Razi presents the eighth view: soul as subtle cardiac bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth view that describes the soul as subtle bodies formed in the left heart cavity and distributed via arteries.

Core Idea
The soul is a set of subtle bodies originating in the heart.

The eighth and final view that Razi presents in this section defines the soul in physiological terms: as subtle bodies generated in the left cavity of the heart, traveling through arteries to all parts of the body. This view aligns with ancient medical theories about 'animal spirits'.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic sentence describing the eighth view. Focus on the location: 'في جانب البطن الأيسر من القلب' and the mode of transmission: 'النافذة في الشرايين'.
2
Identify the origin and spread
The subtle bodies originate in the left heart cavity and then pass through arteries to all body parts.
This is a materialistic view of the soul.
3
Compare with the seventh view
Contrast this view with the previous one: here the soul is a collection of subtle bodies, not a quality.
4
Reflect on the physicality
Consider how this view reduces the soul to a physical substance that can be located and transmitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسابعها : أن النفس عبارة عن المزاج الذي هو عبارة عن اعتدال الأركان والأخلاط، وذلك لأن الأركان والأخلاط إذا امتزج بعضها ببعض حصلت هناك كيفية معتدلة متوسطة - وهي المزاج - وذلك الاعتدال عبارة عن النفس، وعن الحياة. وثامنها : أن النفس عبارة عن الأجسام اللطيفة المتكونة في جانب البطن الأيسر من القلب النافذة في الشرايين النابتة منه إلى جملة أجزاء البدن.
And the seventh of them: that the soul is the temperament, which is the balanced proportion of the elements and humors. That is because when the elements and humors mix with each other, a balanced moderate quality arises – which is the temperament – and that balance is the soul, and is life. And the eighth of them: that the soul is the subtle bodies generated in the left cavity of the heart, passing through the arteries that sprout from it to all parts of the body.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents two more views. The seventh view identifies the soul with the temperament (mizaj), defined as the balanced proportion of the four elements (arkan) and the four humors (akhlat). When these mix, a moderate quality emerges, and that balance is both the soul and life. The eighth view says the soul consists of subtle bodies formed in the left cavity of the heart, which travel through the arteries to all bodily parts. This reflects a physiological theory akin to the 'animal spirit' concept.

Key Terms
الأجسام اللطيفة المتكونة في جانب البطن الأيسر من القلب (subtle bodies in left heart cavity)
الشرايين (arteries)

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) In the liver.

Key Takeaway
The eighth view holds that the soul consists of subtle bodies formed in the heart's left cavity and distributed through arteries.

?

?
This view locates the soul's origin in the left side of the heart — a concrete place. Does your belief about where your soul resides change how you treat your body? What would it mean for your accountability if your soul were distributed through your veins?

Third Philosophers' Proof: Direction and the Celestial Sphere
Razi presents the third philosophical argument for the eternity of bodies based on direction and containment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third philosophical argument for the eternity of celestial spheres, which hinges on the nature of direction and the impossibility of rectilinear motion for the encompassing sphere.

Core Idea
Direction requires a corporeal limiter, making celestial spheres incapable of rectilinear motion.

This passage presents the third argument used by philosophers to support the eternity of the heavens. It begins by defining direction as a perceptible goal, then argues that every direction must have a corporeal limiter, which turns out to be the celestial sphere itself. From there, Razi demonstrates why rectilinear motion is impossible for that sphere, which implies its indestructibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of direction
Read how Razi defines direction as something perceptible and the aim of a moving object. Note that direction is considered a real existent.
This is the starting premise.
2
Grasp the need for a corporeal limiter
Razi states that direction is limited and therefore requires a corporeal limiter, which can only be the encompassing sphere and its center. Recognize that this ties direction to the celestial structure.
The limiter is the celestial sphere itself.
3
Follow the argument against rectilinear motion
The limiter (celestial sphere) cannot undergo rectilinear motion because such motion would require the destination direction to exist before its cause, which is impossible.
This is a key step in the proof.
4
Connect to the conclusion about penetration and fusion
Since rectilinear motion is impossible, penetration (kharq) and fusion (ilti'am) are also impossible for the celestial sphere, supporting its indestructibility.
This is the desired conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة لهم : الجهة شيء مشار إليه بالحس - وهو مقصد المتحرك - وكل ما كان كذلك فهو موجود وهذه الجهة محدودة، فلا بد لها من محدد جسماني، ولا يحصل هذا التحديد إلا بالمحيط والمركز، وتقرير هذه المقدمات قد ذكرت في الكتب الحكمية.
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذا المحدد غير قابل للحركة المستقيمة ؛ لأن الحركة المستقيمة عبارة عن الانتقال من جهة إلى جهة وكل ما صح عليه ذلك كانت الجهة المنتقل عنها والجهة المنتقل إليها محددة قبل ذلك الجسم المنتقل إليها، فيلزم حصول الجهة قبل حصول علتها، وذلك محال، وإذا كان كذلك ثبت أن الحركة المستقيمة ممتنعة على الفلك المحدد، وإذا كان كذلك كان الخرق والالتئام ممتنعا عليه.
The third proof for them: Direction is something perceived by the senses—it is the aim of the moving thing—and everything that is like that exists. This direction is limited, so it must have a corporeal limiter, and this limitation only occurs by means of the encompassing [sphere] and the center. The exposition of these premises has been mentioned in the philosophical books. If this is established, we say: This limiter is not susceptible to rectilinear motion, because rectilinear motion is the transference from one direction to another, and for everything on which that is valid, the direction moved from and the direction moved to are limited before that body transferred to them, thus necessitating the existence of the direction before the existence of its cause, and that is impossible. If that is the case, it is established that rectilinear motion is impossible for the limited celestial sphere, and if that is the case, then penetration and fusion are impossible for it.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents the third philosophical argument for the eternity of celestial bodies. It begins by defining direction as something perceptible and the goal of a moving object, asserting that every such direction is limited. Since it is limited, it requires a corporeal limiter, which can only be achieved through the encompassing sphere and its center. Razi notes that these premises are elaborated in philosophical works. The conclusion is that this limiter (the celestial sphere) cannot undergo rectilinear motion, because rectilinear motion would require a pre-existing direction before the moving body arrives, leading to a priority of the direction over its cause. Therefore, rectilinear motion is impossible for the sphere, and consequently penetration (kharq) and fusion (ilti'am) are also impossible—this serves to support the philosophers' claim of the heavens' indestructibility.

Key Terms
جهة
محدد جسماني
حركة مستقيمة
خرق
التئام
فلك محدِّد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument proves that celestial spheres are simple and unchangeable in all respects.
Correction: The argument only denies rectilinear motion and penetration/fusion, not other types of motion or change.

Checkpoint
Why does the third proof claim that rectilinear motion is impossible for the celestial sphere?
A) Because the sphere is too large to move in a straight line.
B) Because it would require the direction to exist before its cause.
C) Because the sphere is composed of the four elements.
D) Because direction is not a real entity.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that celestial spheres cannot undergo rectilinear motion, thereby supporting their indestructibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that the heavens, by their nature, cannot change their course or be destroyed. How does this certainty about Allah's creation challenge your own habits of taking His signs for granted? Will you commit to observing the sky tonight as a permanent reminder of His power?
?
The philosophers argued that direction cannot exist before its cause. Reflect on your own life: are you often anxious about the future, acting as if the 'direction' of your life is without a cause? How can you entrust your next move to Allah, knowing He is the Cause of all directions?
?
If a celestial sphere cannot alter its motion due to the impossibility of a direction existing before its cause, how much more should you, a human with free will, acknowledge that every step you take is within Allah's predetermined limits? What is one step you can take today to consciously submit your movement to His will?
