Only God's Power Creates Miracles
Razi's second response: only God's power creates miracles, nullifying eight objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the principle that only God's power creates miracles to invalidate the deniers' eight objections.

Core Idea
Only God's power is effective; this single principle collapses all objections.

In this brief but decisive passage, Imam Razi delivers his second response to those who denied miracles. He recalls an earlier established principle—that no effecter exists except the power of God—and shows how this principle alone makes the eight previously mentioned objections irrelevant. The passage demonstrates the power of foundational theological axioms in resolving complex disputes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis to get the flow. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the concise structure: a quote from the opponent, then a terse reply.
2
Identify the key principle
Razi says 'there is no effecter except the power of God.' Understand what 'effecter' means: the one who brings about effects. This is the foundational principle.
3
Understand the consequence
Razi says 'on this estimation the eight aspects fall away.' Recognize that this is a logical deduction: if only God's power is effective, then any objections based on secondary causes become moot.
The eight aspects were presumably objections from the deniers; they are invalidated.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this principle is so powerful in kalam arguments about miracles. It shifts the focus from physical causality to divine agency.
This is a key element of Ash'ari occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ثانيا: "ثم قلتم: إن موجد هذه المعجزات هو الله تعالى؟" قلنا: قد بينا فيما تقدم أنه لا مؤثر إلا قدرة الله وعلى هذا التقدير تسقط الوجوه الثمانية التي ذكرتموها.
His saying, second: 'Then you say that the creator of these miracles is God Almighty?' We say: We have already shown in what preceded that there is no effecter except the power of God, and on this estimation the eight aspects that you mentioned fall away.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is the second response from Razi to the deniers. The deniers questioned whether God is the creator (mujid) of these miracles. Razi replies that it has already been established that no effecter (mu'aththir) exists except the power (qudra) of God. Given this principle (taqdir), the eight aspects (al-wujuh al-thamaniyya) that the deniers had previously enumerated regarding the impossibility or issues with miracles are invalidated (tasqut). The response reaffirms that since only God's power has real causal efficacy, any objections premised on assigning agency to others are unfounded.

Key Terms
مؤثر (effecter)
قدرة الله (God's power)
وجوه ثمانية (eight aspects)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributing miracles solely to God is an inconsequential detail that does not affect other objections.
Correction: Razi shows that the principle 'no effecter except the power of God' is the foundation that makes all eight prior objections irrelevant.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the result of establishing that only God's power is effective?
A) It proves that miracles are impossible.
B) It makes the deniers' eight objections irrelevant.
C) It shows that prophets have no role in miracles.
D) It confirms that miracles are natural events.

Key Takeaway
The principle that only God's power creates miracles invalidates all objections premised on secondary causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a moment you'd label 'coincidence' or 'good luck', do you actually feel that God's power alone caused it, or does your heart secretly attribute it to chance or your own effort? What concrete thought habit could you adopt to train your heart to see only God's hand in every event, especially when something extraordinary happens?
?
The deniers listed eight objections to miracles. What objections or excuses does your own mind raise when you hear about supernatural events in Islamic history? Do you instinctively rationalize them away, or do you let the principle of God's unlimited power silence your doubts? What does your reaction reveal about your faith in God's absolute sovereignty?
?
If you truly internalized that 'no effecter except the power of God' applies to every second of your life—your health, income, relationships—how would your daily reliance on people, money, or your own abilities change? Name one specific attachment you struggle to release and a practical step you will take this week to entrust it to God alone.

Second Proof Against Taklif: God's Foreknowledge
Argues that God's foreknowledge makes taklif either pointless or unjust.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's foreknowledge renders the obligation (taklif) either futile or unjust.

Core Idea
God's foreknowledge renders taklif either pointless or unjust.

This passage presents the second proof against the Ashʿari doctrine of taklif (moral obligation). The argument leverages God's all-encompassing knowledge to show that commanding actions is either unnecessary or oppressive. As you read, notice how the proof unfolds in two parallel branches, each leading to a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the binary structure: known to occur vs. known not to occur.
2
Identify the two branches
The argument presents two cases: if the commanded act is known to occur, taklif is futile; if known not to occur, taklif is unjust. Identify how each leads to the conclusion.
3
Examine the alternative formulation
The passage mentions another way the argument is presented, focusing on reward. Understand how this version parallels the first.
Reward replaces the commanded act as the object of knowledge.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof concludes that taklif is invalid. Consider what assumptions about God's knowledge and the purpose of obligation are needed for this argument to work.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن الباري سبحانه وتعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات، والشيء الذي حصل التكليف به إما أن يكون معلوم الوقوع أو معلوم اللاوقوع، فإن كان معلوم الوقوع كان واجب الوقوع، فالتكليف به عبث وإن كان معلوم اللاوقوع كان ممتنع الوقوع، فكان التكليف به ظلمًا فثبت أن القول بالتكليف باطل. وربما ذكروا هذا الكلام في معرض آخر، وهو أن المقصود من التكليف جلب الثواب، وإن كان ذلك الثواب معلوم الوقوع فحينئذ لا حاجة البتة إلى فعل الطاعة، وإن كان معلوم اللاوقوع كان ممتنع الوقوع، وحينئذ لا فائدة في فعل الطاعة.
The second: that the Creator, glorified and exalted, is knower of all known things. The thing for which taklif occurs is either known to occur or known not to occur. If it is known to occur, it is necessary of occurrence, so taklif with it is futile. And if it is known not to occur, it is impossible of occurrence, so taklif with it is injustice. So it is established that the statement of taklif is invalid. And perhaps they mention this speech in another context: that the purpose of taklif is to attract reward. If that reward is known to occur, then there is no need at all for performing obedience. And if it is known not to occur, its occurrence is impossible, and then there is no benefit in performing obedience.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This lesson presents the second proof against taklif, based on God's foreknowledge. The argument states: God knows all things. The act commanded by taklif is either known by God to occur or known not to occur. If it is known to occur, then it is necessary (wājib al-wuqūʿ), so commanding it is futile—it would happen anyway. If it is known not to occur, it is impossible (mumtaniʿ al-wuqūʿ), so commanding it is unjust—it demands the impossible. Thus taklif is invalid. An alternate formulation focuses on the reward: if the reward for obedience is known to occur, no effort is needed; if known not to occur, effort is useless. Both versions conclude taklif is pointless or unjust.

Key Terms
علم الله (ʿilm Allāh)
واجب الوقوع (wājib al-wuqūʿ)
ممتنع الوقوع (mumtaniʿ al-wuqūʿ)
ثواب (thawāb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's foreknowledge means human actions are predetermined, so obligation is meaningless.
Correction: This argument assumes that God's knowledge causes the occurrence, but Ashʿari theologians hold that knowledge does not cause; it merely aligns with what will happen. Obligation can still have purpose as a test or means of reward.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, if God knows an act will occur, what is the problem with commanding it?
A) It would be impossible to do
B) It would be unjust because it demands the impossible
C) It would be futile because it is necessary
D) It would be a contradiction in God's will

Key Takeaway
The second proof claims that taklif is invalid because God's foreknowledge leaves no room for genuine obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God already knows whether you will obey or disobey, why would your personal effort in performing obligations matter? Yet the Quran commands you to strive. How does this tension affect your intention when you perform an act of worship? Write down one specific way you will renew your intention in the next prayer, acknowledging both God's knowledge and your responsibility.
?
Think of a time you knowingly committed a sin, thinking that God knew you would do it anyway so it didn't matter. This lesson exposes that reasoning as flawed. What is that sin? Now, in light of the argument, how can you still see the command as meaningful and avoid that sin next time? What concrete step will you take to remind yourself that obedience is not futile?
?
The argument says taklif is unjust if the act is known not to occur. But you know that God commands even those He knows will disbelieve. How does that reality make you feel about the commands you struggle to fulfill? Do you ever feel that God is unfair for commanding what He knows you won't do? Confront that feeling honestly: what does it reveal about your view of God's justice and mercy?

Fourth Proof: Taklif Has No Benefit
Argues that taklif has no benefit, thus is futile and unworthy of a wise being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that taklif has no benefit and therefore cannot proceed from a wise God.

Core Idea
Taklif is futile because any possible benefit can be granted directly by God.

This lesson presents the fourth proof from Imam al-Razi's Arba'in against the validity of taklif. The argument examines the concept of benefit (fāʾida) and shows that taklif serves no real benefit that God could not provide directly, making it an act of futility ('abath) unbefitting divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the argument come through.
Notice the logical structure: claim, then two conditional analyses, then conclusion.
2
Identify the two possible recipients of benefit
The argument splits the possibility of benefit into two: it either returns to God or to the worshipper. Pause and consider why these are the only options.
This is a disjunctive syllogism.
3
Analyze why benefit cannot return to God
If benefit returned to God, He would be subject to increase/decrease and harm/benefit—attributes of contingent beings. Since God is Necessary, this is impossible.
4
Analyze why benefit to the worshipper is unnecessary
All possible benefits for the worshipper reduce to obtaining pleasure or removing pain. God can grant these directly without the intermediary of taklif. Thus taklif is an unnecessary burden.
The argument assumes that the only real benefits are pleasure/pain avoidance.
5
Understand the conclusion
Since both options fail, taklif has no benefit. An act with no benefit is futile ('abath), and futility is unworthy of the Wisest of judges. Therefore taklif is invalid.
This conclusion rests on the principle that God's actions must have wisdom and purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن التكليف لا فائدة فيه البتة، فكان عبئًا غير لائق بحكمة الحكيم. وإنما قلنا إنه لا فائدة فيه؛ لأن تلك الفائدة إن عادت إلى المعبود لزم أن يكون في محل النفع والضرر والزيادة والنقصان وهو محال وإن عادت إلى العابد فهو أيضًا باطل ؛ لأن جميع الفوائد محصورة في أمرين : إما اللذة وإما السرور، أو ما يكون مفضيًا إليهما، وإما دفع الألم أو دفع الغم، أو ما يكون مفضيّا إليهما، والمعبود تعالى قادر على تحصيل كل ذلك للعبد بدون واسطة التكليف، فكان توسط التكليف عبثا، وإذا ثبت هذا فظاهر أن العبث لا يليق بأحكم الحاكمين.
The fourth: that taklif has no benefit at all, so it is a burden unbefitting the wisdom of the Wise. And we say it has no benefit because if that benefit returns to the Worshipped One, then He would be subject to benefit and harm, increase and decrease—which is impossible. And if it returns to the worshipper, it is also invalid, because all benefits are confined to two things: either pleasure (al-ladhdha) and joy (al-surūr), or what leads to them, or the removal of pain (al-alam) or removal of grief (al-ghamm), or what leads to them. And the Worshipped One, exalted, is able to obtain all of that for the servant without the intermediary of taklif. So the intermediation of taklif is futile. And when this is established, it is clear that futility is not befitting the Wisest of judges.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This lesson presents the fourth proof: taklif has no benefit (fāʾida), so it is futile and unbefitting God's wisdom. The proof analyzes the possible recipients of benefit. Benefit cannot return to God because that would imply change and imperfection, which is impossible. Benefit to the worshipper is also unnecessary because all possible benefits fall into two categories: obtaining pleasure/joy or removing pain/grief. Since God is able to grant these directly without the intermediary of taklif (obligation), taklif is an unnecessary burden. Hence it is futile, and futility is unworthy of the Wisest of judges. Therefore taklif is invalid.

Key Terms
فائدة (fāʾida)
عبث (ʿabath)
لذة (ladhdha)
سرور (surūr)
دفع الألم (dafʿ al-alam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that taklif has intrinsic benefit, such as spiritual growth or reward in the afterlife, which God cannot grant without the obligation.
Correction: The argument assumes that all genuine benefits are either pleasure or the removal of pain, and God can grant these directly. It dismisses the possibility that the act of obedience itself has value independent of its outcomes.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, why can't benefit from taklif return to God?
A) God is too great for any benefit to reach Him
B) God would then be subject to change, which is impossible
C) God already knows the outcome
D) God has no need of worship

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof argues that since all possible benefits of taklif are either impossible to return to God or unnecessary for the worshipper, taklif is futile and cannot be attributed to a wise God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you could receive all of God's blessings—heavenly pleasures, protection from pain—without ever praying, fasting, or giving charity, would you still choose to worship? What does your honest answer reveal about your understanding of worship's purpose?
?
The proof claims that every benefit of taklif could be given directly by God. Can you think of a benefit you gain through worship that cannot be reduced to pleasure, joy, or the removal of pain? If not, does this challenge your commitment to religious duties?
?
When you find yourself burdened by religious obligations—waking up for Fajr, giving up a sin—do you ever feel that God could have simply made things easier? How do you reconcile the idea of a 'wise God' with the apparent futility of hardship in worship?

Fifth Proof: Taklif of Those Known to Disbelieve
Argues that commanding someone God knows will disbelieve leads to absurdity or injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that arises from imposing religious obligations on individuals whom God knows will disbelieve, and why this challenges the wisdom of such taklif.

Core Idea
Commanding someone God knows will disbelieve leads to either absurdity or injustice.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from Imam al-Razi's Al-Arba'een on the topic of divine attributes and qadar. The argument targets the very notion of taklif (religious obligation) imposed on those whose future disbelief is already known to God. It uses a dilemma to show that such taklif either implies ignorance in God or results in unjust punishment, both of which are incompatible with divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find the two conditional statements: what happens if the commanded act occurs, and what happens if it does not occur.
Notice the terms 'تجهيل' and 'استحقاق العقاب'.
3
Understand why each horn is problematic
Reflect on why making God ignorant is impossible, and why deserving punishment without benefit is unjust.
The argument assumes divine wisdom requires avoiding both.
4
Consider the conclusion
The passage concludes that such taklif is not befitting wisdom, since no benefit arises except these two reprehensible outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: أن تكليف من علم أنه يكفر أو يفسق غير لائق بالحكمة؛ لأن المكلف به إذا دخل في الوجود لزم تجهيل المعبود، وإن لم يدخل في الوجود لزم استحقاق العبد للعقاب، وفعل شيء لا فائدة فيه - إلا أحد هذين الأمرين المحذورين - لا يليق بالحكمة.
The fifth: that taklif of one whom He knows will disbelieve or act wickedly is not befitting wisdom, because if the commanded act occurs, it necessitates making the Worshipped One ignorant (tajhīl al-maʿbūd), and if it does not occur, it necessitates the servant's deserving punishment. And doing something with no benefit except one of these two reprehensible things is not befitting wisdom.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This lesson presents the fifth proof: it is unwise to impose taklif on a person whom God knows will disbelieve or sin. The argument presents a dilemma. If the commanded act (e.g., faith) actually occurs, then God's foreknowledge that he would disbelieve would be false, implying ignorance of God—an absurdity. If the act does not occur (as God foreknew), then the servant becomes liable to punishment for not performing an act that was impossible for him to perform (since God knew he wouldn't). Both outcomes are problematic: either God becomes ignorant (impossible) or the servant suffers unjustly. Since no benefit arises except these two reprehensible options, such taklif is not wise. Hence taklif is invalid.

Key Terms
تجهيل (tajhīl)
استحقاق العقاب (istiḥqāq al-ʿiqāb)
حكمة (ḥikma)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this proof is invalid because taklif could still be a test or manifest justice even if the outcome is foreknown.
Correction: The proof does not deny that taklif could have some purpose; rather, it argues that the specific dilemma (tajhīl or unjust punishment) makes any such taklif irrational, as both options are directly problematic.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth proof, what happens if the commanded act occurs for a person God knew would disbelieve?
A) The servant becomes righteous
B) It necessitates making God ignorant
C) The servant is punished
D) Taklif is fulfilled

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof shows that imposing taklif on someone God knows will disbelieve is incompatible with divine wisdom because it leads to either ignorance in God or unjust punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that it is 'not befitting wisdom' to command someone God knows will fail, does your heart feel any resistance? Is there any command of Allah that you treat as if it's impossible for you, thereby making His command seem pointless? How can you embrace that command as a genuine test today?
?
If God's foreknowledge means your future disbelief is already known, does that make you feel less responsible? This argument shows that such a view leads to injustice. Do you ever use 'it's my destiny' as an excuse to avoid effort? What one area of your life can you change starting now to take full responsibility before Allah?
?
The author insists that God's actions must have wisdom. Think of a trial you faced that seemed pointless. Does this lesson challenge you to trust that even that trial had a wise purpose, even if you don't see it? Write down one thing you will do this week to act on that trust.

The Terms 'Mustafayn' and 'Akhyar' as Proof of Infallibility
Razi argues that Quranic 'mustafayn' and 'akhyar' imply prophetic infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic description of prophets as 'mustafayn' and 'akhyar' serves as a proof for their infallibility, and how Imam al-Razi refutes an objection based on Surah Fatir.

Core Idea
The comprehensiveness of 'mustafayn' and 'akhyar' implies prophets are sinless.

Imam al-Razi now presents a linguistic- theological argument: the Quran calls prophets 'mustafayn' (chosen) and 'akhyar' (excellent). He argues that these terms are all-encompassing, covering every action and omission, and thus logically exclude sin. He then anticipates a potential counterexample from the Quran and resolves it through careful pronoun analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the complete Arabic passage once. Pay attention to the key terms 'mustafayn' and 'akhyar' and the reasoning about exception.
The passage includes both the main argument and the refutation of an objection.
2
Identify the proof's structure
Notice how al-Razi first presents the verse, then argues that the terms cover all acts and abstentions using the exception test, and concludes that sin is negated.
The exception test is crucial: if you can say 'except in X', the terms are comprehensive.
3
Examine the objection
Al-Razi raises an objection: don't other verses (like Fatir 35:32) show that chosen people can be sinners?
The objection would undermine the proof if unanswered.
4
See the rebuttal
Al-Razi's rebuttal hinges on pronoun reference: the pronoun 'from them' in Fatir refers back to 'our servants', not to 'those We chose', so the verse does not attribute sin to the chosen ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة: قال الله تعالى في صفة إبراهيم وإسحاق ويعقوب: ﴿وَإِنَّهُمْ عِندَنَا لَمِنَ الْمُصْطَفَيْنَ الْأَخْيَارِ﴾ [ص: ٤٧] وهذان اللفظان – أعني المصطفين و 'الأخيار' – يتناولان جملة الأفعال والتروك، بدليل جواز الاستثناء، يقال: فلان من المصطفين الأخيار إلا في كذا، والاستثناء يخرج من الكلام ما لولاه لدخل. فدلت هذه الآية على أنهم كانوا من المصطفين الأخيار في كل الأمور، وهذا ينافي صدور الذنب عنهم. ونظيره قوله تعالى: ﴿اللهُ يَصْطَفِي مِنَ الْمَلَائِكَةِ رُسُلًا وَمِنَ النَّاسِ﴾ [الحج: ٧٥] وكذلك قوله: ﴿إِنَّ اللهَ اصْطَفَى آدَمَ وَنُوحًا وَآلَ إِبْرَاهِيمَ وَآلَ عِمْرَانَ عَلَى الْعَالَمِينَ﴾ [آل عمران: ٣٣] وقال في حق إبراهيم: ﴿وَلَقَدِ اصْطَفَيْنَاهُ فِي الدُّنْيَا وَإِنَّهُ فِي الْآخِرَةِ لَمِنَ الصَّالِحِينَ﴾ [البقرة: ١٣٠] وقال في حق موسى: ﴿إِنِّي اصْطَفَيْتُكَ عَلَى النَّاسِ بِرِسَالَاتِي وَبِكَلَامِي﴾ [الأعراف: ١٤٤] وقال تعالى: ﴿وَاذْكُرْ عِبَادَنَا إِبْرَاهِيمَ وَإِسْحَاقَ وَيَعْقُوبَ أُولِي الْأَيْدِي إِنَّا أَخْلَصْنَاهُم بِخَالِصَةٍ ذِكْرَى الدَّارِ وَإِنَّهُمْ عِندَنَا لَمِنَ الْمُصْطَفَيْنَ الْأَخْيَارِ﴾ [ص: ٤٥-٤٧] ولا يقال: الاصطفاء لا يمنع من فعل الذنب، بدليل قوله تعالى: ﴿ثُمَّ أَوْرَثْنَا الْكِتَابَ الَّذِينَ اصْطَفَيْنَا مِنْ عِبَادِنَا فَمِنْهُمْ ظَالِمٌ لِّنَفْسِهِ وَمِنْهُم مُّقْتَصِدٌ وَمِنْهُمْ سَابِقٌ بِالْخَيْرَاتِ﴾ [فاطر: ٣٢] قسم المصطفين إلى الظالم والمقتصد والسابق. نقول: الضمير في قوله 'فمنهم' عائد إلى قوله 'من عبادنا' لا إلى قوله 'اصطفينا'؛ لأن عود الضمير إلى أقرب مذكور واجب.
The eighth proof: Allah Almighty said describing Ibrahim, Ishaq, and Ya'qub: 'Indeed they are with Us among the chosen and the excellent [mustafayn al-akhyar].' [Sad 38:47] These two terms – I mean 'mustafayn' and 'akhyar' – encompass all acts and abstentions, as evidenced by the permissibility of exception: it is said 'So-and-so is among the chosen and excellent except in such-and-such,' and the exception removes from the speech what would otherwise be included. So this verse indicates that they were among the chosen and excellent in all matters, and this negates the occurrence of sin from them. Similar to His saying: 'Allah chooses messengers from the angels and from the people' [Hajj 22:75], and His saying: 'Indeed Allah chose Adam, Noah, the family of Ibrahim, and the family of Imran over the worlds' [Al-Imran 3:33], and He said regarding Ibrahim: 'And we certainly chose him in this world, and indeed he in the Hereafter is among the righteous' [Al-Baqarah 2:130], and He said regarding Musa: 'I have chosen you over the people with My messages and My speech' [Al-A'raf 7:144], and He said: 'And remember our servants Ibrahim, Ishaq, and Ya'qub, men of strength... Indeed We purified them with a pure quality, the remembrance of the home [of the Hereafter], and indeed they are with Us among the chosen and the excellent' [Sad 38:45-47]. And it cannot be said that 'istifa'' (choosing) does not prevent sin, as evidenced by His saying: 'Then We made inherit the Book those whom We chose from among Our servants; among them are the wrongdoer to himself, and among them are the moderate, and among them are the foremost in good deeds' [Fatir 35:32], dividing the chosen into the wrongdoer, the moderate, and the foremost. We say: the pronoun in 'faminhum' refers to 'min ibadina' (from Our servants), not to 'istafayna' (We chose), because the pronoun's reference to the nearest antecedent is obligatory.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a proof (hujja) for the infallibility of prophets. Imam al-Razi presents an argument based on the Qur'anic description of prophets as 'mustafayn' (chosen ones) and 'akhyar' (excellent ones). He argues that these terms are comprehensive, covering all actions and omissions. The evidence for their comprehensiveness is that it is permissible to make an exception: one can say 'He is among the chosen and excellent except in such-and-such,' meaning the exception removes something that would otherwise be included. Therefore, if the verse says they are 'mustafayn' and 'akhyar' without exception, it implies they are so in every respect, which precludes sin. He then supports this with other similar verses that use 'istifa'' for various prophets. Then he addresses a potential objection: that 'istifa'' is not necessarily comprehensive, because in Surah Fatir, Allah divides the chosen ones (those who inherit the Book) into wrongdoers, moderates, and foremost. The objector might claim that this shows that being chosen does not exclude sin. Al-Razi refutes this by pointing out that the pronoun 'faminhum' in that verse refers back to 'min ibadina' (from Our servants), not to 'alladhina istafayna' (those whom We chose). The rule of pronoun reference requires that it refer to the nearest antecedent, so the division applies to the servants, not specifically to the chosen ones. Therefore, the objection fails.

Key Terms
المصطفين (mustafayn)
الأخيار (akhyar)
الاستثناء (exception)
عود الضمير (pronoun reference)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'istifa'' (choosing) in the Quran does not necessarily exclude sin, as Surah Fatir mentions chosen ones who are wrongdoers.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that in Surah Fatir, the pronoun 'from them' refers to 'our servants', not to 'those We chose', so the verse does not attribute sin to the chosen ones.

Checkpoint
In al-Razi's eighth proof, how does he argue that the terms 'mustafayn' and 'akhyar' encompass all actions and abstentions?
A) By citing consensus of scholars
B) By noting that exception is possible, indicating inclusiveness
C) By stating that the prophets themselves claimed it
D) By relying on a hadith

Key Takeaway
The description of prophets as 'mustafayn' and 'akhyar' without exception indicates their comprehensive goodness, establishing infallibility.

Angels Superior in Power, Not Reward: Razi's First Argument
Razi argues that the verse about Jesus shows angels are superior in power but not necessarily in reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses the verse 'The Messiah will never disdain to be a servant of God' to argue that angels are superior to humans in power but not necessarily in reward.

Core Idea
Angels exceed humans in power, but reward depends on humility, not strength.

In this argument, Imam al-Razi addresses a common Christian claim that Jesus's miracles prove his divinity. He clarifies that the Quranic response actually shows that even the angels who are far more powerful than Jesus remain humble servants of God. This leads to a nuanced conclusion about the relative status of angels and humans—superiority in power does not guarantee superior reward.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise about reward and humility
The passage begins by stating that abundant reward comes only from extreme humility. Recognize that this premise is key to the argument.
2
See how the verse is used against Christian claims
Christians claimed Jesus's divinity based on his miracles. The verse rebukes this by stating that Jesus and the angels do not disdain servitude to God.
3
Distinguish between power and reward
The verse establishes that angels are greater in power, but it does not indicate they have more reward. This is the core conclusion.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
The argument also includes a second perspective: angels are created without parents, so they are even more wondrous than Jesus, yet they remain servants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة والثلاثون إن كثرة الثواب لا تحصل إلا بنهاية التواضع والخضوع، وكون العبد موصوفا بنهاية التواضع لله تعالى لا يلائم صيرورته مستنكفًا عن عبودية الله تعالى، بل ينافيها ويناقضها، فامتنع أن يكون المراد من الآية هذا المعنى، أما اتصاف الشخص بالقدرة الشديدة والقوة الكاملة فإنه مناسب للتمرد وترك العبودية. والنصارى لما شاهدوا من المسيح إحياء الموتى وإبراء الأكمه والأبرص أخرجوه بسبب هذا القدر من القدرة عن عبودية الله تعالى فقال تعالى: (لَنْ يَسْتَنْكِفَ الْمَسِيحُ أن يكون عَبْدًا لِلَّهِ ﴾ [النساء : ۱۷۲] أي أن عيسى لن يستنكف بهذا القدر من القدرة عن عبوديتي ولا الملائكة المقربون الذين هم فوقه في القدرة والبطش والاستيلاء على عالم السماوات والأرضين. وبهذا الوجه تنتظم دلالة الآية على أن الملك أفضل من البشر في القدرة والبطش والشدة لكنها لا تدل البتة على أن الملك أفضل من البشر في كثرة الثواب . أو يقال : إنهم إنما ادعوا الهية المسيح لأنه حصل من غير أب فقيل لهم : الملائكة حصلوا لا من أب ولا أم، فكانوا أعجب من عيسى في هذا الباب مع أنهم لا يستنكفون عن عبودية الله تعالى. هذا الاستدلال،
The Thirty-Third Issue: Indeed, abundance of reward is not attained except by extreme humility and submission. And the servant being described with extreme humility to God Almighty is not compatible with his becoming disdainful of the worship of God Almighty; rather it contradicts and opposes it. Thus it is impossible that the intended meaning of the verse is this. As for a person being described with severe power and complete strength, it is suitable for rebellion and abandonment of worship. And when the Christians saw from the Messiah the reviving of the dead and the healing of the blind and the leper, they removed him, because of this amount of power, from the servitude of God Almighty, so God Almighty said: 'The Messiah will never disdain to be a servant of God' [An-Nisa 4:172], meaning that Jesus will not disdain with this amount of power from My worship, nor the angels brought near, who are above him in power, might, and control over the world of the heavens and the earths. And in this way the indication of the verse is established that the angel is superior to humans in power, might, and severity, but it does not indicate at all that the angel is superior to humans in abundance of reward. Or it is said: They only claimed the divinity of the Messiah because he was born without a father, so it was said to them: The angels were born without a father or mother, so they are more wondrous than Jesus in this regard, while they do not disdain the worship of God Almighty. This is the argument.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument in the Thirty-Third Issue about whether angels are superior to humans. It begins by stating a premise: abundant reward comes only from extreme humility. Being extremely humble toward God is incompatible with being disdainful of His worship. Therefore, the verse cannot mean that Jesus's lack of disdain is what made him worthy of reward, because that would contradict the premise. Rather, the Christians saw Jesus's miracles (reviving the dead, healing the blind and leper) and mistakenly elevated him to divinity. God responded that Jesus does not disdain being a servant, and neither do the angels who are greater in power. This indicates that angels are superior in power and might, but not necessarily in reward. The argument is then supported by an alternative interpretation: the Christians claimed Jesus's divinity because he was born without a father, but angels are even more wondrous as they were created without father or mother, yet they do not disdain worship.

التواضع والخضوع
الاستكبار
الملائكة المقربون
القدرة والبطش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the verse directly proves angels are superior in reward.
Correction: Razi clarifies it only shows superiority in power, not necessarily in reward.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the first argument in the Thirty-Third Issue?
A) Angels are superior to humans in reward.
B) Angels are superior to humans in power but not necessarily in reward.
C) Humans are superior to angels.
D) The verse does not discuss angels.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi demonstrates that the verse about Jesus and the angels primarily establishes angelic superiority in power, not in reward, thus refuting Christian claims while maintaining the distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Christians were so impressed by Jesus’s power (miracles) that they elevated him to divinity. Are there people you admire—scholars, leaders, influencers—whose worldly power or success makes you overlook whether they are truly humble before Allah? How might this affect your own spiritual priorities?
?
Razi notes that the angels, with all their immense power and control over the heavens and earth, never disdain to worship Allah. When do you feel you are “too important,” “too busy,” or “too much” to serve Allah with full humility? What is one act of worship you can perform today with the specific intention of cultivating greater khushūʿ (humility)?

The Second Proof: Angels' Lack of Arrogance
Razi presents the second proof and its objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for angelic superiority based on Quran 21:19 and the objections that challenge its strength.

Core Idea
The argument from lack of arrogance only proves strength, not superiority in reward.

Imam Razi now presents a second scriptural argument for the superiority of angels over humans, drawing on a verse that highlights the angels' humility. However, this proof is not without its critics, and Razi faithfully records two objections that challenge its logical force. Understanding this debate is crucial for grasping the subtleties of theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then consult the literal translation. Notice how the proof is structured into two distinct aspects.
2
Understand the first aspect
Focus on the first inference: God uses angels' lack of arrogance to argue that humans should not be arrogant. This works only if angels are stronger than humans.
The analogy is like a king citing the obedience of other kings to command his subjects.
3
Understand the second aspect
The second inference is about the phrase 'those who are with Him' indicating closeness and honor, not spatial proximity.
This aspect emphasizes virtue rather than power.
4
Analyze the objections
Razi provides an objection to each aspect. The first objection says the argument only shows strength, not greater reward. The second objection counter-cites verses showing humans have an even more intimate closeness to God.
Consider how these objections undermine the original proof.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Think about whether the proof is convincing after the objections. This passage shows that theological proofs are often contested and require careful reasoning.
This lesson is part of a larger debate about angelic versus human superiority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لمن قال بتفضيل الملك على البشر التمسك بقوله تعالى : ﴿وَمَنْ عنده لَا يَسْتَكْبِرُونَ عَنْ عِبَادَتِهِ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ۱۹] والاستدلال به من وجهين : الأول : أنه تعالى احتج بعدم استكبار الملائكة عن عبادته على أن البشر يجب أن لا يستكبروا عنها، فلو كان البشر أفضل من الملائكة لما تم السلطان إذا أراد أن يقرر على رعيته وجوب طاعتهم له فإنه يقول : الملوك لا يستكبرون عن طاعتي، فمن هؤلاء المساكين ؟ وبالجملة فظاهر أنه يلزم أن هذا الاستدلال لا يتم إلا بالأقوى على الأضعف. الثاني : أنه تعالى قال : وَمَنْ عِندَهُ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ۱۹] وهذه العندية ليست عندية بالجهة ، بل عندية بالفضيلة والقرب. والاعتراض على الوجه الأول لعل المراد أن الملائكة مع شدة قوتهم لا يتمردون عن طاعة الله فما بال البشر يتمردون عن طاعة الله مع غاية قصورهم وحقارتهم ؟! وهذا يوجب كون الملك أقوى من البشر لكنه لا يوجب كونه أفضل من البشر - بمعنى كثرة الثواب .. واعتراض على الوجه الثاني: أنه معارض بقوله تعالى في صفة البشر : في مَقْعَدِ صِدْقٍ عِندَ مَلِيكٍ مُقتَدِرٍ [القَمَر : ٥٥] وقال حكاية عن الله تعالى: «أنا عند المنكسرة قلوبهم وهذا أفضل لأنه قال في صفة الملائكة : إنهم عند ربهم، وقال في صفة المنكسرة قلوبهم : إن ربهم عندهم.
The second proof: For those who say that angels are superior to humans, they hold fast to His statement: 'And those who are with Him are not arrogant towards His worship' [Al-Anbiya 21:19]. And the inference from it is from two aspects: First: God Almighty argued by the angels' lack of arrogance towards His worship that humans must not be arrogant towards it. If humans were better than angels, the argument would not be sound when a ruler wants to establish upon his subjects the obligation of obeying him, for he would say: 'Kings are not arrogant towards my obedience, so who are these wretches?' In summary, it is apparent that this inference is only completed with the stronger upon the weaker. Second: He said 'those who are with Him,' and this 'with-ness' is not a spatial with-ness, but a with-ness of virtue and proximity. The objection to the first aspect: Perhaps the meaning is that the angels, despite their intense strength, do not rebel against the obedience of God, so what about humans rebelling against the obedience of God despite their extreme weakness and lowliness? This requires that the angel is stronger than humans, but it does not require that he is better than humans in the sense of abundance of reward. And an objection to the second aspect: It is opposed by His statement describing humans: 'In a seat of truth with an Omnipotent King' [Al-Qamar 54:55], and He narrated from God Almighty: 'I am with those whose hearts are broken,' and this is better because He said in describing the angels: 'They are with their Lord,' and He said in describing the broken-hearted: 'Their Lord is with them.'
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the superiority of angels over humans, based on Quran 21:19. The proof has two aspects. First, God uses the angels' lack of arrogance to argue that humans should not be arrogant either. If humans were superior to angels, this argument would be like a king telling his subjects that kings obey him, so why don't you? The logic only works if what is being cited is stronger (angels) to press the weaker (humans). Second, the phrase 'those who are with Him' indicates proximity and virtue, not physical location. Then two objections are raised. To the first: the argument only shows angels are stronger, not better in reward. To the second: there are verses that describe humans with a more intimate proximity: 'in a seat of truth with an Omnipotent King' and a hadith qudsi saying 'I am with those whose hearts are broken.' This shows that humans can have a closeness that angels do not, suggesting human superiority in some sense.

Key Terms
الاستدلال
الوجه الأول
الوجه الثاني
العندية
الملك
البشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the verse directly proves angels are superior in reward.
Correction: The objection shows that the verse only proves strength, not greater reward, and other verses suggest humans can have even greater proximity.

Checkpoint
What is the second aspect of the inference from the verse 'And those who are with Him are not arrogant'?
A) It proves spatial proximity.
B) It indicates closeness in virtue and honor.
C) It shows humans are better.
D) It proves angels are more powerful.

Key Takeaway
This passage illustrates that scriptural proofs often have nuanced logical structures and are subject to counterarguments that distinguish between different kinds of excellence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that angels do not show arrogance towards God's worship, what does it reveal about the moments you find yourself feeling too important or too busy to pray? Will you commit to catching yourself in those moments and humbling yourself?
?
The ruler analogy compares angels to kings and humans to wretched subjects. Have you ever truly considered your own lowliness before God? How would your daily worship change if you began each prayer with that awareness?
?
The objection says the verse only proves strength, not reward. Are you more concerned with appearing strong and capable in your religious practice, or with the inner quality of your deeds that brings reward? What is one thing you will change today to prioritize sincerity over show?

Al-Razi's Second Proof: The Logical Aporia of Obligating Reward
Al-Razi's second proof using logical disjunction to refute obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's second proof against the Mu'tazili view that obedience necessitates reward, and how it preserves divine freedom and perfection.

Core Idea
If reward were obligatory, God would either be a machine or deficient.

In this dense passage, al-Razi deploys a perfect logical disjunction. He shows that the notion of 'obligation' on God leads to two equally impossible outcomes. This proof is a masterpiece of kalam reasoning, defending God's absolute free agency and perfection. Let's walk through it step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read Al-Razi's argument
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: 'if... then either... or...'. The argument moves by eliminating both possibilities.
2
Trace the two branches
Identify the two possibilities: impossible to not reward, or possible to not reward. What follows from each?
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Both branches lead to absurdities, so the original premise must be false. Why does this matter for God's attributes? This refutes the Mu'tazili position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لو كان العمل علة لوجوب الثواب لكان إما أن يمتنع من أن لا يثيب أو يصح فإن امتنع أن لا يثيب فحينئذ يكون الصانع علة موجبة لذلك الثواب لا فاعلا مختاراً، وإن صح فبتقدير أن لا يثيب إن لم يصر مستحقا للذم لم يتحقق معنى الوجوب، وإن صار مستحقا للذم لزم أن يكون ناقصا لذاته مستكملا بسبب ذلك الفعل الذي يفعله، وذلك محال.
The second proof: If the act were a cause for the obligation of reward, then it would either be impossible that He not reward, or it would be possible. If it is impossible that He not reward, then the Maker would be a necessitating cause of that reward, not a free agent. And if it is possible, then assuming that He does not reward, if He does not become deserving of blame, the meaning of obligation is not realized; and if He becomes deserving of blame, then it would imply that He is deficient in His essence and perfecting Himself by means of that act which He does, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage presents al-Razi's second proof (al-ḥujjah al-thāniyah) against the Mu'tazili view that obedience is a cause for obligatory reward. He uses a disjunction (taqsīm): if the act were a cause for the obligation of reward, then either it is impossible for God to not reward, or it is possible. If impossible, then God is a necessitating cause (mu'aththir mūjib) rather than a free agent (fā'il mukhtār), which contradicts divine freedom. If possible, then consider the case where God does not reward: if He does not become blameworthy, then there is no real obligation; if He does become blameworthy, then that would imply deficiency in God's essence and that He perfects Himself through another's act, which is impossible for the perfect God. Thus, the initial premise that obedience is a cause for obligation leads to absurdity, so the Mu'tazili position is false.

Key Terms
علة
وجوب
موجب بالذات
فاعل مختار
ناقص لذاته
مستكمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies God's power to reward.
Correction: It denies that reward is an obligation, not that God can reward. God remains all-powerful and free to reward or not.

Checkpoint
What is the first alternative in al-Razi's second proof, and what is its implication?
A) That it is possible for God not to reward, implying obligation is meaningless.
B) That it is impossible for God not to reward, which implies God is a necessitating cause, not free.
C) That the act itself is not a cause, so the argument is invalid.
D) That God is both free and obligated simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's second proof elegantly shows that obligating reward undermines both God's free agency and perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your prayers, fasting, or charity place God under an obligation to reward you? How does al-Razi's argument expose that assumption as not only false but as diminishing God's perfection and freedom? Will you repent from this subtle form of entitlement and worship God purely as a Giver, not a debtor?
?
When you sin, do you ever think that God is now 'obliged' to punish you? How does this proof help you realize that God's justice is not a mechanical cause-effect but flows from His will? How must your fear of God change—from fear of a system to awe of a Personal Will who is both just and free?
?
If you truly believe that God's reward is pure grace (fadl) and not obligation, why do you still feel disappointed when you don't 'feel' rewarded after a good deed? What does that reveal about your heart's deep-seated expectation that God 'owes' you something? How will you train yourself to expect nothing and thank Him for everything?

Razi's Fifth Argument: Mercy Preponderates
Razi cites and analyzes Quranic verses to prove mercy outweighs wrath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the wording of Quranic verses to argue that God's mercy outweighs His wrath.

Core Idea
The Quran's own phrasing reveals mercy as God's predominant attribute.

Imam Razi now turns to a subtle yet powerful argument from the Quran itself. He examines how God speaks about forgiveness versus punishment, showing that even the choice of words points to the preponderance of mercy. This lesson will walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text of verses 4:110-111 along with the translation. Notice that God promises forgiveness for those who seek it, but for sin He simply says it harms the sinner.
2
Analyze the key contrast
Razi highlights that God does not threaten punishment for sin in the same verse. Instead, He only says the sin is against the self. This asymmetry indicates mercy is more prominent.
Contrast with what God could have said but did not.
3
Examine the parallel from Surah Al-Isra
Razi brings a second verse where good deeds are mentioned twice and evil once, reinforcing the idea that God magnifies mercy while minimizing mention of punishment.
This pattern is intentional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْخَامِسُ: قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَمَنْ يَعْمَلْ سُوءًا أَوْ يَظْلِمْ نَفْسَهُ ثُمَّ يَسْتَغْفِرِ اللَّهَ يَجِدِ اللَّهَ غَفُورًا رَحِيمًا * وَمَنْ يَكْسِبْ إِثْمًا فَإِنَّمَا يَكْسِبُهُ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ وَكَانَ اللَّهُ عَلِيمًا حَكِيمًا﴾ [النساء: 110-111] وَالِاسْتِغْفَارُ طَلَبُ الْمَغْفِرَةِ، وَطَلَبُ الْمَغْفِرَةِ لَيْسَ نَفْسَ التَّوْبَةِ. وَخَرَجَ عَنْ هَذِهِ الْآيَةِ بِأَنَّهُ سَوَاءٌ تَابَ أَوْ لَمْ يَتُبْ فَإِذَا اسْتَغْفَرَ اللَّهَ غَفَرَ اللَّهُ لَهُ، وَلَمْ يَقُلْ فِي جَانِبِ الْمَعْصِيَةِ: "وَمَنْ يَكْسِبْ إِثْمًا فَإِنَّهُ يَجِدِ اللَّهَ مُعَذِّبًا مُعَاقِبًا"، بَلْ قَالَ: ﴿فَإِنَّمَا يَكْسِبُهُ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ﴾ [النساء: 111] فَدَلَّتْ هَذِهِ الْآيَاتُ عَلَى أَنَّ جَانِبَ الْحَسَنَةِ رَاجِحٌ عِنْدَ اللَّهِ، وَنَظِيرُهُ قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى: ﴿إِنْ أَحْسَنْتُمْ أَحْسَنْتُمْ لِأَنْفُسِكُمْ وَإِنْ أَسَأْتُمْ فَلَهَا﴾ [الإسرَاء: 7] وَلَمْ يَقُلْ: "وَإِنْ أَسَأْتُمْ فَلَهَا" فَكَأَنَّهُ تَعَالَى بَالَغَ فِي إِظْهَارِ أَفْعَالِهِ الْحَسَنَةِ، حَيْثُ ذَكَرَهَا مَرَّتَيْنِ وَسَتَرَ عَلَيْهِ إِسَاءَتَهُ حَيْثُ لَمْ يَذْكُرْهَا إِلَّا مَرَّةً وَاحِدَةً، وَكُلُّ ذَلِكَ يَدُلُّ عَلَى أَنَّ جَانِبَ الْحَسَنَاتِ رَاجِحٌ.
The fifth: His statement exalted: {And whoever does evil or wrongs himself then seeks forgiveness of Allah will find Allah Forgiving, Merciful. And whoever earns a sin only earns it against himself. And Allah is Knowing and Wise.} [4:110-111] And seeking forgiveness is asking for forgiveness, and asking for forgiveness is not itself repentance. And it came out of this verse that whether he repents or does not repent, if he seeks forgiveness of Allah, Allah forgives him, and He did not say on the side of disobedience: 'And whoever earns a sin indeed he will find Allah punishing and avenging,' but He said: {He only earns it against himself} [4:111]. So these verses indicate that the side of good deed is preponderant with Allah, and its parallel is His statement exalted: {If you do good, you do good for yourselves; and if you do evil, it is for it} [17:7] and He did not say: 'And if you do evil, it is for it.' So it is as if He exalted exaggerated in manifesting His good deeds, as He mentioned them twice, and concealed his evil deed as He mentioned it only once. And all of that indicates that the side of good deeds is preponderant.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) from the Quran for the preponderance of hope (wad) over fear (wa'id). The author argues that in verse 4:110-111, God promises forgiveness for those who seek it even without explicit repentance, and for sin He only says it harms the sinner, not that He will punish. This shows God's mercy outweighs wrath. He also cites 17:7 where good is mentioned twice and evil once, demonstrating God's emphasis on mercy.

Key Terms
استغفار
توبة
حسنة
سيئة
راجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that seeking forgiveness (istighfar) automatically guarantees forgiveness without any condition, or that it is the same as repentance.
Correction: Razi clarifies that istighfar is not repentance, but the verse still shows that God forgives upon seeking it, indicating mercy. The condition is implicit in the act of seeking.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, what does the Quranic wording in 4:110-111 reveal about God's attributes?
A) God punishes every sin without exception
B) God's mercy is more prominent than His wrath
C) God only forgives after formal repentance
D) God does not punish sins at all

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the Quran's own asymmetrical treatment of mercy and punishment to argue that mercy is God's preponderant attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you rush to istighfar, or do you delay because you feel God's wrath is more immediate? How does Razi's argument that mercy is preponderant challenge your hesitation?
?
The Quran repeats 'if you do good, you do good for yourselves' twice and mentions evil only once. Do you truly believe your good deeds benefit you more than your evil harms you? If so, how would that change your daily choices?
?
If God's mercy is truly preponderant, why do you still fear His punishment more than you hope for His forgiveness? What would it take for you to live with raja' (hope) outweighing khawf (fear)?

Repetition as Emphasis for Mercy
Razi notes the repeated mercy verse and contrasts it with unrepeated threat verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the repetition of a mercy verse in the Quran indicates that Allah emphasizes mercy over punishment.

Core Idea
Repetition in the Quran signals emphasis; mercy is repeated, threat is not.

Razi continues his arguments for the forgiveness of major sins. Here he points to a subtle linguistic feature: a verse about forgiveness is repeated in the same sura, while no verse of threat is ever repeated in the same way. This, he argues, shows that mercy is given greater emphasis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
2
Identify the key observation
Notice that the mercy verse (Quran 4:48) is repeated twice in the same sura. How does Razi use this fact?
The repetition is not accidental; it is deliberate.
3
Understand the principle
Razi states that repetition is only good for emphasis. Why would repetition be a tool for emphasis in Quranic rhetoric?
In Arabic rhetoric, repetition reinforces meaning.
4
Contrast with threat verses
Razi points out that no verse of threat is ever repeated in the same wording, neither in one sura nor in many suras. What does this contrast imply?
The absence of repetition for threat highlights the emphasis on mercy.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Razi concludes that Allah's care in promise (mercy) is more complete than His exaggeration in threat. How does this support the doctrine of forgiving major sins?
This argument prioritises divine mercy over punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السَّادِسُ: أَنَا سَنُبَيِّنُ إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى أَنَّ قَوْلَهُ: ﴿وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَنْ يَشَاءُ﴾ [النساء: 48] لَا يَدُلُّ إِلَّا عَلَى الْعَفْوِ عَنْ صَاحِبِ الْكَبِيرَةِ، ثُمَّ إِنَّهُ تَعَالَى أَعَادَ هَذِهِ الْآيَةَ فِي السُّورَةِ الْوَاحِدَةِ مَرَّتَيْنِ، وَالْإِعَادَةُ لَا تَحْسُنُ إِلَّا لِلتَّأْكِيدِ، وَلَمْ يَذْكُرْ شَيْئًا مِنْ آيَاتِ الْوَعِيدِ عَلَى وَجْهِ الْإِعَادَةِ بِلَفْظٍ وَاحِدٍ لَا فِي سُورَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ وَلَا فِي سُوَرٍ كَثِيرَةٍ، فَدَلَّ عَلَى أَنَّ عِنَايَةَ اللَّهِ فِي الْوَعْدِ أَتَمُّ مِنْ مُبَالَغَتِهِ فِي جَانِبِ الْوَعِيدِ.
The sixth: We will clarify, if Allah wills, that His statement: {And He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills} [4:48] only indicates forgiveness of the major sinner, then He exalted repeated this verse in the one sura twice, and repetition is only good for emphasis, and He did not mention any of the verses of threat on the face of repetition with one wording, neither in one sura nor in many suras. So it indicates that Allah's care in promise is more complete than His exaggeration in the side of threat.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This is another proof. The author points out that the verse about forgiving major sins (4:48) is repeated in the same sura (An-Nisa) twice. Repetition implies emphasis. However, no verse of threat is ever repeated in the same way. This shows God puts more emphasis on mercy.

تأكيد

وعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repetition in the Quran is redundant or meaningless.
Correction: Razi explains that repetition is a deliberate rhetorical device for emphasis, not redundancy.

Checkpoint
What does the repetition of the mercy verse (4:48) in the same sura indicate according to this argument?
A) That God loves repetition
B) That God emphasizes mercy more than threat
C) That the verse is ambiguous
D) That major sins are always forgiven

Key Takeaway
Repetition of the mercy verse and absence of repetition for threat shows that Allah's mercy outweighs His threat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If Allah repeats His promise of forgiveness twice in one surah, are you still hesitant to turn to Him in repentance? What sin or doubt holds you back from fully trusting that He will forgive you?
?
Knowing that Allah's care in promise surpasses His emphasis on threat, do you live with hope or fear? Which dominates your relationship with Allah, and what one step will you take today to align your heart with the greater weight of mercy?

Seventh Argument: Customary Preference for Mercy
Razi argues that interpreting threat verses leniently is preferable in custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses customary preference (istihsan) to argue for lenient interpretation of threat verses when they conflict with promise verses.

Core Idea
In custom, leniency on threats is good, but breaking promises is bad.

Faced with apparently conflicting general verses about divine promises and threats, Razi appeals to a principle rooted in human custom: it is praiseworthy to pardon a threat but blameworthy to break a promise. This argument builds on the idea that God, being the most generous, would naturally choose the nobler course of action. Let's examine how he constructs this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the concise logical flow: conflict → necessity of interpretation → preference for leniency on threats.
2
Identify the two sides and the conflict
Identify the two categories: general verses of promise and general verses of threat. They conflict, requiring interpretation of one side. Which side should be interpreted?
3
Understand the customary argument
Razi argues that pardoning a threat is customarily considered good, while neglecting a promise is customarily considered bad. Therefore, it is more appropriate to interpret the threat verses leniently. Reflect on why this analogy applies to God.
This argument assumes God acts in the most perfect manner, consistent with His generosity.
4
Check your understanding with the quiz
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the core reasoning. The answer is based solely on this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السَّابِعُ: أَنَّ عُمُومَاتِ الْوَعِيدِ وَالْوَعْدِ لَمَّا تَعَارَضَتْ فَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَأْوِيلِ أَحَدِ الْجَانِبَيْنِ، وَصَرْفُ التَّأْوِيلِ إِلَى جَانِبِ الْوَعِيدِ أَحْسَنُ مِنْ صَرْفِهِ إِلَى جَانِبِ الْوَعْدِ؛ وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّ الْعَفْوَ عَنِ الْوَعِيدِ مُسْتَحْسَنٌ فِي الْعُرْفِ، أَمَّا إِهْمَالُ الْوَعْدِ فَإِنَّهُ مُسْتَقْبَحٌ، فَكَانَ صَرْفُ التَّأْوِيلِ إِلَى جَانِبِ الْوَعِيدِ أَوْلَى مِنْ صَرْفِهِ إِلَى جَانِبِ الْوَعْدِ.
The seventh: Since the generalities of threat and promise conflict, there must be interpretation of one of the two sides. And turning interpretation to the side of threat is better than turning it to the side of promise; that is because pardoning the threat is customarily considered good, whereas neglecting the promise is customarily considered bad. So turning interpretation to the side of threat is more appropriate than turning it to the side of promise.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This is a rational argument. When general verses of promise and threat conflict, we must interpret some. It is better to interpret threat verses in a mitigated way (e.g., apply them only to disbelievers) rather than watering down promises, because in human custom, leniency is praiseworthy while withholding a promise is blameworthy. Since God is the most generous, He would act in the nobler way.

Key Terms
عمومات
تعارض
تأويل
عرف
استحسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all general verses must be taken literally without any interpretation, even when they conflict.
Correction: Razi argues that when general verses of promise and threat conflict, interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary. It is not that we discard literal meaning entirely, but we must reconcile them in a way that best befits God's perfection.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say interpreting threat verses (wa'id) is preferable to interpreting promise verses?
A) Because threat verses are stronger
B) Because custom finds pardoning a threat praiseworthy but breaking a promise blameworthy
C) Because promise verses are clearer
D) Because threat verses are more numerous

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that when general verses of promise and threat conflict, it is preferable to interpret threat verses leniently because custom deems pardoning threats praiseworthy and breaking promises blameworthy, and God acts in the most perfect manner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your dealings with others, which are you more likely to soften: your threats or your promises? Examine your heart: do you hold others to strict accountability while hoping for leniency for yourself? How does Razi's argument challenge you to reflect God's balance of mercy and justice in your own character? Will you commit this week to be more forgiving of others' faults and more faithful to your own promises?
?
Razi argues that God, being perfect, chooses the more praiseworthy course. Consider: when you are in a position of authority, do you lean toward mercy or severity? Are you more concerned with maintaining your reputation or with being compassionate? Pray on this: what would it look like for you to emulate God's attribute of mercy in a specific relationship or role?
?
The passage says 'pardoning the threat is customarily considered good.' Have you ever held a grudge or threatened someone in anger? In light of this lesson, how can you let go of that threat and extend pardon, even if it feels undeserved? Write one concrete step you will take today to practice forgiveness.

Eighth Argument: Quran's Abundant Mercy Descriptions
Razi lists Quranic names of mercy and contrasts with scarcity of severity verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the Quran's frequent mercy names and the structure of verse 40:3 to argue that mercy outweighs threat.

Core Idea
The Quran's emphasis on mercy over threat is embedded in its very wording.

Razi presents a cumulative textual argument: he catalogs the many Quranic names and descriptions that highlight God's forgiveness and mercy, and notes the lack of contrary descriptions. He then examines verse 40:3, where mercy and severity are juxtaposed, showing that mercy gets the final word.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing to analyze. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage is in two parts: a general catalog of mercy names and a specific verse analysis.
2
Identify the catalog of mercy names
Find the list of attributes like Ghafir, Ghafur, Rahim, etc. Notice how Razi emphasizes their abundance and the absence of attributes suggesting remoteness from pardon.
This sets up the claim that mercy dominates in the Quran.
3
Analyze the verse structure
Look at how Razi breaks down Quran 40:3. He points out that after mentioning 'Severe in punishment,' the verse ends with 'Possessor of bounty,' not another threat. This shows mercy is the final emphasis.
This is the key textual evidence for the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّامِنُ: أَنَّ الْقُرْآنَ مَمْلُوءٌ مِنْ كَوْنِهِ غَافِرًا وَغَفُورًا، وَأَنَّ لَهُ الْغُفْرَانَ وَالْمَغْفِرَةَ، وَأَنَّهُ رَحِيمٌ كَرِيمٌ، وَلَهُ الْعَفْوَ وَالْإِحْسَانَ، وَالْفَضْلَ وَالْإِفْضَالَ، وَالْأَخْبَارُ الدَّالَّةُ عَلَى هَذِهِ الْمَعَانِي قَدْ بَلَغَتْ مَبْلَغًا عَظِيمًا فِي الْكَثْرَةِ وَالْقُوَّةِ، وَلَيْسَ فِي الْقُرْآنِ مَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى أَنَّهُ بَعِيدٌ عَنِ الصَّفْحِ وَالتَّجَاوُزِ وَالْكَرَمِ، وَكُلُّ ذَلِكَ يُفِيدُ رُجْحَانَ جَانِبِ الْوَعْدِ عَلَى جَانِبِ الْوَعِيدِ. أَلَا تَرَى إِلَى قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: ﴿غَافِرِ الذَّنْبِ وَقَابِلِ التَّوْبِ شَدِيدِ الْعِقَابِ ذِي الطَّوْلِ﴾ [غَافِر: 3] فَقَوْلُهُ: "غَافِرِ الذَّنْبِ" إِنْ كَانَ الْمُرَادُ مِنْهُ أَنَّهُ يَغْفِرُ مَعَ التَّوْبَةِ صَارَ هَذَا عَيْنَ قَوْلِهِ: "قَابِلِ التَّوْبِ" فَيَحْصُلُ التَّكْرَارُ، ثُمَّ إِنَّهُ تَعَالَى لَمَّا ذَكَرَ عَقِيبَ هَذَيْنِ الْوَصْفَيْنِ مَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى الْوَعِيدِ، وَهُوَ قَوْلُهُ: "شَدِيدُ الْعِقَابِ" مَا ذَكَرَ عَقِيبَهُ مَرَّةً أُخْرَى مَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى الْعَفْوِ وَالصَّفْحِ فَقَالَ: "ذِي الطَّوْلِ" وَكُلُّ ذَلِكَ يَدُلُّ قَطْعًا عَلَى تَرْجِيحِ جَانِبِ الْوَعْدِ وَالْإِحْسَانِ.
The eighth: That the Quran is filled with His being Forgiving and Oft-Forgiving, and that He has forgiveness and pardon, and that He is Merciful, Generous, and He has pardon and beneficence, and grace and bounty. And the reports indicating these meanings have reached a great extent in abundance and strength, and there is nothing in the Quran that indicates He is far from pardoning, overlooking, and generosity. And all of that brings the preponderance of the side of promise over the side of threat. Do you not see His statement exalted: {Forgiver of sin, Acceptor of repentance, Severe in punishment, Possessor of bounty} [40:3]? So His statement 'Forgiver of sin' if the intention is that He forgives with repentance, then this becomes the same as His statement 'Acceptor of repentance', so repetition occurs. Then when He mentioned after these two attributes what indicates threat, which is His statement 'Severe in punishment', He did not mention after it again what indicates pardon and forgiveness, but He said 'Possessor of bounty'. And all of that decisively indicates the preponderance of the side of promise and beneficence.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This is a cumulative argument. The author catalogues the many Quranic descriptions of God's mercy (Ghafir, Ghafur, Rahim, Karim, Afw, Ihsan, Fadl, Idfal) and notes the lack of descriptions indicating remoteness from pardon. He then analyzes verse 40:3, where mercy attributes (forgiveness, acceptance) are paired with severity (severe punishment), but the sequence ends with 'bounty', not another threat. This shows mercy is the final emphasis.

Misconception: The presence of 'Severe in punishment' in verse 40:3 balances the mercy attributes equally.
Correction: Razi shows that the verse ends with 'Possessor of bounty' after severity, indicating that mercy is the final emphasis and outweighs threat.

Checkpoint
What does the author point out about the order of attributes in Quran 40:3?
A) It mentions mercy only
B) It mentions threat twice
C) After mentioning severity, it ends with a mercy attribute (bounty)
D) It has no repetition

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the Quran's overwhelming emphasis on mercy, and the specific wording of verse 40:3, decisively shows that God's mercy outweighs His wrath.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After seeing how the Quran itself emphasizes mercy over wrath, do you still allow fear of punishment to overwhelm your hope in God’s mercy? What one step will you take today to tilt your heart toward hope?
?
If you truly believed that God’s forgiveness is more abundant than your failures, how would you change the way you view your past mistakes? What sin have you been holding back from repenting of because you thought it was too big?
?
When you read verse 40:3 and see that God ends with 'Possessor of bounty' even after mentioning severe punishment, does that challenge your assumption about how God sees you? Will you let this verse shape your next sincere du'a for forgiveness?

Faith Outweighs Sin: Razi's Ninth Argument
Razi compares immense good of faith with lesser evil of sin in a servanthood analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's rational argument that the immense good of faith outweighs the lesser evil of sin, based on an analogy of a generous master and a servant.

Core Idea
The best act (faith) outweighs a lesser sin because God is infinitely generous.

Razi presents a compelling rational argument using a simple analogy: a master who favors a minor fault over extraordinary service would be considered stingy. How much more does this apply to Allah, the Most Generous? This lesson walks you through the argument step by step, showing why the believer's good deeds outweigh their sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation. Let the logic and analogy sink in before dissecting.
Notice how Razi contrasts extremes (best faith vs. not-worst sin).
2
Identify the two key premises
The argument has two main premises: (1) the believer performed the best act (faith), (2) he did not commit the worst sin (disbelief). Understand why these extremes matter.
The 'middle' refers to sins less than the ultimate evil.
3
Analyze the analogy
Razi uses a master-slave analogy: if a servant gives the greatest service but then commits a minor disobedience, a generous master would not penalize him heavily. This illustrates God's generosity.
Think about how human standards of generosity apply even more to Allah.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since Allah is the Most Generous, He will not let a minor sin outweigh tremendous faith. Therefore, mercy (promise) overcomes punishment (threat). Reflect on the implication for divine justice and mercy.
This argument supports the Ash'ari view that faith alone can lead to ultimate salvation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التَّاسِعُ: أَنَّ هَذَا الْعَاصِي أَتَى بِأَحْسَنِ الطَّاعَاتِ - وَهُوَ الْإِيمَانُ - وَلَمْ يَأْتِ بِأَقْبَحِ الْقَبَائِحِ - وَهُوَ الْكُفْرُ - فَأَتَى فِي طَبَقَةِ الْخَيْرَاتِ بِمَا هُوَ الْغَايَةُ الْقُصْوَى، وَأَتَى فِي طَبَقَةِ الشَّرِّ لَا بِمَا هُوَ الْغَايَةُ، بَلْ بِمَا هُوَ دُونَ الْوَسَطِ، وَالرَّجُلُ إِذَا كَانَ لَهُ عَبْدٌ ثُمَّ أَتَى عَبْدُهُ بِأَعْظَمِ أَنْوَاعِ الْخِدْمَةِ وَالطَّاعَةِ ثُمَّ أَتَى فِي بَابِ الْمَعَاصِي وَالذُّنُوبِ بِمَعْصِيَةٍ لَيْسَتْ فِي غَايَةِ الْقُبْحِ، بَلْ دُونَ الْوَسَطِ، فَلَوْ أَنَّ الْمَوْلَى رَجَّحَ تِلْكَ الْمَعْصِيَةَ الْخَفِيفَةَ عَلَى تِلْكَ الطَّاعَةِ الْعَظِيمَةِ لَعُدَّ ذَلِكَ السَّيِّدُ وَالْمَوْلَى لَئِيمًا مُؤْذِيًا، بَعِيدًا عَنِ الْكَرَمِ، وَمَعْلُومٌ أَنَّ هَذَا غَيْرُ لَائِقٍ بِكَرَمِ أَكْرَمِ الْأَكْرَمِينَ وَأَرْحَمِ الرَّاحِمِينَ، فَعَلِمْنَا أَنَّ الرُّجْحَانَ حَاصِلٌ لِجَانِبِ الْوَعْدِ.
The ninth: That this sinner has performed the best of acts of obedience - which is faith - and has not committed the ugliest of evil deeds - which is disbelief. So he has come in the category of good with what is the ultimate end, and he has come in the category of evil not with what is the ultimate, but with what is below the middle. And a man, if he has a slave and then his slave performs the greatest types of service and obedience, then commits in the category of sins and disobediences a sin that is not at the extreme of ugliness but below the middle, if the master were to prefer that light sin over that great obedience, that master would be considered base and harmful, far from generosity. And it is known that this is not befitting the generosity of the Most Generous of the generous and the Most Merciful of the merciful. So we know that the preponderance is for the side of promise.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This is a rational argument using analogy (qiyas) of a master and slave. A believer has the ultimate good (faith) and only a moderate sin (not kufr). A generous master would not disregard immense service for a minor fault; God, being infinitely generous, would not let sin outweigh faith. Thus, mercy prevails.

Key Terms
إيمان
كفر
طاعة
معصية
راجح
كرم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any sin completely cancels out a believer's good deeds, leaving the scales empty.
Correction: Razi argues that the immense good of faith is so great that a lesser sin cannot outweigh it, especially given God's generosity.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth argument, what makes the believer's case unique?
A) He has no sins at all
B) His faith is the best act and his sin is not the worst
C) His sin is equal to his faith
D) He has committed disbelief

Key Takeaway
Razi's ninth argument uses a rational analogy to show that for a believer, the immense good of faith necessarily outweighs a lesser sin because God is infinitely generous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine your own life: You claim faith, yet you commit sins. Do you truly believe that your act of faith is greater than your sins, or do you live with a guilty sense that your sins outweigh your belief? How does Razi's analogy of the generous master confront your inner doubt about God's forgiveness?
?
Imagine you are the slave in Razi's example. You have served your Master with the greatest service (faith) but have also committed a minor fault. How does your relationship with your Master look? Do you expect Him to be base and hold the fault against you, or do you trust His generosity? What does your expectation say about your understanding of God's mercy?
?
Razi argues it is unbefitting for the Most Generous to let a minor sin outweigh immense faith. When you sin, do you focus on the sin itself or on the immense gift of faith God has given you? Which perspective would change how you approach repentance and hope?

Yahya ibn Mu'adh's Argument on Tawhid Over Sin
Yahya ibn Mu'adh's argument on tawhid outweighing sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a moment of tawhid outweighs a lifetime of sin, and the logical implication for hope in God's mercy.

Core Idea
A moment of tawhid cancels a lifetime of sin — such is its power.

Imam Razi presents a tenth argument drawn from the spiritual insight of Yahya ibn Mu'adh. Using a striking comparison, Yahya argues that if sincere monotheism can erase years of disbelief, it can certainly erase a single sin. This argument appeals to both divine justice and hope, showing that faith is ultimately more powerful than disobedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation. Notice the parallel structure and the rhetorical question.
2
Analyze the analogy
Yahya compares the effect of tawhid on kufr (disbelief) to its effect on sin. If a short period of tawhid can destroy fifty years of kufr, it should logically destroy a single sin.
The analogy assumes that sin is lesser than kufr.
3
Examine the justice argument
The second part uses a principle of justice: if kufr nullifies all good deeds, then faith should nullify all evil deeds. If not, kufr would be more powerful than faith, which is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Even if one is not fully convinced by the justice argument, at least there remains hope (raja') for God's pardon. This encourages optimism in God's mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْعَاشِرُ: قَالَ يَحْيَى بْنُ مُعَاذٍ: إِلَهِي إِنْ كَانَ تَوْحِيدُ سَاعَةٍ يَهْدِمُ كُفْرَ خَمْسِينَ سَنَةً، كَيْفَ لَا يَهْدِمُ مَعْصِيَةَ سَاعَةٍ؟ إِلَهِي لَمَّا كَانَ الْكُفْرُ لَا يَنْفَعُ مَعَهُ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الطَّاعَاتِ، كَانَ مُقْتَضَى الْعَدْلِ أَنَّ الْإِيمَانَ لَا يَضُرُّ مَعَهُ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْمَعَاصِي، وَإِلَّا فَالْكُفْرُ أَعْظَمُ مِنَ الْإِيمَانِ، فَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ كَذَلِكَ فَلَا أَقَلَّ مِنْ رَجَاءِ الْعَفْوِ، وَهَذَا كَلَامٌ حَسَنٌ.
The tenth: Yahya ibn Mu'adh said: O my God, if one hour of tawhid destroys fifty years of disbelief, how can it not destroy one hour of sin? O my God, since disbelief does not benefit with it any of the acts of obedience, then the requirement of justice is that faith does not harm with it any of the sins. Otherwise, disbelief would be greater than faith. If that is not the case, then at the very least there is hope of pardon. And this is good speech.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
The author presents a devotional insight from Yahya ibn Mu'adh. If a moment of tawhid (monotheism) can erase long-term kufr, it surely can erase a minor sin. By justice, if kufr nullifies all good, then faith should nullify all evil; otherwise kufr would be more powerful than faith. At minimum, hope for pardon remains.

Key Terms
توحيد
يهدم
كفر
معصية
عدل
رجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a single sin can outweigh a lifetime of good deeds or that sin is more powerful than faith.
Correction: This argument shows that faith (tawhid) is more powerful than both kufr and sin; if it can destroy the greater evil (kufr), it can certainly destroy the lesser (sin).

Checkpoint
Yahya ibn Mu'adh argues that if tawhid can destroy kufr, it can destroy what?
A) All faith
B) A single act of worship
C) A single sin
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
If tawhid can erase a lifetime of disbelief, it certainly can erase a single sin, giving hope in God's mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that a single moment of sincere tawhid can erase a lifetime of sin? If yes, why do you still despair after sinning?
?
When you commit a sin, do you immediately think of the power of your faith, or do you let shame and guilt overshadow the hope that this lesson demands you have?
?
If you really internalized that your tawhid makes your sins harmless (in terms of eternal punishment), how would your relationship with God and your fear of Him change?
