Eleventh Argument: Interpreting the Forgiveness Verse (4:48)
Razi argues that 4:48 must refer to major sins before repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'He forgives what is less than that' (4:48) must refer to major sins before repentance, according to Razi's argument.

Core Idea
The verse must apply to major sins before repentance or it becomes meaningless.

Razi presents his eleventh argument for the thesis that Allah forgives major sins for believers without requiring repentance. He focuses on a key Qur'anic verse (4:48) and shows through logical elimination that it must refer to major sins before repentance. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the verse's scope
The verse says Allah forgives what is less than shirk. Begin by recognizing the general statement and its possible applications.
The verse is general, but not unlimited; it excludes shirk.
2
Eliminate alternative interpretations
Consider three possibilities: minor sins, major sins after repentance, and major sins before repentance. Razi argues the first two are invalid: minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins, and repented major sins are already forgiven. Therefore the verse must apply to major sins before repentance.
This is a process of elimination (talazum).
3
Assess the consequence of rejection
If we refuse to apply the verse to major sins before repentance, the verse becomes meaningless (ta'til). The alternative is to specify the general threat verses to apply only to disbelievers, which is specification (takhsis) – a lighter interpretive move.
Razi prefers specification over nullification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْحَادِي عَشَرَ: أَنَا سَنُبَيِّنُ إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى أَنَّ قَوْلَهُ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَنْ يَشَاءُ﴾ [النساء: 48] لَا يُمْكِنُ حَمْلُهُ عَلَى الصَّغِيرَةِ وَلَا الْكَبِيرَةِ بَعْدَ التَّوْبَةِ، فَلَوْ لَمْ نَحْمِلْهُ عَلَى الْكَبِيرَةِ قَبْلَ التَّوْبَةِ لَزِمَ تَعْطِيلُ الْآيَةِ، أَمَّا لَوْ خَصَّصْنَا عُمُومَاتِ آيَةِ الْوَعِيدِ بِالْكُفَّارِ، لَا يَلْزَمُ التَّعْطِيلُ، بَلْ يَلْزَمُ التَّخْصِيصُ، وَمَعْلُومٌ أَنَّ التَّخْصِيصَ أَهْوَنُ مِنَ التَّعْطِيلِ.
The eleventh: We will clarify, if Allah wills, that His statement exalted: {And He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills} [4:48] cannot be applied to the minor sin nor to the major sin after repentance. So if we do not apply it to the major sin before repentance, nullification of the verse becomes necessary. But if we specify the generalities of the verses of threat to the disbelievers, nullification does not become necessary, but rather specification. And it is known that specification is easier than nullification.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This is an interpretive argument. The verse 'He forgives what is less than that' (i.e., other than shirk) cannot refer to minor sins (because they are forgiven anyway) nor to major sins after repentance (since repentance already removes them). So it must refer to major sins before repentance. If we reject that, the verse becomes meaningless. The alternative is to apply threat verses only to disbelievers, which is a specification (takhsis) of the general text, which is easier than nullifying a verse.

Key Terms
صغيرة
كبيرة
توبة
تعطيل
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'He forgives what is less than that' includes all sins, including minor and repented sins.
Correction: Razi argues that minor sins are already forgiven by avoiding major sins, and repented major sins are forgiven by repentance, so the verse must refer to unrepented major sins.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the verse 4:48 cannot apply to minor sins?
A) Because minor sins are always punished
B) Because minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins
C) Because the verse is about major sins only
D) Because minor sins are not mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
The verse 'He forgives what is less than that' must refer to major sins before repentance, or else the verse would be empty of meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you delaying repentance for a major sin, thinking that Allah's forgiveness will cover it anyway? What concrete step will you take today to turn back to Him?
?
If you knew that a major sin you committed is not forgiven until you repent, what would you do differently this week?
?
How does your view of Allah's forgiveness change when you realize that it is tied to repentance and not automatic? What sin are you holding onto that you need to let go of right now?

God's Purpose in Creation is Mercy
Razi asserts that God's primary intent in creation is mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's twelfth argument that God's primary purpose in creation is mercy, not punishment, and how this shapes Islamic theology.

Core Idea
God's mercy is the original purpose in creation; punishment is secondary.

Razi concludes his theological arguments with a profound reflection on God's motivation for creation. He asserts that mercy, not punishment, is the ultimate divine purpose. This lesson examines the basis for this claim and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it, then the English translation. Let the argument's flow sink in before interpreting.
Notice the hadith quoted: 'My mercy preceded My wrath.'
2
Identify the assertion and evidence
Razi states explicitly that God's purpose is mercy. He supports this with two hadith. List them and see how they prove his point.
The opponent (those who emphasize punishment) would also agree that God is merciful.
3
Consider the implications
Reflect on the conclusion: if mercy is the original purpose, punishment is not intended for its own sake. How does this affect hope and fear in a believer?
This argument reinforces hope in God's mercy.

الثَّانِي عَشَرَ: أَنَّ مَقْصُودَ الرَّحِيمِ الْكَرِيمِ مِنَ الْإِيجَادِ وَالْخَلْقِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الرَّحْمَةُ، لَا الْعَذَابُ، وَالْخَصْمُ أَوْلَى النَّاسِ بِهَذَا الْقَوْلِ. وَالْأَخْبَارُ الْوَارِدَةُ فِي هَذَا الْمَعْنَى كَثِيرَةٌ مِنْهَا: قَوْلُهُ حِكَايَةً عَنِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى: "سَبَقَتْ رَحْمَتِي غَضَبِي" وَقَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى: "خَلَقْتُكُمْ لِتَرْبَحُوا عَلَيَّ لَا لِأَرْبَحَ عَلَيْكُمْ". وَإِذَا كَانَ كَذَلِكَ فَالْمَقْصُودُ الْأَصْلِيُّ هُوَ الرَّحْمَةُ وَالْإِحْسَانُ، وَالْعَذَابُ لَيْسَ بِمَقْصُودٍ الْبَتَّةَ.
The twelfth: That the purpose of the Merciful, the Generous in bringing into existence and creation is only mercy, not punishment. And the opponent is the most entitled of people to this statement. And the reports narrated on this meaning are many, including: His saying narrated from Allah exalted: 'My mercy preceded My wrath,' and His saying exalted: 'I created you that you may profit from Me, not that I may profit from you.' And if that is so, then the original purpose is mercy and beneficence, and punishment is not intended at all.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
The final argument is teleological: God's purpose in creating beings is to show mercy. Even the opponent (the one who argues for severity) would agree that God is merciful. The author cites two hadith qudsi: 'My mercy prevailed over My wrath' and 'I created you to gain from Me'. Thus, punishment is not God's primary intention; mercy is. Therefore, hope outweighs fear.

Misconception: One might think God's wrath is equal to mercy in intensity and purpose.
Correction: The hadith 'My mercy preceded My wrath' shows that mercy is primary and greater; punishment is not God's original intention.

Key Takeaway
God created the universe primarily to show mercy, not to punish – mercy precedes and outweighs wrath.

The Purpose of Creation: Mercy as the Primary Intent
Imam Razi argues that mercy is God's primary purpose in creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the purpose of creation to argue that God's promise of mercy outweighs the threat of punishment.

Core Idea
God's mercy is the original purpose; punishment is only secondary.

Imam Razi presents a proof based on the very purpose of creation. He argues that since God is Merciful and Generous, His primary intention in creating is to bestow mercy, not to punish. This is supported by prophetic traditions and a logical hierarchy that gives precedence to the original purpose over the subsidiary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text of the twelfth proof, then its literal translation. Notice the key terms like 'maqsud' (intent) and 'rahma' (mercy).
The passage is dense but clear in its argument.
2
Identify the core claim and evidence
The core claim is that God's intent in creation is mercy, not punishment. Identify the two hadith used as evidence: 'My mercy preceded My anger' and 'I created you to profit from you.'
3
Understand the logical inference
Since mercy is the original intent and punishment is secondary, giving preponderance to the promise (mercy) is giving precedence to the original over the subsidiary. Therefore, promise outweighs threat.
This logic hinges on the principle that the original has priority over the branch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني عشر : أن مقصود الرحيم الكريم من الإيجاد والخلق إنما هو الرحمة، لا العذاب، والخصم أولى الناس بهذا القول. والأخبار الواردة فى هذا المعنى كثيرة منها : قوله حكاية عن الله تعالى : سبقت رحمتي غضبي وقوله تعالى : " خلقتكم لتربحوا علي لا لأربح عليكم. وإذا كان كذلك فالمقصود الأصلي هو الرحمة و الإحسان والعذاب ليس بمقصود البتة، فترجح الوعد على الوعيد يقتضي ترجيح الأصل على التبع والفرع، وترجح الوعيد على الوعد يقتضي ترجيح التبع على الأصل، فكان الأول أولى.
The twelfth: That the intent of the Merciful, the Generous in bringing into existence and creation is only mercy, not punishment, and the opponent is the most entitled to this statement. And the reports transmitted on this meaning are many, among them: His statement, narrating from God the Exalted: 'My mercy preceded My anger' and His statement: 'I created you to profit from you, not for Me to profit from you.' And if that is so, then the original intent is mercy and beneficence, and punishment is not intended at all. So the preponderance of the promise over the threat necessitates preponderance of the original over the subsidiary and branch, and the preponderance of the threat over the promise necessitates preponderance of the subsidiary over the original. So the first is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth proof, which is based on the purpose of creation. The author states that the intended purpose (maqsud) of the Merciful and Generous God in creating is mercy (rahma), not punishment ('adhab). The opponents (the Mu'tazila) are actually the ones who should hold this view, as they emphasize God's justice. The author supports this with prophetic traditions (akhbar) such as the hadith qudsi 'My mercy preceded My anger' and another saying 'I created you to profit from you, not for Me to profit from you.' Since the original purpose is mercy and beneficence (ihsan), while punishment is only a secondary consequence, giving preponderance to the promise (wa'd) is giving precedence to the original over the subsidiary (taba') and branch (far'). Conversely, giving preponderance to the threat (wa'eed) would be elevating the subsidiary over the original, which is less appropriate. Therefore, the promise should outweigh the threat.

Key Terms

الرحمة
العذاب
سبقت رحمتي غضبي
الأصل
التبع
الفرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if mercy is the primary purpose, then God's justice is compromised or punishment is insignificant.
Correction: Imam Razi does not deny punishment; he only argues that mercy is the original intent, while punishment is a secondary consequence. Both exist, but mercy takes precedence in God's overall plan.

Checkpoint
According to the twelfth proof, what is the 'original intent' (al-maqsud al-asli) of God in creation?
A) Punishment
B) Testing
C) Mercy
D) Justice

Key Takeaway
The purpose of creation is mercy, so God's promise of mercy takes precedence over the threat of punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You claim to believe God's mercy precedes His anger, but do you live as though His anger is more imminent than His mercy? Identify one area where your actions reflect a fear of punishment rather than hope in His mercy, and commit to changing your perspective.
?
How would your reactions to your own sins and failures shift if you truly internalized that God created you to 'profit' from you in mercy? Write down one specific sin you regret, and ask: can you trust that God's original intent for you in that moment was mercy, not punishment?

Mercy as God's Essential Intention
Al-Razi dismisses the third Mu'tazilite argument by asserting that mercy is primary and punishment secondary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi refutes the Mu'tazilite argument that the need for deterrence gives priority to warnings, by distinguishing between essential and accidental divine intentions.

Core Idea
Mercy is God's primary intent; punishment is secondary.

In this passage, al-Razi addresses the third of three Mu'tazilite proofs that attempt to prioritize verses of threat over verses of promise. The Mu'tazilites argued that human corruption demands a strong deterrent, so warnings should take precedence. Al-Razi counters by clarifying the nature of divine intent: mercy is essential, punishment incidental.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Familiarize yourself with the key terms: tarjīḥ, raḥma, taʿdhīb, dhāt, ʿaraḍ.
Notice the concise structure: claim, reason, conclusion.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
Recall that the Mu'tazilites argued that because humans are prone to evil, the need for a deterrent (zājir) is greater, so warnings (waʿīd) should be prioritized.
This is the third tarjīḥ that al-Razi responds to.
3
Analyze al-Razi's distinction
Identify that al-Razi distinguishes between mercy (raḥma) as intended essentially (bi-al-dhāt) and punishment (taʿdhīb) as intended accidentally (bi-al-ʿaraḍ). Reflect on what that means for divine attributes.
Essential intention means it is the primary purpose; accidental means it is a means to an end.
4
Draw the conclusion
Conclude that because mercy is essential, it is more proper (awla) to give it precedence. Thus, verses of promise outweigh verses of threat according to al-Razi.
This supports the Sunni view that Allah's mercy prevails over His wrath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الترجيح الثالث: فضعيف لأن الرحمة مقصودة بالذات، والتعذيب مقصود بالعرض، وترجيح ما هو مقصود بالذات على ما هو مقصود بالتبع والعرض أولى من العكس.
As for the third tarjīḥ, it is weak because mercy is intended essentially (bi-al-dhāt), while punishment is intended accidentally (bi-al-ʿaraḍ). Giving preponderance to what is intended essentially over what is intended by way of subsequence and accident is more proper than the reverse.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the third Mu'tazilite argument, which claimed that since humans are naturally inclined to corruption and evil, the need for a deterrent (zājir) is greater, thus the side of threat (waʿīd) should be prioritized. Al-Razi dismisses this as weak. He explains that mercy (raḥma) is intended by God as a primary, essential purpose (maqṣūd bi-al-dhāt), whereas punishment (taʿdhīb) is only intended accidentally or incidentally (bi-al-ʿaraḍ) and as a subordinate means. Therefore, it is more appropriate to give precedence to that which is intended essentially over that which is intended by way of accident and subsequence. Hence, the verses of promise (waʿd) deserve priority over verses of threat.

عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that since humans are prone to evil, Allah's threat should be emphasized to deter them.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that mercy is Allah's essential attribute, so promises of mercy are primary, and threats are secondary tools.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi consider the third Mu'tazilite tarjīḥ weak?
A) Because deterrence is not needed.
B) Because mercy is intended essentially and punishment accidentally.
C) Because humans are not naturally corrupt.
D) Because the verses are equally balanced.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes the Mu'tazilite argument by showing that mercy is God's essential intention, so it should be prioritized over punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Razi argues that mercy is God's essential intention. Does your own heart treat God's mercy as primary, or do you sometimes act as if God's wrath is more immediate? What would change if you truly internalized that mercy is God's first intent?
?
Consider a time when you sinned. Did you despair of God's mercy? How does this lesson's statement that mercy is intended essentially (bi-al-dhāt) challenge your response to your own failures? Will you now repent with greater hope?

Scriptural Proof and Definition of Divine Forgiveness
Al-Razi cites verses and consensus, then defines 'afw.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses Quranic verses and consensus to establish God's attribute of pardon, and how he begins to define 'afw through a disjunctive analysis.

Core Idea
Pardon is not mere non-punishment; it requires a deserved punishment waived.

Al-Razi opens his discussion of divine forgiveness by grounding it in scripture and consensus. He then moves from textual evidence to conceptual analysis, defining 'afw as the remission of deserved punishment. This lesson traces his initial steps, including the rejection of an alternative definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Identify the three verses from Surah al-Shura and note how each emphasizes God's pardoning.
The verses are from 42:25, 30, and 34.
2
Recognize the role of consensus
Al-Razi adds ijma' as a second support. See how he uses communal agreement to reinforce the scriptural proof.
3
Understand the disjunctive definition
Al-Razi presents two possibilities for 'afw: remission from deserved punishment or from undeserved. He will reject the second.
This is a standard kalam method of exhaustive division.
4
Examine the first reason for rejection
The first reason: punishing the undeserving is ugly, and refraining from ugliness is not called pardon.
The passage ends before the second reason is given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في دلائلنا على العفو
الحجة الأولى: الآيات الدالة على كون الله تعالى عفوًا كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَقْبَلُ التَّوْبَةَ عَنْ عِبَادِهِ وَيَعْفُو عَنِ السَّيِّئَاتِ وَيَعْلَمُ مَا تَفْعَلُونَ﴾ [الشورى: ٢٥] وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أَصَابَكُم مِّن مُّصِيبَةٍ فَبِمَا كَسَبَتْ أَيْدِيكُمْ وَيَعْفُو عَن كَثِيرٍ﴾ [الشورى: ٣٠] وقوله: ﴿وَمِنْ آيَاتِهِ الْجَوَارِي فِي الْبَحْرِ كَالْأَعْلَامِ﴾ [الشورى: ٣٢] إلى قوله: ﴿أَوْ يُوبِقْهُنَّ بِمَا كَسَبُوا وَيَعْفُ عَن كَثِيرٍ﴾ [الشورى: ٣٤] وأيضًا أجمعت الأمة على أنه تعالى يعفو عن عباده وأجمعوا على أن من أسمائه العفو.
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: العفو إما أن يكون عبارة عن إسقاط العذاب عمن يحسن عقابه، أو عمن لا يحسن عقابه.
والقسم الثاني باطل لوجهين:
الحجة الأولى: أن عقاب من يقبح عقابه قبيح، ومن ترك مثل هذا القبيح لا يقال: إنه عفا، فإن الإنسان إذا لم يظلم أحدًا لا يقال إنه عفا عنه، أما إذا كان له أن
Chapter Four: On Our Proofs for Pardon
The first proof: The verses indicating that Allah is Pardoning, such as His statement: {And it is He who accepts repentance from His servants and pardons misdeeds, and He knows what you do} [al-Shūrā 42:25], and His statement: {And whatever disaster strikes you is for what your hands have earned, and He pardons much} [al-Shūrā 42:30], and His statement: {And among His signs are the ships in the sea like mountains} [al-Shūrā 42:32] up to His statement: {Or He destroys them for what they have earned while He pardons much} [al-Shūrā 42:34]. Also, the umma is unanimous that He, exalted, pardons His servants, and they are unanimous that among His names is al-ʿAfuww (the Pardoner).
If this is established, we say: al-ʿafw (pardon) is either an expression for the remission of punishment from one whose punishment is good (i.e., deserved), or from one whose punishment is not good (i.e., not deserved).
The second division is invalid for two reasons:
The first proof: Punishing one whose punishment is ugly (i.e., undeserved) is ugly, and one who refrains from such ugliness is not said to have pardoned. For if a person does not wrong anyone, it is not said that he pardoned him. But if he has the right to
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage initiates the fourth chapter of the work, providing the author's own proofs for God's pardon (ʿafw). Al-Razi first cites three Quranic verses from Sūrat al-Shūrā that affirm God's attribute of pardoning, along with the consensus of the Muslim community (umma) that God pardons His servants and that al-ʿAfuww is one of His names. After establishing this, he turns to a conceptual analysis of 'afw. He presents a disjunctive definition: pardon is either (1) the remission of punishment from one whose punishment is good (i.e., deserved, ḥasana) or (2) from one whose punishment is not good (i.e., undeserved, not ḥasana). He immediately rejects the second division as invalid, giving two reasons—only the first of which is fully preserved on this page. The first reason is that punishing someone who does not deserve it would be morally ugly (qabīḥ), and refraining from such ugliness is not called 'pardon'. For example, if a person never wrongs anyone, we do not say he 'pardons' them—pardon presupposes the right to punish. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the argument continues on subsequent pages.

عقاب
قبيح
حسن
إسقاط العذاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'afw simply means 'not punishing' regardless of desert.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that 'afw requires a context where punishment would be deserved; otherwise, abstaining from punishment is not called pardon.

Checkpoint
What is al-Razi's first reason for rejecting the second division of ʿafw?
A) Because punishing the undeserving is ugly, and refraining from that is not called pardon.
B) Because the verses only mention forgiveness for believers.
C) Because the consensus does not support that definition.
D) Because punishment is always good.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi establishes God's attribute of pardon through Quranic verses and consensus, then defines ʿafw as the remission of deserved punishment, rejecting any definition that does not presuppose a right to punish.

?
If you have wronged someone and they forgave you without acknowledging the wrong, does that feel like true pardon? How does that mirror or differ from your expectation of Allah's pardon?

Defining 'Afw: Pardon as Dropping Deserved Punishment
Razi defines 'afw and rejects undeserved punishment interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's definition of 'afw (pardon) as the dropping of deserved punishment, and why he rejects the interpretation that it applies to undeserved punishment.

Core Idea
Forgiveness only makes sense when punishment is deserved.

Razi begins his proof for divine forgiveness by establishing a precise definition. He argues that the term 'afw (pardon) linguistically and theologically applies only when the punisher has the right to punish. This lesson unpacks that definition and the two arguments he gives to eliminate the alternative view that 'afw could apply to cases where punishment is not even good.

Guided Path
1
Read the scriptural and consensus basis
Start by reading the verses and the statement of consensus that establish that God is 'Afuw. This sets the stage for the logical analysis that follows.
Notice that Razi uses both revelation (Qur'an) and reason (consensus).
2
Understand the definition of 'afw
Identify the two possible definitions: dropping punishment from one who deserves it, or from one who does not. Note that Razi explicitly states the second is invalid.
This is a standard kalam move: dividing a concept into exhaustive categories.
3
Follow the first reason against the undeserved interpretation
Razi gives a linguistic argument: we only say 'he pardoned' when the person had the right to punish. If no wrong was done, there is no 'pardon'.
He uses everyday language as a touchstone.
4
Follow the second reason from scriptural redundancy
The verse mentions both 'accepting repentance' and 'pardoning evil deeds'. If pardon meant dropping punishment from the repentant, it would be redundant. This proves the broader meaning.
Razi uses the Qur'an's own wording to refine the definition.
5
Synthesize the correct definition
Conclude that 'afw must mean dropping punishment from one whose punishment is good (deserved). This is the foundation for the rest of the proof.
This definition will be used in subsequent lessons on forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في دلائلنا على العفو
الحجة الأولى : الآيات الدالة على كون الله تعالى عفوًا كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ الَّذِي يقبل التَّوْبَةَ عَنْ عِبَادِهِ وَيَعْفُوا عَنِ السَّيِّئَاتِ وَيَعْلَمُ مَا تَفْعَلُونَ [الشورى: ٢٥] وقوله تعالى : ورما أصبَكُم مِّن مُّصِيبَةٍ فَبِمَا كَسَبَتْ أَيْدِيكُمْ وَيَعْفُوا عَن كَثِيرٍ [الشورى: ۳۰] وقوله : ووَمِنَ ايَاتِهِ الجَوَارِ في البَحْرِ كَالْأَعْلَيْهِ ﴾ [الشورى: ۳۲] إلى قوله : أَو يُوبِقَهُنَّ بما كسبوا وَيَعْفُ عَن كَثِيرٍ ﴾ [الشورى: ٣٤] وأيضًا أجمعت الأمة على أنه تعالى يعفو عن عباده وأجمعوا على أن من أسمائه العفو.
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : العفو إما أن يكون عبارة عن إسقاط العذاب عمن يحسن عقابه، أو عمن لا يحسن عقابه.
والقسم الثاني باطل لوجهين :
الحجة الأولى : أن عقاب من يقبح عقابه قبيح، ومن ترك مثل هذا القبيح لا يقال : إنه عفا، فإن الإنسان إذا لم يظلم أحداً لا يقال إنه عفا عنه، أما إذا كان له أن يعذبه فترك تعذيبه يقال : إنه عفا عنه، ولهذا قال تعالى: ﴿وَان تَعفُوا أَقْرَبُ لِلتَّقْوَى [البقرة : ٢٣٧]
الثاني: أنه تعالى قال: ﴿ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَقْبَلُ التَّوْبَةَ مَنْ عِبَادِهِ، وَيَعْفُوا عَنِ السَّيِّئَاتِ [الشورى: ٢٥] فلو كان العفو عبارة عن إسقاط العقاب عن التائب لكان في هذا تكرير من غير فائدة، فعلمنا أن العفو عبارة عن إسقاط العقاب عمن يحسن عقابه. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون العفو عبارة عن عدم إيصال العقاب إليه في الدنيا، وتأخيره إلى الآخرة ؟ قلنا : لأن الجزاءات مؤخرة إلى الآخرة، قال تعالى : اليَوْمَ تُجْزَى كل نَفْسٍ بِمَا كَسَبَتْ [غافر: ١٧] وإذا عرفنا أن الجزاءات مؤخرة إلى الآخرة لم يكن تأخيرها إلى الآخرة عفوا. وأيضًا لو كان هذا القدر مسمى باليعفو لوجب أن يقال : عفو الله عن الكفار أكثر من عفوه عن المسلمين؛ لأن حصول المرادات لهم ودفع المكاره عنهم - أعني الكفار - في الدنيا أكثر، قال: ﴿وَلَوْلَا أَن يَكُونَ النَّاسُ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً لَجَعَلْنَا لِمَن يَكْفُرُ بِالرَّحْمَنِ البيوتهم سُقُنًا مِّن فِضَّةٍ [الزّخرُف : ۳۳] وقال الخص البلاء بالأنبياء ثم الأولياء، ثم الأمثل فالأمثل.
The fourth chapter: On our proofs for forgiveness.
The first proof: The verses indicating that God Almighty is 'afuw (pardoning), such as His saying: 'And it is He who accepts repentance from His servants and pardons evil deeds and knows what you do' [Shura 42:25]; and His saying: 'And whatever disaster strikes you is for what your hands have earned, and He pardons much' [Shura 42:30]; and His saying: 'And of His signs are the ships in the sea like mountains' [Shura 42:32] until His saying: 'Or He destroys them for what they have earned, and He pardons much' [Shura 42:34]; and also the community (al-ummah) has agreed that He Almighty pardons His servants, and they agreed that among His names is 'al-'Afuw' (The Pardoner).
If this is established, we say: 'al-'afw' (pardon) is either an expression for dropping punishment from one whose punishment is good (i.e., deserved), or from one whose punishment is not good (i.e., not deserved).
The second division is invalid for two reasons:
The first reason: that punishing one whose punishment is ugly is ugly, and one who refrains from such ugliness is not said to have pardoned. For if a person does not wrong anyone, it is not said that he pardoned him. But if he has the right to punish him and refrains from punishing him, it is said that he pardoned him. For this reason, God Almighty said: 'And to pardon is nearer to piety' [Baqara 2:237].
The second: that He Almighty said: 'And it is He who accepts repentance from His servants and pardons evil deeds' [Shura 42:25]. If pardon (al-afw) were an expression for dropping punishment from the repentant, there would be in this a repetition without benefit. So we know that pardon is an expression for dropping punishment from one whose punishment is good (deserved). If it is said: Why is it not permissible that pardon be an expression for not inflicting punishment upon him in this world and postponing it to the Hereafter? We say: Because recompenses are postponed to the Hereafter. God Almighty said: 'Today every soul will be recompensed for what it has earned' [Ghafir 40:17]. And if we know that recompenses are postponed to the Hereafter, then their postponement to the Hereafter is not pardon. Also, if this amount were called 'afw, it would be necessary to say that God's pardon of the disbelievers is greater than His pardon of the Muslims, because the attainment of their desires and the removal of hardships from them—I mean the disbelievers—in this world is greater. He said: 'And if it were not that the people would become one community, We would have made for those who disbelieve in the Most Gracious roofs of silver for their houses' [Zukhruf 43:33]. And it was said: 'Affliction is sent upon the prophets, then upon the saints, then upon the best, then the best.'
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for God's forgiveness. Razi begins by quoting verses asserting that God is 'Afuw (Pardoner) and notes consensus among Muslims. He then defines 'afw as dropping punishment from one who deserves it, rejecting the alternative that it applies to one who does not deserve punishment. He gives two reasons for this rejection: (1) linguistically, 'forgiveness' is only used when the punisher has the right to punish; (2) if it meant dropping punishment from the repentant, then as the verse mentions both repentance and forgiveness, it would be redundant. An objection arises: maybe 'afw means postponing punishment to the afterlife? Razi answers that since all recompense is in the afterlife, postponement is not forgiveness; also, if it were, then God would be more forgiving to disbelievers because they enjoy more worldly benefits, which is absurd.

Key Terms
العفو
العقاب
التوبة
التكرير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'afw simply means 'not punishing' regardless of whether punishment was due.
Correction: Razi shows that 'afw is only meaningful when punishment was deserved; otherwise the term loses its force.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the possibility that 'afw applies to dropping punishment from one who does not deserve it?
A) Because the Qur'an says God punishes all sinners.
B) Because linguistically, 'pardon' is only used when the punisher has a right to punish.
C) Because the community agreed that 'afw applies only to believers.
D) Because it would mean God forgives without repentance.

Key Takeaway
Razi defines 'afw as dropping punishment from one who deserves it, not from one who does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your sins deserve punishment? If so, how does the reality of God's 'afw affect your daily attitude towards sin?
?
When you ask for forgiveness, do you realise you are asking for a reprieve from a just punishment? How does that change your sincerity?
?
Is there a sin you have normalized, thinking that God will just 'let it slide' without repentance? What does this lesson tell you about that assumption?

Refuting the Interpretation of 'Afw as Postponement
Razi refutes the postponement interpretation of 'afw.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the objection that 'afw means postponing punishment to the Hereafter rather than annulment.

Core Idea
Postponing punishment is not the same as pardoning it.

After establishing that 'afw means dropping deserved punishment, Razi addresses a possible objection: could 'afw simply mean postponing punishment to the afterlife? He gives two arguments to show this interpretation fails—first, all recompense is already in the afterlife, so postponement is meaningless; second, it would lead to absurd consequences like God being 'more forgiving' to disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
State the objection clearly
The objection: maybe 'afw is not dropping punishment but delaying it to the Hereafter. Understand this alternative before reading Razi's response.
This objection comes from someone trying to preserve the literal meaning of 'postponement'.
2
Examine the first refutation: recompense is already in the Hereafter
Razi quotes the verse that every soul will be recompensed on that day. Since punishment is already meant for the afterlife, postponement is not a special act of pardon.
This shows that 'postponement' is a tautology.
3
Examine the second refutation: absurd consequences
If 'afw meant mere postponement, then God would be said to 'pardon' the disbelievers more, since they enjoy more worldly blessings. This is conceptually absurd.
Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Understand the supporting ḥadīth
Razi ends with a well-known saying that affliction is sent upon the prophets then the saints. This supports the idea that worldly benefits are not signs of forgiveness.
The ḥadīth is not part of the objection but strengthens the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في دلائلنا على العفو
الحجة الأولى : الآيات الدالة على كون الله تعالى عفوًا كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ الَّذِي يقبل التَّوْبَةَ عَنْ عِبَادِهِ وَيَعْفُوا عَنِ السَّيِّئَاتِ وَيَعْلَمُ مَا تَفْعَلُونَ [الشورى: ٢٥] وقوله تعالى : ورما أصبَكُم مِّن مُّصِيبَةٍ فَبِمَا كَسَبَتْ أَيْدِيكُمْ وَيَعْفُوا عَن كَثِيرٍ [الشورى: ۳۰] وقوله : ووَمِنَ ايَاتِهِ الجَوَارِ في البَحْرِ كَالْأَعْلَيْهِ ﴾ [الشورى: ۳۲] إلى قوله : أَو يُوبِقَهُنَّ بما كسبوا وَيَعْفُ عَن كَثِيرٍ ﴾ [الشورى: ٣٤] وأيضًا أجمعت الأمة على أنه تعالى يعفو عن عباده وأجمعوا على أن من أسمائه العفو.
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : العفو إما أن يكون عبارة عن إسقاط العذاب عمن يحسن عقابه، أو عمن لا يحسن عقابه.
والقسم الثاني باطل لوجهين :
الحجة الأولى : أن عقاب من يقبح عقابه قبيح، ومن ترك مثل هذا القبيح لا يقال : إنه عفا، فإن الإنسان إذا لم يظلم أحداً لا يقال إنه عفا عنه، أما إذا كان له أن يعذبه فترك تعذيبه يقال : إنه عفا عنه، ولهذا قال تعالى: ﴿وَان تَعفُوا أَقْرَبُ لِلتَّقْوَى [البقرة : ٢٣٧]
الثاني: أنه تعالى قال: ﴿ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَقْبَلُ التَّوْبَةَ مَنْ عِبَادِهِ، وَيَعْفُوا عَنِ السَّيِّئَاتِ [الشورى: ٢٥] فلو كان العفو عبارة عن إسقاط العقاب عن التائب لكان في هذا تكرير من غير فائدة، فعلمنا أن العفو عبارة عن إسقاط العقاب عمن يحسن عقابه. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون العفو عبارة عن عدم إيصال العقاب إليه في الدنيا، وتأخيره إلى الآخرة ؟ قلنا : لأن الجزاءات مؤخرة إلى الآخرة، قال تعالى : اليَوْمَ تُجْزَى كل نَفْسٍ بِمَا كَسَبَتْ [غافر: ١٧] وإذا عرفنا أن الجزاءات مؤخرة إلى الآخرة لم يكن تأخيرها إلى الآخرة عفوا. وأيضًا لو كان هذا القدر مسمى باليعفو لوجب أن يقال : عفو الله عن الكفار أكثر من عفوه عن المسلمين؛ لأن حصول المرادات لهم ودفع المكاره عنهم - أعني الكفار - في الدنيا أكثر، قال: ﴿وَلَوْلَا أَن يَكُونَ النَّاسُ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً لَجَعَلْنَا لِمَن يَكْفُرُ بِالرَّحْمَنِ البيوتهم سُقُنًا مِّن فِضَّةٍ [الزّخرُف : ۳۳] وقال الخص البلاء بالأنبياء ثم الأولياء، ثم الأمثل فالأمثل.
The fourth chapter: On our proofs for forgiveness.
The first proof: The verses indicating that God Almighty is 'afuw (pardoning), such as His saying: 'And it is He who accepts repentance from His servants and pardons evil deeds and knows what you do' [Shura 42:25]; and His saying: 'And whatever disaster strikes you is for what your hands have earned, and He pardons much' [Shura 42:30]; and His saying: 'And of His signs are the ships in the sea like mountains' [Shura 42:32] until His saying: 'Or He destroys them for what they have earned, and He pardons much' [Shura 42:34]; and also the community (al-ummah) has agreed that He Almighty pardons His servants, and they agreed that among His names is 'al-'Afuw' (The Pardoner).
If this is established, we say: 'al-'afw' (pardon) is either an expression for dropping punishment from one whose punishment is good (i.e., deserved), or from one whose punishment is not good (i.e., not deserved).
The second division is invalid for two reasons:
The first reason: that punishing one whose punishment is ugly is ugly, and one who refrains from such ugliness is not said to have pardoned. For if a person does not wrong anyone, it is not said that he pardoned him. But if he has the right to punish him and refrains from punishing him, it is said that he pardoned him. For this reason, God Almighty said: 'And to pardon is nearer to piety' [Baqara 2:237].

And if we know that recompenses are postponed to the Hereafter, then their postponement to the Hereafter is not pardon. Also, if this amount were called 'afw, it would be necessary to say that God's pardon of the disbelievers is greater than His pardon of the Muslims, because the attainment of their desires and the removal of hardships from them—I mean the disbelievers—in this world is greater. He said: 'And if it were not that the people would become one community, We would have made for those who disbelieve in the Most Gracious roofs of silver for their houses' [Zukhruf 43:33]. And it was said: 'Affliction is sent upon the prophets, then upon the saints, then upon the best, then the best.'

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for God's forgiveness. Razi begins by quoting verses asserting that God is 'Afuw (Pardoner) and notes consensus among Muslims. He then defines 'afw as dropping punishment from one who deserves it, rejecting the alternative that it applies to one who does not deserve punishment. ?

?

How does this truth challenge your assumptions about who is 'blessed'? Will you now look for spiritual blessings as the true measure of God's mercy?
?

Proving God's Forgiveness from Verses on Maghfirah
Razi proves God's forgiveness from verses about maghfirah and its definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that maghfirah must mean dropping punishment from those who deserve it, based on the Quranic context of favor.

Core Idea
Forgiveness is a favor, so it must remove deserved punishment.

Razi presents his second proof for God's attribute of forgiveness by analyzing the very meaning of maghfirah as used in the Quran. He cites several verses that mention God as Ghafir, Ghafur, and Ghaffar, and then argues that forgiveness must be a genuine favor to the servant. This leads him to a precise definition that excludes the possibility that forgiveness could be merely refraining from punishing the innocent. In this lesson, we will follow Razi's logical reasoning and see how he defends the standard Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text of the passage carefully, then check the English translation to get a clear sense of the argument.
Pay attention to the Quranic citations and how Razi uses them to support his definition.
2
Identify the definition of maghfirah
Notice how Razi defines maghfirah by negation: it is not dropping punishment from the undeserving. Find the positive definition he offers.
He says: 'so it must be an expression for dropping punishment from one whose punishment is good (deserved).'
3
Understand the negative argument
Razi explains why the first division (applying to the undeserving) is invalid. Reflect on his reasoning that refraining from evil is not a favor but a benefit to oneself.
He uses the concept of 'imtinan' (bestowing favor) from the verses.
4
Apply the argument
Consider how this argument challenges a hypothetical opponent who claims that forgiveness could be simply not punishing the innocent. Why does Razi reject this?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الآيات الدالة على كونه تعالى غافرًا وغفورًا وغفارًا، قال تعالى: ﴿غَافِرِ الذَنْبِ ﴾ [غَافر : ٣] ﴿وَرَبُّكَ الْغَفُورُ ﴾ [الكهف: ٥٨] اسْتَغْفِرُوا رَبَّكُمْ إنه كَانَ غَفَّارًا [نوح: ١٠] غُفْرَانَكَ رَبَّنَا ﴾ [البَقَرَة : ٢٨٥] والمغفرة ليست عبارة عن إسقاط العقاب عمن لا يجوز عقابه فتعين أن تكون عبارة عن إسقاط العقاب عمن يحسن عقابه.
وإنما قلنا : إن القسم الأول باطل لأنه تعالى ذكر صفة المغفرة في معرض الامتنان على العباد، ولو حملناه على هذا الوجه لم يبق هذا المعنى؛ لأن ترك القبيح لا يكون إحسانًا، بل ترك القبيح بأن يكون إحسانا من الفاعل إلى نفسه أولى بأن يكون إحسانًا إلى غيره فإن على مذهب الخصم لو فعله لا يستحق الذم، فهو بترك ذلك القبيح أحسن إلى نفسه حيث خلصها عن استحقاق الذم.
The second proof: The verses indicating that He Almighty is Ghafir, Ghafur, and Ghaffar (Forgiver). God Almighty said: 'Forgiver of sin' [Ghafir 40:3]; 'And your Lord is the Forgiving' [Kahf 18:58]; 'Ask forgiveness of your Lord, indeed He is ever a Forgiver' [Nuh 71:10]; 'Your forgiveness, our Lord' [Baqara 2:285]. And maghfirah (forgiveness) is not an expression for dropping punishment from one whose punishment is not permissible, so it must be an expression for dropping punishment from one whose punishment is good (deserved).
We say: The first division (that it applies to one whose punishment is impermissible) is invalid, because He Almighty mentions the attribute of forgiveness in the context of bestowing favor upon the servants. If we interpreted it in that way, this meaning would not remain, because refraining from evil is not a favor. Rather, refraining from evil is a favor from the doer to himself more than it is a favor to another. For according to the opponent's view, if he does it, he does not deserve blame, so by refraining from that evil he is good to himself, since he saves himself from deserving blame.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for God's forgiveness, based on verses using the names Ghafir, Ghafur, Ghaffar. Razi argues that maghfirah must mean dropping punishment from one who deserves it, since it cannot mean dropping it from one who does not deserve it (since that would not be forgiveness). He then refutes the opposing view: if it meant dropping punishment from one who does not deserve it, then it would be merely refraining from evil, which is not a favor to the recipient but to oneself. But the verses present forgiveness as a favor to the servants, so it must apply to those who deserve punishment.

Key Terms
المغفرة
غافر
غفور
غفار
الامتنان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Forgiveness could simply mean refraining from punishing someone who does not deserve punishment.
Correction: Razi shows that this would not be a favor to the servant, contradicting the Quranic presentation of forgiveness as grace.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that maghfirah cannot be dropping punishment from one whose punishment is impermissible?
A) Because such a person does not exist.
B) Because it would make forgiveness a mere refraining from evil, which is not a favor to the servant.
C) Because God does not forgive those who do not deserve punishment.
D) Because the verses apply only to believers.

Key Takeaway
Forgiveness (maghfirah) is a favor granted by God to those who deserve punishment, not merely refraining from punishing the innocent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe you deserve punishment for your sins, or do you assume God's forgiveness is automatic? How would your repentance change if you felt the weight of deserving punishment?
?
When you ask Allah for maghfirah, do you see it as a favor He grants despite your deserving punishment, or do you feel entitled to it? What does that reveal about your view of His mercy?
?
If forgiveness only applies to the deserving, have you ever trivialized it by thinking God 'must' forgive you? What would it mean to seek forgiveness with the humility of someone who knows they deserve the opposite?

Objection: Could 'Afw and Maghfirah Mean Postponing Punishment?
Objection and beginning of refutation regarding 'afw and maghfirah as postponement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an objection to the definition of divine forgiveness and why interpreting it as mere postponement is problematic.

Core Idea
Forgiveness is not delaying punishment—it is removing it entirely.

In this passage, an objector proposes that 'afw and maghfirah might mean postponing punishment from this world to the afterlife, citing a verse about the Jews. Imam Razi begins to refute this, but the text is cut off. This lesson examines the objection and its implications for understanding divine forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: 'If it is said...' introduces the objection.
The objection challenges the prior definition that forgiveness means dropping punishment.
2
Identify the evidence
The objector cites verse 2:52 about the Jews, claiming 'afw there means postponement. Note how the verse is interpreted.
The objector argues that 'afw can mean delay, not just cancellation.
3
Recognize the incomplete answer
The passage breaks off mid-verse. Understand that the full refutation would rely on earlier arguments showing forgiveness is complete removal.
Even without the full answer, the objection is weak because it contradicts the essential meaning of forgiveness.

فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز حمل العفو والمغفرة على تأخير العقاب عن الدنيا إلى الآخرة ؟ والدليل على أن لفظ العفو مستعمل في هذا المعنى قوله تعالى في قصة اليهود ثُمَّ عَفَوْنَا عَنكُم مِّنْ بَعْدِ ذَلِكَ ﴾ [البَقَرَة : ٥٢] وليس المراد إسقاط العذاب، بل تأخيره إلى الآخرة وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أَصَبَكُم مِّن مُّصِيبَةٍ فَبِمَا كَسَبَتْ
If it is said: Why is it not permissible to interpret 'afw and maghfirah as the postponement of punishment from this world to the Hereafter? The evidence that the word 'afw is used in this meaning is the saying of God Almighty in the story of the Jews: 'Then We pardoned you after that' [Baqara 2:52], and what is meant is not the removal of punishment, but its postponement to the Hereafter. Similarly, His saying: 'And whatever disaster strikes you is for what your hands have earned'
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the earlier definition: why can 'afw and maghfirah not be interpreted as delaying punishment from this world to the afterlife? The objector cites the verse about the Jews (2:52) where 'afw is interpreted by some as postponement. The answer begins but is cut off. The objection itself is refuted by the earlier arguments, and the incomplete answer would likely continue to show the absurdity of interpreting forgiveness as mere postponement.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'afw and maghfirah can mean simply delaying punishment to the afterlife.
Correction: True forgiveness removes punishment entirely; postponement is not forgiveness, as it leaves the punishment still pending.

The Meaning of 'Afw: Dropping Deserved Punishment
Razi argues that 'afw must drop deserved punishment, using Qur'an and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'afw (divine pardon) must mean the cancellation of deserved punishment, not merely postponement or forgiveness of the penitent.

Core Idea
'Afw cancels deserved punishment, not just delays it.

This lesson examines the second argument from Imam Razi's Al-Arba'in on the definition of divine pardon. The author uses a key Qur'anic verse to show that 'afw cannot be identical to accepting repentance, then refutes the possibility that it means simply postponing punishment. By the end, you'll see why 'afw is specifically the dropping of deserved punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author builds his argument.
The passage uses a verse from Surah ash-Shura as a starting point.
2
Identify the argument and the objection
Find where the author states his argument (if 'afw meant dropping from the penitent, it would be redundant). Then find the objection that 'afw could mean postponement. Understand how these two elements structure the passage.
3
Examine the refutation
The author gives two responses to the objection: first, that postponement is not distinct from the general postponement of recompense; second, that it would lead to the absurd conclusion that disbelievers receive more 'afw. Reflect on the logic.
Notice the use of additional Qur'anic quotes to support the second response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه تعالى قال: ﴿ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَقْبَلُ التَّوْبَةَ مَنْ عِبَادِهِ، وَيَعْفُوا عَنِ السَّيِّئَاتِ [الشورى: ٢٥] فلو كان العفو عبارة عن إسقاط العقاب عن التائب لكان في هذا تكرير من غير فائدة، فعلمنا أن العفو عبارة عن إسقاط العقاب عمن يحسن عقابه. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون العفو عبارة عن عدم إيصال العقاب إليه في الدنيا، وتأخيره إلى الآخرة ؟ قلنا : لأن الجزاءات مؤخرة إلى الآخرة، قال تعالى : اليَوْمَ تُجْزَى كل نَفْسٍ بِمَا كَسَبَتْ [غافر: ١٧] وإذا عرفنا أن الجزاءات مؤخرة إلى الآخرة لم يكن تأخيرها إلى الآخرة عفوا. وأيضًا لو كان هذا القدر مسمى باليعفو لوجب أن يقال : عفو الله عن الكفار أكثر من عفوه عن المسلمين؛ لأن حصول المرادات لهم ودفع المكاره عنهم - أعني الكفار - في الدنيا أكثر، قال: ﴿وَلَوْلَا أَن يَكُونَ النَّاسُ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً لَجَعَلْنَا لِمَن يَكْفُرُ بِالرَّحْمَنِ لِبُيُوتِهِمْ سُقُفًا مِّن فِضَّةٍ [الزّخرُف : ۳۳] وقال: خَصَّ البلاء بالأنبياء ثم الأولياء، ثم الأمثل فالأمثل.
The second: He, exalted, said: 'And He is the One who accepts repentance from His servants and pardons evil deeds' (ash-Shura 25). If pardon were the dropping of punishment from the penitent, this would be a repetition without benefit. So we know that pardon is the dropping of punishment from one whose punishment is good (i.e., deserved). If it is said: Why is it not permissible that pardon be the non-infliction of punishment in this world and its postponement to the hereafter? We say: Because recompenses are postponed to the hereafter. He said, exalted: 'Today every soul is recompensed for what it earned' (Ghafir 17). And if we know that recompenses are postponed to the hereafter, their postponement to the hereafter would not be pardon. Also, if this extent were called pardon, it would be necessary to say: God's pardon of the disbelievers is greater than His pardon of the Muslims, because the attainment of their desires and the repulsion of harms from them—I mean the disbelievers—in this world is greater. He said: 'And were it not that mankind would become one community, We would have made for those who disbelieve in the Beneficent, for their houses, ceilings of silver' (az-Zukhruf 33). And He said: 'He has specifically [appointed] trial to the prophets, then the saints, then the more excellent, then the more excellent.'
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a second argument (الثاني) presenting a proof for the definition of 'afw (divine pardon). The author argues: If 'afw meant dropping punishment from the penitent, then the verse 'accepts repentance and pardons evil deeds' would be redundant—pardoning the penitent is already included in accepting repentance. Therefore 'afw must mean dropping punishment from one who deserves it (i.e., a sinner who has not repented or whose punishment is otherwise deserved). An objection is raised: Could 'afw mean merely postponing punishment to the hereafter? The author responds: Since all recompense is postponed to the hereafter, postponement is not forgiveness; also, if it were, disbelievers would receive more 'forgiveness' because they enjoy more worldly blessings. The author then quotes a saying that trials are specifically severe for prophets and the best, indicating that worldly ease is not a sign of forgiveness.

التائب
العقاب المستحق

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'afw means merely delaying punishment to the afterlife.
Correction: The author shows that since all recompense is already delayed, this would make 'afw redundant; also it would imply disbelievers receive more 'afw, which is false.

Checkpoint

A) Because the verse would then be redundant
B) Because the penitent does not deserve punishment
C) Because 'afw only applies to disbelievers
D) Because dropping punishment is always obligatory

Key Takeaway
'Afw means God drops deserved punishment, not just postpones it or forgives the penitent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage shows that worldly blessings do not indicate divine favor. Are you more concerned with comfort and prosperity in this world than with seeking the true 'afw that cancels punishment in the next? How will you reorient your priorities starting now?

Second Proof: Maghfirah as Dropping Deserved Punishment
The author presents the second proof and refutes the postponement interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that divine forgiveness (maghfirah) must mean the dropping of deserved punishment, and why it cannot be mere postponement.

Core Idea
Divine forgiveness is grace, not duty—it cancels deserved punishment, not merely delays it.

Imam al-Razi presents a second linguistic and rational argument that forgiveness is a gratuitous act, not an obligation. He first establishes that the Qur'anic contexts of forgiveness imply favor, then refutes the alternative interpretation of postponement. This lesson walks through his proof and his response to the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: proof, objection, answer.
The passage is dense; take your time with each part.
2
Identify the key claim
Al-Razi argues that maghfirah must refer to dropping punishment from those who deserve it. Locate the logical step where he excludes the alternative.
He uses the concept of imtinan (showing favor) to support this.
3
Examine the objection
The opponent suggests that 'afw and maghfirah could mean postponement to the afterlife. Find the three Qur'anic examples they cite.
These examples are about not punishing immediately in this world.
4
Analyze the answer
Al-Razi responds by examining the root meaning of 'afw (removal) and provides evidence from the Qur'an and common usage.
Pay attention to the creditor analogy.
5
Reflect on implications
Consider why this distinction matters: forgiveness as cancellation rather than delay highlights God's grace.
This connects to broader themes in divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الآيات الدالة على كونه تعالى غافرًا وغفورًا وغفارًا، قال تعالى: ﴿غَافِرِ الذَنْبِ ﴾ [غَافر : ٣] ﴿وَرَبُّكَ الْغَفُورُ ﴾ [الكهف: ٥٨] اسْتَغْفِرُوا رَبَّكُمْ إنه كَانَ غَفَّارًا [نوح: ١٠] غُفْرَانَكَ رَبَّنَا ﴾ [البَقَرَة : ٢٨٥] والمغفرة ليست عبارة عن إسقاط العقاب عمن لا يجوز عقابه فتعين أن تكون عبارة عن إسقاط العقاب عمن يحسن عقابه. وإنما قلنا : إن القسم الأول باطل لأنه تعالى ذكر صفة المغفرة في معرض الامتنان على العباد، ولو حملناه على هذا الوجه لم يبق هذا المعنى؛ لأن ترك القبيح لا يكون إحسانًا، بل ترك القبيح بأن يكون إحسانا من الفاعل إلى نفسه أولى بأن يكون إحسانًا إلى غيره فإن على مذهب الخصم لو فعله لا يستحق الذم، فهو بترك ذلك القبيح أحسن إلى نفسه حيث خلصها عن استحقاق الذم. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز حمل العفو والمغفرة على تأخير العقاب عن الدنيا إلى الآخرة ؟ والدليل على أن لفظ العفو مستعمل في هذا المعنى قوله تعالى في قصة اليهود ثُمَّ عَفَوْنَا عَنكُم مِّنْ بَعْدِ ذَلِكَ ﴾ [البَقَرَة : ٥٢] وليس المراد إسقاط العذاب، بل تأخيره إلى الآخرة وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أَصَبَكُم مِّن مُّصِيبَةٍ فَبِمَا كَسَبَتْ أَيْدِيكُمْ وَيَعْفُوا عَن كَثِيرٍ ﴾ [الشورى: ۳۰] أي أنه تعالى قد يجعل المصائب إما على جهة الابتلاء أو على جهة العقوبة المعجلة بسبب ذنوبكم، ثم إنه تعالى لا يعجل العقوبة والابتلاء على كثير منها، وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمِنْ آيَاتِهِ الْجَوَارِ فِي الْبَحْرِ الْأَعْلَامِ إِن يَشَا يُسَكِينِ الرِّيحَ فَيَظْلَلْنَ رَوَاكِدَ عَلَى ظَهْرِ: إِنَّ فِي ذَلِك لايت لكل صبارٍ شَكُورٍ أَوْ يُوبِقَهُنَّ بِمَا كَسَبُوا وَيَعْفُ عَن كَثِيرٍ ) [الشورى: ٣٢ - ٣٤] أي أنه تعالى لو شاء إهلاكهم لأهلكهم إلا أنه تعالى لا يهلكهم على كثير من الذنوب. والجواب : العفو أصله من عفا أثره أي أزاله، وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن يكون مسمى العفو هو الإزالة، ولهذا قال تعال: فَمَنْ عُفِيَ لَهُ مِنْ أَخِيهِ شَيْءٌ [البَقَرَة : ۱۷۸] وليس المراد منه التأخير إلى وقت معلوم، بل الإزالة، وكذا قوله تعالى: ﴿وَان تَعفُوا أقرب لِلتَّقْوَى [البَقَرَة : ۲۳۷] ليس المراد منه التأخير إلى وقت معلوم، بل المراد منه هو الإسقاط المطلق. وأيضًا: الغريم إذا أخر المطالبة ، لا يقال : إنه عفا عنه ولو أسقطه يقال : إنه عفا عنه، فثبت أن العفو لا يمكنم تفسيره بالتأخير.
The second proof: The verses indicating that He, exalted, is Forgiver, Forgiving, and Oft-Forgiving. He said, exalted: 'Forgiver of sin' (Ghafir 3), 'And your Lord is the Forgiving' (al-Kahf 58), 'Ask forgiveness of your Lord; indeed, He is Oft-Forgiving' (Nuh 10), 'Your forgiveness, our Lord' (al-Baqarah 285). And forgiveness is not the dropping of punishment from one whose punishment is not permissible (i.e., does not deserve punishment), so it is determined that it is the dropping of punishment from one whose punishment is good (i.e., deserved). And we say: The first division is invalid because He, exalted, mentioned the attribute of forgiveness in the context of showing favor to the servants; if we interpret it in this way, this meaning would not remain, because refraining from what is evil is not a favor. Rather, refraining from evil by the agent is more appropriate as a favor to himself than as a favor to another, for according to the opponent's doctrine, if he does it, he does not deserve blame, so by refraining from that evil, he has been good to himself, freeing himself from deserving blame. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to interpret pardon and forgiveness as postponing punishment from this world to the hereafter? The evidence that the term 'afw is used in this sense is His saying in the story of the Jews: 'Then We pardoned you after that' (al-Baqarah 52); the intended meaning is not the dropping of punishment, but its postponement to the hereafter. Likewise His saying: 'And whatever misfortune befalls you is due to what your hands have earned, and He pardons much' (ash-Shura 30), meaning that He, exalted, may make misfortunes either as a trial or as an immediate punishment due to your sins, then He does not hasten punishment and trial regarding much of it. Likewise His saying: 'And of His signs are the ships in the sea like mountains. If He wills, He calms the wind, so they become motionless on its surface. Indeed in that are signs for every patient, grateful person. Or He destroys them because of what they earned and He pardons much' (ash-Shura 32-34), meaning that if He willed their destruction, He would destroy them, but He does not destroy them for many sins. And the answer: The origin of 'afw is 'afa atharahu', i.e., he removed it. When this is the case, the meaning of 'afw must be removal. Hence He said: 'But whoever is pardoned something by his brother' (al-Baqarah 178); the intended meaning is not postponement to an appointed time, but removal. Likewise His saying: 'And if you pardon, it is nearer to piety' (al-Baqarah 237); the intended meaning is not postponement to an appointed time, but rather absolute dropping. Also, if a creditor postpones the demand, it is not said that he has pardoned him; but if he drops it, it is said that he has pardoned him. So it is established that 'afw cannot be interpreted as postponement.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (الحجة الثانية) for the meaning of divine forgiveness. The author cites verses describing God as Ghafir, Ghafur, Ghaffar. He argues: maghfirah cannot mean dropping punishment from one who does not deserve it (like the obedient), because that would be a duty, not grace. Hence it must mean dropping punishment from one who deserves it. He then refutes a potential objection: Could maghfirah mean postponement to the hereafter? The objector cites verses where 'afw is used for not punishing immediately in this world. The author responds by examining the root meaning of 'afw: it means removal (izalah), not postponement. He supports this with the Qur'anic use in 2:178 and 2:237, and with a linguistic analogy: a creditor who postpones is not said to have pardoned, but one who drops the debt is. Thus, 'afw cannot be interpreted as mere postponement; it is cancellation of deserved punishment.

Key Terms
المغفرة
العفو
إسقاط العقاب
التأخير
الإزالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'afw and maghfirah can mean merely postponing punishment rather than cancelling it.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that the root meaning of 'afw is removal, not delay, and this is supported by Qur'anic usage and common language (e.g., creditor pardoning debt).

Checkpoint
How does the author argue that maghfirah must apply to those who deserve punishment?
A) Because God's forgiveness is mentioned as a favor, and dropping punishment from the undeserving is not a favor
B) Because the verses only mention sinners
C) Because the term 'maghfirah' always implies punishment
D) Because the obedient do not need forgiveness

Key Takeaway
Divine forgiveness (maghfirah/'afw) means the complete cancellation of deserved punishment, not mere postponement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Third Proof: Divine Mercy Must Be Shown to Sinners
Razi divides divine mercy into cases and shows it must be for the disobedient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that divine mercy, in its essential form, must be directed toward those who deserve punishment, not toward the obedient who receive their due reward.

Core Idea
Divine mercy is not about rewarding the deserving, but about sparing the undeserving from punishment.

Razi continues his series of proofs for divine mercy by examining the meaning of the term 'mercy' (rahmah). He argues that if God is truly Rahman and Rahim, mercy cannot simply be giving people what they deserve. Rather, true mercy involves forgiving or holding back punishment from those who deserve it. In this passage, Razi systematically eliminates alternative interpretations to arrive at this conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two main divisions
The passage splits mercy into two categories: mercy to the obedient and mercy to the disobedient. Notice how Razi argues the first category fails to qualify as mercy.
2
Understand the reasoning for the first division
Razi argues that giving the obedient their due reward is an obligation, not mercy. And giving extra reward is an increase in beneficence, not mercy. This eliminates the idea that mercy is for the obedient.
3
Examine the second division as far as provided
Razi then turns to mercy for the disobedient, distinguishing between leaving punishment that is not deserved and leaving punishment that is deserved. The argument aims to show that true mercy is forgiveness of deserved punishment.
The text cuts off mid-argument, but the direction is clear.
4
Reflect on the implications for understanding divine mercy
Consider how this reorients our understanding: mercy is primarily about forgiveness, not reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : الآيات الدالة على كونه تعالى رحماناً رحيماً، والاستدلال أن رحمته إما أن تظهر بالنسبة إلى المطيعين الذين هم أهل الثواب، أو بالنسبة إلى العصاة الذين هم أهل العقاب، والأول باطل ؛ لأن رحمته إما أن تكون عبارة عن أنه أعطاهم الثواب الذي هو حقهم، أو عبارة عن أنه يفضل عليهم بما هو أزيد من حقهم، والأول باطل؛ لأن أداء الواجب لا يسمى رحمة، لأن من حقه قهرًا لا يقال في مثل هذا الإعطاء إنه رحمة. والثاني باطل : لأن المكلف صار بما أخذ من الثواب الذي هو كالمستغني، وتلك الزيادة تسمى زيادة في الإنعام ولا تسمى - البتة - رحمة، ألا ترى أن السلطان إذا أعطى الوزير مملكة ثم ضم إليها مملكة أخرى لا يقال لهذه الزيادة: إنها رحمة، بل يقال : زاد في الإنعام. وأما القسم الثاني - وهو أن رحمته ظهرت بالنسبة إلى أهل العقاب - فنقول : هذه الرحمة إما أن تكون عبارة عن ترك العقاب - الذي هو غير مستحق - أو عن ترك العقاب - الذي هو مستحق. والأول باطل وإلا لكان كل كافر وظالم رحيما لأجل أنه ترك الظلم، أو
The third proof: The verses indicating that He, exalted, is Most Gracious, Most Merciful. The inference is that His mercy either appears with respect to the obedient who are people of reward, or with respect to the disobedient who are people of punishment. The first is invalid because His mercy is either that He gave them the reward which is their right, or that He favors them with more than their right. The first is invalid: because fulfilling an obligation is not called mercy, because [if] from his right [he takes] by force, it is not said in such giving that it is mercy. The second is invalid: because the responsible [servant] [acquires] from the reward that which is like [making him] independent, and that increase is called increase in beneficence and is not called—at all—mercy. Do you not see that if the sultan gives the minister a kingdom and then annexes another kingdom to it, this increase is not said to be mercy, rather it is said: he increased in beneficence. As for the second division—that His mercy appears with respect to the people of punishment—we say: This mercy is either the leaving of punishment which is not deserved, or the leaving of punishment which is deserved. The first is invalid, otherwise every disbeliever and tyrant would be merciful because he refrains from injustice, or...
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is the third proof (الحجة الثالثة) based on verses describing God as Rahman and Rahim. The author divides mercy into two categories: mercy to the obedient and mercy to the disobedient. He argues the first is invalid: mercy to the obedient cannot be giving them their due reward (that is an obligation, not mercy), nor can it be giving extra reward (that is an increase in beneficence, not mercy per se). Therefore, mercy must be directed to the disobedient (ahl al-'iqab) who deserve punishment. Then, within the second category, mercy is either leaving punishment that is not deserved (which would be trivial) or leaving punishment that is deserved. The text is cut off, but the argument aims to show that divine mercy (rahmah) in its true sense involves sparing from deserved punishment, thus supporting the same conclusion as previous proofs.

الرحمة
الثواب
العقاب
الإنعام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine mercy is primarily about rewarding the obedient for their obedience.
Correction: Razi argues that giving due reward is an obligation, not mercy, and extra reward is increase in beneficence, not mercy. True mercy is sparing those who deserve punishment.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea that mercy to the obedient is giving them their due reward?
A) Because giving a due reward is an obligation, not an act of mercy
B) Because the obedient do not deserve mercy
C) Because reward is always a favor
D) Because mercy can only be shown to sinners

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that divine mercy, by definition, cannot be simply rewarding the obedient but must involve forgiveness of those who deserve punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi argues that giving extra reward is not mercy but beneficence. When you receive blessings you don't deserve, do you recognize them as grace, or do you slip into thinking God owes them to you? What would change if you saw them as pure mercy?
?
If mercy is only for the undeserving, then why do you sometimes feel entitled to reward from God? What would it mean to let go of that entitlement and instead rely solely on His forgiveness?

Razi's Definition of 'Afw: Removal, Not Delay
Razi answers an objection about the meaning of 'afw with linguistic and textual evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi insists that 'afw (pardon) must mean complete removal or cancellation, and why it cannot be reduced to mere postponement of punishment.

Core Idea
Pardon in the Quran means cancellation, not delay.

Imam Razi addresses a common misunderstanding about the divine attribute of 'afw (pardon). Some have suggested that when God 'pardons' sins, He merely postpones punishment. Razi counters this by examining the Arabic root and Quranic usage, showing that 'afw fundamentally means removal of the trace of sin, not deferment. This lesson unpacks his linguistic and textual argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Quranic verses
Read the opening verses carefully. They describe God's pardon in the context of calamities. Note how some might interpret 'afw as postponement.
The objector's view is not stated directly but is implied in Razi's response.
2
Understand Razi's linguistic definition
Razi traces 'afw to its root meaning of 'erasing a trace,' i.e., complete removal. This is the foundation of his argument.
Arabic roots often carry concrete physical meanings that inform abstract concepts.
3
Examine the two Quranic proofs
Razi quotes verse 2:178 about retaliation and 2:237 about divorce. In both, 'afw means cancellation of a right, not delay. Notice how he stresses that delay was not intended.
4
Consider the rational example
Razi gives a simple analogy: a creditor who delays payment is not said to have pardoned; only cancellation is called pardon. This solidifies the linguistic point.
This example appeals to common usage.
5
Conclude that 'afw means cancellation
Based on the evidence, Razi establishes that 'afw must mean removal, not postponement. Reflect on why this distinction matters for understanding God's attributes.
If 'afw were only delay, it would imply eventual punishment, contradicting God's mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة والثلاثون
أَيْدِيكُمْ وَيَعْفُوا عَن كَثِيرٍ أي أنه تعالى قد يجعل المصائب إما على جهة الابتلاء أو على جهة العقوبة المعجلة بسبب ذنوبكم، ثم إنه تعالى لا يعجل العقوبة والابتلاء على كثير منها، وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمِنْ آيَاتِهِ الْجَوَارِ فِي الْبَحْرِ كَالْأَعْلَامِ إِن يَشَأْ يُسَكِّنِ الرِّيحَ فَيَظْلَلْنَ رَوَاكِدَ عَلَى ظَهْرِهِ إِنَّ فِي ذَلِكَ لَآيَاتٍ لِكُلِّ صَبَّارٍ شَكُورٍ أَوْ يُوبِقْهُنَّ بِمَا كَسَبُوا وَيَعْفُ عَن كَثِيرٍ﴾ أي أنه تعالى لو شاء إهلاكهم لأهلكهم إلا أنه تعالى لا يهلكهم على كثير من الذنوب.
والجواب : العفو أصله من عفا أثره أي أزاله، وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن يكون مسمى العفو هو الإزالة، ولهذا قال تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ عُفِيَ لَهُ مِنْ أَخِيهِ شَيْءٌ﴾ وليس المراد منه التأخير إلى وقت معلوم، بل الإزالة، وكذا قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَن تَعْفُوا أَقْرَبُ لِلتَّقْوَى﴾ ليس المراد منه التأخير إلى وقت معلوم، بل المراد منه هو الإسقاط المطلق.
وأيضًا: الغريم إذا أخر المطالبة، لا يقال: إنه عفا عنه، ولو أسقطه يقال: إنه عفا عنه، فثبت أن العفو لا يمكن تفسيره بالتأخير.
The thirty-fifth issue. '...your hands and He pardons much.' Meaning that God Almighty may bring calamities either as a test or as an immediate punishment for your sins, then He does not hasten punishment and trial for much of them. Likewise His saying: 'And among His signs are the ships sailing in the sea like mountains. If He wills, He calms the wind, so they become motionless on its back. Indeed in that are signs for every patient, grateful person. Or He destroys them for what they have earned, and He pardons much.' Meaning that if He willed to destroy them, He would destroy them, but He does not destroy them for many sins. The answer: Pardon (al-‘afw) originally means 'he erased its trace,' i.e., he removed it. Since that is the case, the meaning of pardon must be removal. For this reason God said: 'Then whoever is pardoned something by his brother' and the intent is not postponement to a known time, but removal. Likewise His saying: 'And that you pardon is nearer to piety'—the intent is not postponement to a known time, but rather absolute cancellation. Also: If a creditor delays demanding payment, it is not said that he pardoned him; but if he cancels the debt, it is said that he pardoned him. Thus it is established that pardon cannot be interpreted as postponement.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection regarding the meaning of 'afw (pardon) as used in the Quran. Razi begins by quoting two verses from Surah Ash-Shura that speak of God pardoning many sins despite causing calamities. The objector apparently claimed that 'afw here means postponement of punishment. Razi responds by defining the root meaning of 'afw as 'removing a trace,' i.e., complete cancellation. He supports this with two Quranic verses: first, in the verse on retaliation (Al-Baqarah 2:178), 'afw means cancelling the right to retaliation, not delaying it; second, in the verse on divorce (Al-Baqarah 2:237), 'afw means dropping the claim entirely. He then gives a rational example: if a creditor delays demanding payment, we do not say he 'pardoned' the debt; only if he cancels it. Therefore, 'afw in God's attributes must mean removal of punishment, not mere delay.

التأخير

Correction: Razi shows that 'afw linguistically and in Quranic usage means complete removal or cancellation, not delay.

Checkpoint

B) Removal or cancellation

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you living as if God's judgment is merely postponed, or have you truly accepted that your sins can be entirely wiped away? The creditor analogy: if someone forgave a huge debt you owed, would you not be overwhelmed with gratitude and never take it for granted? God cancels your eternal debt. Do you live with that same urgency and thankfulness, or do you treat divine forgiveness as a casual concept?
?
Razi proves that 'afw means removal, not delay. Does your heart tremble at the thought that if you do not repent, there is no 'delay'—only either punishment or genuine pardon? Are you relying on a false hope that God will simply postpone your reckoning, or are you seeking His true 'afw today?
