Third Proof for Mercy: God's Mercy Requires Forgiveness of Major Sins Before Repentance
Razi proves God's mercy requires forgiveness of major sins before repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi systematically argues that God's mercy necessarily includes forgiveness of major sins before repentance.

Core Idea
God's mercy is shown in forgiving the undeserving, not in rewarding the obedient.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents the third proof for God's attribute of mercy (rahma). He carefully eliminates all other possible meanings of mercy until only one remains: that God's mercy forgives unrepentant major sinners. This argument is a hallmark of Razi's analytical method, using division and refutation to isolate the correct interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the logical structure of the proof.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فإن قيل' which signals an objection.
2
Identify the two main divisions
Razi divides the recipients of mercy into obedient and disobedient. He then subdivides each category. Trace how he disproves each alternative.
3
Focus on the conclusion for the disobedient
After refuting other options, Razi concludes that mercy must mean refraining from deserved punishment for unrepentant major sinners. This is the core of the proof.
Note how he uses the opponent's own principles to eliminate minor sins and repentance.
4
Analyze the objection and reply
Razi anticipates an objection that mercy could be shown through creation, obligation, or mitigation. See how he counters each point.
5
Review the argument's structure
Look at the guided breakdown and learning flow to see the argument's logical tree. This helps understand the progression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : الآيات الدالة على كونه تعالى رحماناً رحيماً، والاستدلال أن رحمته إما أن تظهر بالنسبة إلى المطيعين الذين هم أهل الثواب، أو بالنسبة إلى العصاة الذين هم أهل العقاب، والأول باطل؛ لأن رحمته إما أن تكون عبارة عن أنه أعطاهم الثواب الذي هو حقهم، أو عبارة عن أنه يفضل عليهم بما هو أزيد من حقهم، والأول باطل؛ لأن أداء الواجب لا يسمى رحمة، لأن من أخذ حقه قهرًا لا يقال في مثل هذا الإعطاء إنه رحمة. والثاني باطل؛ لأن المكلف صار بما أخذ من الثواب كالمستغني، وتلك الزيادة تسمى زيادة في الإنعام ولا تسمى البتة رحمة، ألا ترى أن السلطان إذا أعطى الوزير مملكة ثم ضم إليها مملكة أخرى لا يقال لهذه الزيادة: إنها رحمة، بل يقال: زاد في الإنعام.
وأما القسم الثاني - وهو أن رحمته ظهرت بالنسبة إلى أهل العقاب - فنقول: هذه الرحمة إما أن تكون عبارة عن ترك العقاب الذي هو غير مستحق، أو عن ترك العقاب الذي هو مستحق. والأول باطل وإلا لكان كل كافر وظالم رحيما لأجل أنه ترك الظلم، أو لأجل أنه ترك الزيادة على ذلك الظلم الذي فعله، ولما بطل هذا بقي أنه رحمن؛ لأنه ترك العقاب المستحق، وذلك لا يتحقق في حق صاحب الصغيرة ولا في حق صاحب الكبيرة بعد التوبة، على قول الخصم - لأن عذابهما قبيح - فبقي أن رحمته إنما تظهر بالنسبة إلى صاحب الكبيرة قبل التوبة وهو المطلوب.
فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: أن رحمته إنما تظهر لأن الخلق والتكليف والرزق كلها تفضل ورحمة أو لأجل أنه تعالى يخفف عن عقاب صاحب الكبيرة؟ قلنا: أما الأول فهو يفيد كونه رحيما في الدنيا، فأين رحمته في الآخرة؟ مع أن الأمة مجمعة على أن رحمة في الآخرة أعظم من رحمة الدنيا. وأما الثاني: فعند المعتزلة التخفيف عن العذاب المستحق غير جائز. وأيضًا: هذا إشارة إلى تفسير الرحمة بترك الزيادة في التعذيب، ولو كان الأمر كذلك لكان أكثر الناس تعذيبًا للخلق وإيصالا للبلاء إليهم والزيادة على ذلك القدر، ومن المعلوم أنه باطل.
The third proof: The verses indicating that He is All-Merciful (Rahman) and Most Merciful (Rahim). The argument is that His mercy either appears in relation to the obedient who are the people of reward, or in relation to the disobedient who are the people of punishment. The first is invalid, because His mercy is either an expression that He gave them the reward which is their right, or an expression that He bestows upon them more than their right. The first is invalid because fulfilling an obligation is not called mercy, since if someone takes his right by force, such a giving is not called mercy. The second is invalid because the obligated person, having taken the reward, becomes as if independent; that extra is called an increase in beneficence and is never called mercy. Do you not see that if a sultan gives a vizier a kingdom and then adds another kingdom, this addition is not called mercy, but rather it is said: he increased in beneficence. As for the second division—that His mercy appears in relation to the people of punishment—we say: this mercy is either an expression of leaving off punishment that is not deserved, or of leaving off punishment that is deserved. The first is invalid; otherwise every disbeliever and wrongdoer would be merciful because he refrained from injustice or because he refrained from increasing his injustice. Since this is invalid, it remains that He is All-Merciful because He left off deserved punishment. This does not apply to the person of a minor sin nor to the person of a major sin after repentance, according to the opponent's view—since punishing them would be evil. So it remains that His mercy appears only in relation to the person of a major sin before repentance, and that is the desired conclusion. If it is said: Why is it not allowed to say that His mercy appears because creation, obligation, and provision are all grace and mercy, or because He mitigates the punishment of the major sinner? We reply: As for the first, it only makes Him merciful in this world—so where is His mercy in the afterlife? The community is unanimous that mercy in the afterlife is greater than mercy in this world. As for the second: according to the Mu'tazila, mitigation of deserved punishment is not permissible. Also, this implies interpreting mercy as refraining from increasing punishment, and if so, then the majority of people who inflict pain and suffering on others would be 'merciful' by not increasing it—which is obviously false.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage contains a full proof (hujja) demonstrating that God's mercy necessarily includes forgiveness of major sins before repentance. Razi structures the argument systematically. He divides the recipients of mercy into two groups: the obedient (people of reward) and the disobedient (people of punishment). He first disproves that mercy applies to the obedient: if mercy means giving them their deserved reward, that is not mercy because it is obligatory; if it means giving extra reward, that is called increase in beneficence, not mercy. Then he turns to the disobedient. Mercy towards them must mean either refraining from undeserved punishment (which is trivial, as everyone refrains from wronging others) or refraining from deserved punishment. The latter is the only plausible meaning. But this cannot apply to the minor sinner or the repentant major sinner, since the opponent (Mu'tazila) holds that punishing them is evil and thus not deserved. Thus, the only remaining possibility is that God's mercy manifests in forgiving the unrepentant major sinner. Razi then anticipates an objection: why not say mercy is shown through creation, obligation, and provision, or through mitigation? He answers: the first applies only to this world, but divine mercy in the afterlife is greater; the second (mitigation) is rejected by the Mu'tazila themselves, and if mercy means merely not increasing punishment, then even cruel people would be merciful.

رَحْمَان
رَحِيم
ثواب
عقاب
تفضل
إنعام
استحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's mercy is primarily about giving extra rewards to the obedient.
Correction: Razi argues that extra reward is called 'increase in beneficence', not mercy. Mercy in its strict sense involves forgiveness of deserved punishment, which is shown toward unrepentant major sinners.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does mercy toward the obedient not prove God's mercy in the afterlife?
A) Reward is given by obligation, not mercy
B) The obedient are already perfect
C) Mercy is only for punishment
D) The afterlife is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Razi's third proof concludes that God's mercy is most clearly manifested in forgiving major sins before repentance, as this alone involves refraining from deserved punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever committed a major sin and postponed repentance, assuming you could repent later? How does this passage challenge your assumption about God's mercy being dependent on your repentance?
?
If God's mercy is most perfectly shown in forgiving the undeserving, how should this reshape your attitude toward those who have wronged you? Will you forgive someone who has not apologized?
?
Do you truly believe that God's mercy covers even your unrepentant moments, or do you subconsciously think you must earn His love first? What would change in your daily life if you lived as if His mercy preceded your repentance?

Fourth Proof: The Verse on Forgiveness
Razi argues that the verse 'God forgives not shirk but forgives what is less for whom He wills' refers to major sins before repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses Quran 4:48 to argue that major sins before repentance can be forgiven by God's grace.

Core Idea
The verse speaks of grace, not obligation — so it applies only to unrepentant major sins.

Imam Razi presents his fourth proof from the Quran, focusing on Surah Nisa 4:48. The verse distinguishes between shirk and lesser sins, but Razi argues that the phrase 'for whom He wills' points specifically to major sins before repentance. This lesson unpacks his linguistic and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and Razi's claim
Start by reading the Quranic verse and Razi's initial argument that the verse cannot apply to minor sins or major sins after repentance.
Notice how Razi sets up the categories.
2
Understand grace vs. obligation
Razi distinguishes between forgiveness as a grace (tafaddul) and forgiveness as a right (istihqaq). The verse's negation and affirmation both concern grace.
This is the key doctrinal move.
3
Follow the linguistic analogy
Razi gives an analogy: if someone says 'He does not bestow 100 as a favor, but bestows 10 as a favor,' both parts refer to favor. Likewise, the verse's negation and affirmation both refer to grace.
4
Conclude the application
Since minor sins and major sins after repentance are obligatory forgiveness, they cannot be objects of grace. Therefore, the verse applies to unrepentant major sinners.
This confirms the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : قوله تعالى : ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ﴾ [النساء: ٤٨] ووجه الاستدلال به أن قوله تعالى : ولمن يشاء لا يجوز أن يتناول صاحب الصغيرة ولا صاحب الكبيرة بعد التوبة فوجب أن يكون المراد منه حمله على صاحب الكبيرة قبل التوبة.
إنما قلنا : لا يجوز حمله على صاحب الصغيرة ولا على صاحب الكبيرة بعد التوبة لوجوه :
أحدها: أن قوله تعالى : ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ﴾ معناه أنه تعالى لا يغفره تفضلا، لأنَّا أجمعنا على أنه يغفر استحقاقا، فإنه إذا تاب عن كفره وآمن فإنه تعالى يغفر له. وإذا كان قوله : ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ﴾ معناه أنه تعالى لا يغفر له تفضلا لزم أن يكون قوله : ﴿وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ﴾ أي ويغفر ما دون ذلك لمن يشاء الله تفضلا، حتى يكون النفي والإثبات متوجهين إلى شيء واحد. ألا ترى أنه إذا قيل : فلان يتفضل بمائة دينار ويعطي العشرة لمن استحقها كان الكلام فاسدًا، أما لو قال : لا يتفضل بالمائة ولكنه يتفضل بالعشرة، كان منتظماً. ولما كان غفران صاحب الصغيرة وصاحب الكبيرة بعد التوبة واجبًا امتنع حمل الآية عليهما، فتعين حملها على صاحب الكبيرة قبل التوبة.
The fourth proof: His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills.' The argument from it is that His saying 'for whom He wills' cannot apply to the person of a minor sin nor to the person of a major sin after repentance, so it must be taken as referring to the person of a major sin before repentance. We say: It is not permissible to apply it to the person of a minor sin nor to the person of a major sin after repentance for reasons: One of them: that His saying 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' means that He does not forgive it as a grace (tafaddulan), because we agree that He forgives it as a right (istihqaqan), for if one repents from his disbelief and believes, God forgives him. Since His saying 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' means that He does not forgive it as a grace, it necessarily follows that His saying 'and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' means He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills as a grace, so that the negation and affirmation apply to the same thing. Do you not see that if one says: 'So-and-so bestows a hundred dinars as a favor and gives ten to those who deserve it,' the speech is flawed; but if he says: 'He does not bestow a hundred as a favor, but he bestows ten as a favor,' it is coherent. Since forgiveness of the minor sinner and the major sinner after repentance is obligatory, it is impossible to apply the verse to them; thus it must be applied to the major sinner before repentance.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, based on the well-known verse from Surah Nisa (4:48) which states that God does not forgive shirk but forgives what is less than that for whom He wills. Razi argues that the phrase 'for whom He wills' cannot refer to minor sins or major sins after repentance because forgiveness for those is obligatory (according to the Mu'tazila's own principles). Therefore, it must refer to major sins before repentance, which are forgiven solely by God's grace (tafaddul), not as a right. He supports this by linguistic analysis: the verse's negation (does not forgive shirk) and affirmation (forgives what is less) must both be about grace-based forgiveness, not obligation. If shirk is unforgivable by grace, then what is less is forgivable by grace. Since minor sins and major sins after repentance are forgiven as a right, they are not objects of grace, so the verse cannot apply to them. Hence, it applies to the unrepentant major sinner, confirming the Asharite position that God may forgive major sins without repentance by His grace.

Key Terms
غفران
مغفرة
تفضل
استحقاق
شرك
كبيرة
صغيرة
توبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse automatically forgives any sin less than shirk for anyone.
Correction: Razi shows forgiveness is conditional on God's will (for whom He wills) and applies specifically to unrepentant major sins, not minor sins or repentant major sinners.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does the verse 'God forgives what is less than shirk for whom He wills' not apply to minor sins?
A) Minor sins are not mentioned in the Quran
B) Forgiveness of minor sins is obligatory, not by grace
C) Minor sins are unforgivable
D) The verse only applies to major sins after repentance

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof establishes that major sins before repentance are forgivable by God's grace, not by obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi distinguishes between forgiveness as a right and forgiveness as a grace. On which do you ultimately rely for your own sins? Is your hope in God’s grace or in your own repentance and deeds?
?
If God forgives an unrepentant major sinner solely by His will, what does that say about your own need to repent? Does this truth push you to more sincere repentance or tempt you to presumption? How will you respond today?

God's Mercy Towards the Unrepentant Major Sinner
Razi argues that God's mercy (Rahman) is towards the major sinner before repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam ar-Razi identifies the divine attribute 'Rahman' as specifically referring to God's mercy towards the major sinner who has not yet repented.

Core Idea
God's mercy ('Rahman') is most evident in withholding punishment from the unrepentant major sinner.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, in his work Al-Arba'in fi Usul al-Din, engages in a theological debate about the scope of God's mercy. He argues that the attribute 'Rahman' (the All-Merciful) cannot be applied to minor sins or to major sins after repentance, based on the opponent's own premises. This leads him to a distinctive conclusion: God's mercy is most manifest in His forbearance towards the major sinner before repentance. The passage presents this argument and then addresses potential objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic source passage and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the flow of reasoning: the initial conclusion, the objection, and the author's response.
2
Identify the main argument
Focus on how Razi derives the conclusion that 'Rahman' applies only to the major sinner before repentance. Note the reasoning about leaving off deserved punishment and why other cases are excluded.
The argument relies on the Mu'tazilite premise that punishing the minor sinner or the repentant major sinner would be unjust.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Look at the objection: why not interpret mercy as creation, obligation, and provision, or as alleviating punishment? Then see how Razi refutes each point, showing their shortcomings.
Razi uses the consensus of the Ummah and the absurd consequences of the opponent's interpretation.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to test your comprehension of the passage's main claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأجل أنه ترك الزيادة على ذلك الظلم الذي فعله، ولما بطل هذا بقي أنه رحمن ؛ لأنه ترك العقاب المستحق، وذلك لا يتحقق في حق صاحب الصغيرة ولا في حق صاحب الكبيرة بعد التوبة، على قول الخصم - لأن عذابهما قبيح - فبقي أن رحمته إنما تظهر بالنسبة إلى صاحب الكبيرة قبل التوبة وهو المطلوب. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : أن رحمته إنما تظهر لأن الخلق والتكليف والترزيق كلها تفضل ورحمة أو لأجل أنه تعالى يخفف عن عقاب صاحب الكبيرة؟. قلنا : أما الأول فهو يفيد كونه رحيما في الدنيا، فأين رحمته في الآخرة ؟ مع أن الأمة مجمعة على أن رحمة في الآخرة أعظم من رحمة الدنيا. وأما الثاني : فعند المعتزلة التخفيف عن العذاب المستحق غير جائز. وأيضًا : هذا إشارة إلى تفسير الرحمة بترك الزيادة في التعذيب، ولو كان الأمر كذلك لكان أكثر الناس تعذيبًا للخلق وإيصالا للبلاء إليهم و الزيادة على ذلك القدر، ومن المعلوم أنه باطل.
Because He left off increasing that injustice which He had done, and when this is invalidated, it remains that He is 'Rahman' because He leaves off the deserved punishment, and that is not realized in the case of the minor sinner nor in the case of the major sinner after repentance, according to the opponent's view — because punishing them is ugly — so it remains that His mercy only appears with respect to the major sinner before repentance, and that is the desired conclusion. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that His mercy only appears because creation, obligation, and provision are all favor and mercy, or because He alleviates the punishment of the major sinner? We say: As for the first, it indicates that He is merciful in this world, so where is His mercy in the Hereafter? while the community is agreed that mercy in the Hereafter is greater than mercy in this world. As for the second: according to the Mu'tazila, alleviating the deserved punishment is not permissible. Also, this is a reference to interpreting mercy as leaving off increase in punishment, and if that were the case, then the one who punishes people most and brings calamity to them would be the most merciful, and that is known to be false.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's attribute 'Rahman' (the Merciful) applies specifically to His not punishing the major sinner before repentance, not to minor sins or sins after repentance. The author begins by establishing that the previous interpretations are invalid, so what remains is that God is 'Rahman' because He leaves off the deserved punishment. This cannot apply to the minor sinner or the repentant major sinner because, according to the opponent (the Mu'tazila), punishing them would be unjust. Thus, mercy only appears towards the major sinner who has not repented. Then an objection is raised: perhaps mercy means the favors of creation, obligation, and provision in this world, or alleviating punishment. The author answers: the first only shows mercy in this world, but mercy in the Hereafter is greater, and the second is impossible because alleviating deserved punishment is not allowed according to the Mu'tazila. Additionally, if mercy meant leaving off increase in punishment, then the most severe punisher would be the most merciful, which is absurd.

رحمن
رحيم
صاحب الكبيرة
صاحب الصغيرة
العقاب المستحق
المعتزلة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'Rahman' and 'Rahim' are interchangeable or both refer to general mercy for all believers.
Correction: Razi specifies that 'Rahman' particularly denotes God's mercy in not punishing the major sinner before repentance, distinguishing it from other forms of mercy.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, to whom does God's attribute 'Rahman' primarily apply in the context of forgiveness?
A) The minor sinner
B) The major sinner after repentance
C) The major sinner before repentance
D) All believers

Key Takeaway
Razi pinpoints the attribute 'Rahman' as God's withholding of deserved punishment from the unrepentant major sinner, rejecting broader interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you consider your own sins, do you truly rely on God's attribute of withholding punishment before you repent, or do you assume His mercy means something else? How does this lesson challenge your concept of repentance?

?
How does understanding 'Rahman' as specifically withholding deserved punishment affect your urgency to repent? Do you take His mercy for granted, or do you realize the severity of deserving punishment?

The Fourth Argument: Forgiveness of Sins Less than Shirk and the Objection
Imam Razi argues from the Quran and rebuts an objection about the meaning of forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's relied-upon argument for God's forgiveness of all sins less than shirk and the objection that redefines forgiveness as deferment of worldly punishment.

Core Idea
Forgiveness of all sins less than shirk is certain, but only for some — not all.

Imam Razi presents his fourth and most relied-upon argument, citing the Quranic verse 'He forgives what is less than that' (4:48) to establish that God may forgive any sin except shirk. However, an objection is raised: perhaps 'forgiveness' here means only deferment of worldly punishment, not eternal pardon. Razi then defends his interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has three clear parts: the argument, the objection, and the response.
2
Identify the key verse and Razi's argument
Focus on the first sentence. Razi uses Quran 4:48 to argue that God forgives everything except shirk. Note the role of 'for whom He wills' in limiting the scope.
The argument is that the verse grants certainty that all sins less than shirk are forgivable, but not for everyone.
3
Examine the objection's redefinition of forgiveness
The objector argues that 'forgiveness' merely means removal of punishment, which could be temporary. Consider how this changes the meaning of the verse.
If forgiveness is temporary, the verse might refer to deferring worldly punishment, not eternal forgiveness.
4
Understand Razi's response
Read Razi's rebuttal carefully. He argues that the objector's interpretation would make the verse a warning about worldly punishment, which is not its apparent meaning.
Razi's response shows why the original interpretation is stronger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : وعليه الاعتماد - أن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ﴾ يفيد القطع بأنه تعالى يغفر كل ما سوى الشرك، وذلك يندرج فيه الصغيرة والكبيرة بعد التوبة وقبلها، ثم قوله بعد ذلك : لِمَن يَشَاء يدل على أنه تعالى يغفر كل هذه الأقسام لكن لا للكل بل للبعض، فثبت أنه تعالى يغفر كل هذه الأقسام للبعض، وذلك هو المطلوب.
فإن قيل : لا نسلم ان المغفرة عبارة عن أن لا يعذب العصاة في الآخرة، بيانه : أن المغفرة إسقاط العذاب، والمفهوم من إسقاط العذاب أعم من إسقاطه دائما، أو لا دائما، واللفظ الموضوع بإزاء القدر المشترك لا إشعار فيه لكل واحد من ذينك القيدين، فإذن لفظ المغفرة لا دلالة فيه على كون الإسقاط دائماً. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد أن الله تعالى لا يؤخر عقوبة الشرك عن الدنيا، لكن يؤخر ما دون الشرك عن الدنيا في حق من يشاء ؟
لا يقال : كيف يصح عقاب الدنيا على المؤمنين ؟ هذا التأويل ونحن لا نشاهد البتة اليوم مزية للكفار في لأنا نقول : تقدير الآية أن الله تعالى لا يؤخر عقاب الشرك في الدنيا لمن يشاء، ويؤخر عقاب ما دون الشرك في الدنيا لمن يشاء، فحصل بذلك تخويف كلا الفريقين ؛ لأن الكفار والفساق يخافون أن يعجل عقابهم في الدنيا، وإن كان لا يفعل ذلك بكثير منهم،
And the fourth [argument] – and upon it is reliance – is that His statement, the Exalted, 'And He forgives what is less than that' necessitates the certainty that He, the Exalted, forgives everything other than shirk, and that includes the minor and major [sin] after repentance and before it. Then His statement after that 'for whom He wills' indicates that He, the Exalted, forgives all these categories but not for everyone, rather for some. So it is established that He, the Exalted, forgives all these categories for some, and that is what is desired. If it is said: We do not concede that forgiveness is an expression of not punishing the disobedient in the Hereafter. Its explanation: that forgiveness is the removal of punishment, and the concept of removal of punishment is more general than its removal permanently or not permanently, and the word placed for the common meaning gives no indication of either of these two restrictions. Therefore, the word 'forgiveness' has no indication of the removal being permanent. If this is established, we say: Why is it not permissible that what is intended is that God, the Exalted, does not postpone the punishment of shirk from this world, but postpones what is less than shirk from this world for whom He wills? Let it not be said: How can punishment in this world be valid for believers? This interpretation [is problematic] and we do not observe at all today any advantage for the disbelievers in? Because we say: The estimation of the verse is that God, the Exalted, does not postpone the punishment of shirk in this world for whom He wills, and postpones the punishment of what is less than shirk in this world for whom He wills. Thus, a warning for both groups is obtained; because the disbelievers and the wicked fear that their punishment may be hastened in this world, even if He does not do that to many of them.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Razi's fourth and relied-upon argument, followed by an objection and its response. The argument: The phrase 'and He forgives what is less than that' (in Quran 4:48) indicates certainty that God forgives everything except shirk, covering minor and major sins both before and after repentance. The subsequent phrase 'for whom He wills' shows that He forgives these categories not for everyone but for some, establishing the desired conclusion that God may forgive any sin less than shirk for some people. Then an objection is raised: the objector denies that forgiveness necessarily means not punishing in the Hereafter; rather, it is the removal of punishment, which could be temporary or permanent, and the word 'forgiveness' itself does not imply permanence. Therefore, the objector suggests the verse might mean that God does not delay the punishment of shirk in this world (i.e., He hastens it) but delays the punishment of lesser sins in this world for whom He wills. Imam Razi responds by rejecting this interpretation: he argues that the verse would then mean that God either hastens punishment for shirk or delays it for lesser sins, creating a warning for both disbelievers and sinners, even if He does not always carry it out. This response is presented as 'we say' and 'let it not be said'.

مغفرة
إسقاط
عذاب
آخرة
دنيا
شرك
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse only promises deferment of worldly punishment, not forgiveness in the Hereafter.
Correction: Razi argues that the verse, combined with 'for whom He wills', establishes that God forgives any sin less than shirk in the Hereafter for some, not that He merely postpones worldly punishment.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise in the objector's redefinition of forgiveness in this passage?
A) Forgiveness only applies to those who repent
B) Forgiveness is removal of punishment, which may not be permanent
C) Forgiveness is only possible in the Hereafter
D) Forgiveness is the same as not punishing at all

Key Takeaway
Razi's fourth argument establishes that God forgives all sins except shirk for some people, as derived from Quran 4:48, and he refutes the objection that forgiveness means only deferment of worldly punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that God forgives all sins less than shirk for some, but not for all. Where do you stand? Are you among those for whom He wills forgiveness? What evidence in your life shows that you are truly seeking His forgiveness and not presuming upon it?
?
The objector tried to reduce forgiveness to a mere deferment of worldly punishment. Have you ever subtly reduced God's mercy to something temporary or conditional in your own heart, acting as if His forgiveness is only for the next life but not transforming how you live now? Confess honestly: in what area of your life have you treated God's forgiveness as cheap or distant?
?
Imam Razi says the verse creates a warning for both disbelievers and sinful believers (الكفار والفساق) that punishment may be hastened in this world. If you are a believer who sins, do you live with that reverent fear? Or have you become complacent, assuming that God's forgiveness means you will never face consequences in this life? What specific sin do you need to repent from today, taking this warning seriously?

The Generality of 'What is Less than That'
Imam Razi defends the generality of 'what is less than that' using essence, exception, and avoidance of ambiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's reasoning for why the Qur'anic phrase 'what is less than that' necessarily indicates universal forgiveness for all sins less than shirk.

Core Idea
The phrase points to an essence, so it applies to every instance of that essence.

In this passage, Imam Razi responds to an objection that the phrase 'what is less than that' (مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ) does not convey generality. He presents three arguments: it refers to the essence of sins less than shirk, it allows valid exceptions, and if it were not general the verse would be ambiguous. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objector first denies that the phrase indicates generality, then provides evidence by substituting 'all' and 'some'.
2
Analyze the essence argument
Imam Razi argues that the phrase refers to the essence (ماهية) of what is less than shirk. Since forgiveness is attached to this essence, it must apply whenever the essence is present. This is the core of his proof.
The essence is a single type, so the ruling applies universally.
3
Consider the exception argument
Imam Razi adds that it is valid to exclude any individual sin from the phrase, which proves it is general; exceptions presuppose a general rule.
4
Reflect on the ambiguity argument
If the phrase were not general, the verse would be ambiguous (مجمل), but the default is that the Qur'an is clear. This supports generality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن الغفران عبارة عن الإسقاط بالكلية، فلم قلتم : إنه لا يمكن حمله على مغفرة التائب ومغفرة صاحب الصغيرة؟
أما الوجوه الثلاثة الأولُ : فهي مبنية على أصول المعتزلة، وأنتم لا تقولون بها. وأما الوجه الرابع : فلا نسلم أن قوله : ﴿وَمَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ﴾ يفيد العموم.
والدليل عليه : أنه يصح إدخال لفظي الكل والبعض عليه على البدل، مثل أن يقال : ويغفر بعض ما دون ذلك، ويغفر كل ما دون ذلك، وذلك يدل على أن هذه الصيغة لا تفيد العموم، وإلا لكان الأول نقضا، والثاني تكريرا.
سلمنا : أنه للعموم، ولكنا نخصصه بصاحب الصغيرة والتائب ؛ لأن الآيات الواردة في الوعيد كل واحد منها مختص بنوع واحد من الكبائر مثل القتل والزنا والربا، وهذه الآية متناولة لجميع المعاصي والخاص مقدم على العام، فآيات الوعيد يجب أن تكون مقدمة على هذه الآية.
والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أنه لو كان المراد ما ذكرتم لزم أن يكون عقاب المشركين في الدنيا أكثر من عقاب غيرهم، وإلا لم يبق البتة في التقسيم والتفصيل فائدة، ومعلوم أنه ليس كذلك بدليل الاستقراء والمشاهدة.
قوله : لم قلتم بأن قوله : ﴿مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ﴾ يفيد العموم) ؟
قلنا : لأن قوله : ﴿مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ﴾ يفيد الإشارة إلى الماهية الموصوفة بأنها دون الشرك، وهذه الماهية من حيث هي ماهية واحدة نوعية، وقد حكم بأنه يغفرها، ففي كل صورة تتحقق فيها هذه الماهية وجب تحقق الغفران، فثبت أنه للعموم، وأيضا يصح استثناء كل واحد من المعاصي عنها عند الوعيدية، وصحة الاستثناء تدل على العموم. وأيضًا: فلأنه لو كان المراد بعض الأنواع مع أنه لم يحصل ما يدل على ذلك المعنى صارت الآية مجملة، والأصل عدم الإجمال.
قوله : آيات الوعيد أخص قلنا لكن هذه الآية أخص منها ؛ لأنها تفيد العفو في حق البعض دون البعض وآيات الوعيد تفيد حصول الوعيد في حق كل من فعل ذلك الفعل، وأيضًا قد بينا أن عند التعارض بين الوعد والوعيد وجب أن يكون جانب العفو والوعد راجحا والله أعلم.
الحجة الخامسة : أن يتمسك بالدلائل التي تمسك بها مقاتل في قطع العفو،
We concede that forgiveness is the complete removal, so why do you say that it cannot be applied to the forgiveness of the repentant and the minor sinner? As for the first three arguments, they are built upon the principles of the Mu'tazila, and you do not hold them. As for the fourth argument, we do not concede that His statement 'and what is less than that' indicates generality. The evidence for it is that it is valid to insert the words 'all' and 'some' alternately, such as saying 'and He forgives some of what is less than that' and 'He forgives all of what is less than that'. This shows that this formulation does not indicate generality; otherwise, the first would be a contradiction and the second a repetition. We concede that it is for generality, but we specify it for the minor sinner and the repentant because the verses of warning each are specific to one type of major sins such as murder, adultery, and usury, while this verse encompasses all sins, and the specific is given precedence over the general. So the verses of warning must take precedence over this verse. The answer to the first question: If what you mentioned was intended, then it would necessitate that the punishment of the polytheists in this world is greater than the punishment of others; otherwise, there would be absolutely no benefit in the division and detail, and it is known that this is not the case by the evidence of induction and observation. His statement: Why did you say that His statement 'what is less than that' indicates generality? We said: Because His statement 'what is less than that' indicates reference to the essence described as being less than shirk, and this essence, inasmuch as it is a single specific essence, and it has been judged that He forgives it, so in every case where this essence is realized, forgiveness must be realized. Thus, it is established that it is for generality. Also, it is valid to exclude any single sin from it according to the warning [verses], and the validity of exclusion indicates generality. Also, because if what was intended was some of the types while there is nothing to indicate that meaning, the verse would become ambiguous (mujmal), and the default is lack of ambiguity. His statement: The verses of warning are more specific. We said: But this verse is more specific than them because it indicates pardon for some but not others, while the verses of warning indicate the occurrence of warning for everyone who does that act. Also, we have explained that when there is conflict between a promise and a warning, the side of pardon and promise must be given priority. And God knows best. The fifth argument: To hold by the proofs that Muqatil held to in affirming pardon.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage contains a series of objections and Imam Razi's responses. First, an objector concedes that forgiveness means complete removal but asks why it cannot apply to the repentant and minor sinner. Imam Razi responds that the first three arguments are based on Mu'tazila principles, not his own. Then the objector denies that 'what is less than that' indicates generality (عموم), arguing that one can substitute 'all' or 'some' into the phrase, showing it is not inherently general. Imam Razi then concedes (hypothetically) that it is general but specifies it to minor sinners and the repentant based on warning verses (آيات الوعيد) which are specific and take precedence. Imam Razi answers this by arguing that if the objector's interpretation were true, it would imply polytheists face more worldly punishment, which is not observed. He then provides a detailed argument for the generality of 'what is less than that': the phrase refers to the essence (ماهية) of what is less than shirk, and since the judgment (forgiveness) is attached to that essence, it applies in every instance of that essence; also, the validity of exceptions from it proves generality; and if it were not general, the verse would be ambiguous (مجمل), which is not the default. Finally, he responds to the claim that warning verses are more specific by arguing that this verse is more specific because it limits pardon to some, while warning verses apply to all, and when promise and warning conflict, the side of pardon is given precedence. The passage ends with a mention of the fifth argument, which is incomplete.

Key Terms
عموم
ماهية
مجمل
استثناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'what is less than that' could be interpreted as referring only to some sins, not all.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the phrase refers to the essence of sins less than shirk, so it necessarily includes every instance of that essence.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi argue that 'what is less than that' indicates generality?
A) Because it is impossible that the verse could be ambiguous
B) Because the phrase refers to the essence of sins less than shirk, so it applies to all such sins
C) Because Muqatil said so
D) Because the Mu'tazila agree

Key Takeaway
The phrase indicates generality because it refers to the essence of sins less than shirk, and forgiveness is attached to that essence universally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you genuinely believe that your sin is included in the essence that Allah has promised to forgive? Or do you subconsciously think your sin is too great or too small for that promise? How does Imam Razi's argument force you to reconsider your exception?
?
What sin do you keep repeating because you assume it might be outside the scope of 'what is less than that'? Will you repent today with the full conviction that the verse includes it?
?
If you truly accept that Allah's forgiveness covers every sin less than shirk, what change would that make in your daily repentance and in your view of others' sins?

Specification by Warning Verses and Reconciliation
Imam Razi responds to the specification by warning verses and argues that the forgiveness verse is more specific and that promise takes precedence over warning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi resolves the apparent conflict between general forgiveness and specific warning verses, and how he prioritizes promise over warning.

Core Idea
The forgiveness verse is more specific than warning verses, and promise takes precedence.

The objector concedes that the forgiveness verse is general but argues that warning verses (آيات الوعيد) are specific to major sins and thus take precedence. Imam Razi counters that the forgiveness verse is actually more specific because it limits pardon to some, while warning verses threaten all who commit a sin. He also invokes the principle that when promise and warning conflict, the side of pardon is given precedence. Let's examine this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objector's specification argument
The objector accepts the generality of the forgiveness verse but claims it is specified by the warning verses, which are specific to each major sin. The specific takes precedence over the general.
2
Study Imam Razi's answer to the first question
Imam Razi responds to an earlier objection about the punishment of polytheists, showing that the division of sins into major and minor is meaningless if polytheists don't face greater punishment.
3
Analyze the argument that the forgiveness verse is more specific
Imam Razi argues that the forgiveness verse is actually more specific because it limits pardon to some sinners, whereas warning verses apply to all who commit the act.
4
Understand the principle of preferring promise over warning
When there is conflict, the side of pardon and promise must be given priority. This is a key principle in Imam Razi's theology.
This reflects the emphasis on Allah's mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن الغفران عبارة عن الإسقاط بالكلية، فلم قلتم : إنه لا يمكن حمله على مغفرة التائب ومغفرة صاحب الصغيرة؟
أما الوجوه الثلاثة الأولُ : فهي مبنية على أصول المعتزلة، وأنتم لا تقولون بها. وأما الوجه الرابع : فلا نسلم أن قوله : ﴿وَمَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ﴾ يفيد العموم.
والدليل عليه : أنه يصح إدخال لفظي الكل والبعض عليه على البدل، مثل أن يقال : ويغفر بعض ما دون ذلك، ويغفر كل ما دون ذلك، وذلك يدل على أن هذه الصيغة لا تفيد العموم، وإلا لكان الأول نقضا، والثاني تكريرا.
سلمنا : أنه للعموم، ولكنا نخصصه بصاحب الصغيرة والتائب ؛ لأن الآيات الواردة في الوعيد كل واحد منها مختص بنوع واحد من الكبائر مثل القتل والزنا والربا، وهذه الآية متناولة لجميع المعاصي والخاص مقدم على العام، فآيات الوعيد يجب أن تكون مقدمة على هذه الآية.
والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أنه لو كان المراد ما ذكرتم لزم أن يكون عقاب المشركين في الدنيا أكثر من عقاب غيرهم، وإلا لم يبق البتة في التقسيم والتفصيل فائدة، ومعلوم أنه ليس كذلك بدليل الاستقراء والمشاهدة.
قوله : لم قلتم بأن قوله : ﴿مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ﴾ يفيد العموم) ؟
قلنا : لأن قوله : ﴿مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ﴾ يفيد الإشارة إلى الماهية الموصوفة بأنها دون الشرك، وهذه الماهية من حيث هي ماهية واحدة نوعية، وقد حكم بأنه يغفرها، ففي كل صورة تتحقق فيها هذه الماهية وجب تحقق الغفران، فثبت أنه للعموم، وأيضا يصح استثناء كل واحد من المعاصي عنها عند الوعيدية، وصحة الاستثناء تدل على العموم. وأيضًا: فلأنه لو كان المراد بعض الأنواع مع أنه لم يحصل ما يدل على ذلك المعنى صارت الآية مجملة، والأصل عدم الإجمال.
قوله : آيات الوعيد أخص قلنا لكن هذه الآية أخص منها ؛ لأنها تفيد العفو في حق البعض دون البعض وآيات الوعيد تفيد حصول الوعيد في حق كل من فعل ذلك الفعل، وأيضًا قد بينا أن عند التعارض بين الوعد والوعيد وجب أن يكون جانب العفو والوعد راجحا والله أعلم.
الحجة الخامسة : أن يتمسك بالدلائل التي تمسك بها مقاتل في قطع العفو،
We concede that forgiveness is the complete removal, so why do you say that it cannot be applied to the forgiveness of the repentant and the minor sinner? As for the first three arguments, they are built upon the principles of the Mu'tazila, and you do not hold them. As for the fourth argument, we do not concede that His statement 'and what is less than that' indicates generality. The evidence for it is that it is valid to insert the words 'all' and 'some' alternately, such as saying 'and He forgives some of what is less than that' and 'He forgives all of what is less than that'. This shows that this formulation does not indicate generality; otherwise, the first would be a contradiction and the second a repetition. We concede that it is for generality, but we specify it for the minor sinner and the repentant because the verses of warning each are specific to one type of major sins such as murder, adultery, and usury, while this verse encompasses all sins, and the specific is given precedence over the general. So the verses of warning must take precedence over this verse. The answer to the first question: If what you mentioned was intended, then it would necessitate that the punishment of the polytheists in this world is greater than the punishment of others; otherwise, there would be absolutely no benefit in the division and detail, and it is known that this is not the case by the evidence of induction and observation. His statement: Why did you say that His statement 'what is less than that' indicates generality? We said: Because His statement 'what is less than that' indicates reference to the essence described as being less than shirk, and this essence, inasmuch as it is a single specific essence, and it has been judged that He forgives it, so in every case where this essence is realized, forgiveness must be realized. Thus, it is established that it is for generality. Also, it is valid to exclude any single sin from it according to the warning [verses], and the validity of exclusion indicates generality. Also, because if what was intended was some of the types while there is nothing to indicate that meaning, the verse would become ambiguous (mujmal), and the default is lack of ambiguity. His statement: The verses of warning are more specific. We said: But this verse is more specific than them because it indicates pardon for some but not others, while the verses of warning indicate the occurrence of warning for everyone who does that act. Also, we have explained that when there is conflict between a promise and a warning, the side of pardon and promise must be given priority. And God knows best. The fifth argument: To hold by the proofs that Muqatil held to in affirming pardon.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage contains a series of objections and Imam Razi's responses. First, an objector concedes that forgiveness means complete removal but asks why it cannot apply to the repentant and minor sinner. Imam Razi responds that the first three arguments are based on Mu'tazila principles, not his own. Then the objector denies that 'what is less than that' indicates generality (عموم), arguing that one can substitute 'all' or 'some' into the phrase, showing it is not inherently general. Imam Razi then concedes (hypothetically) that it is general but specifies it to minor sinners and the repentant based on warning verses (آيات الوعيد) which are specific and take precedence. Imam Razi answers this by arguing that if the objector's interpretation were true, it would imply polytheists face more worldly punishment, which is not observed. He then provides a detailed argument for the generality of 'what is less than that': the phrase refers to the essence (ماهية) of what is less than shirk, and since the judgment (forgiveness) is attached to that essence, it applies in every instance of that essence; also, the validity of exceptions from it proves generality; and if it were not general, the verse would be ambiguous (مجمل), which is not the default. Finally, he responds to the claim that warning verses are more specific by arguing that this verse is more specific because it limits pardon to some, while warning verses apply to all, and when promise and warning conflict, the side of pardon is given precedence. The passage ends with a mention of the fifth argument, which is incomplete.

Key Terms
تخصيص
وعيد
وعد
خاص
عام
تعارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Warning verses always take precedence over general forgiveness verses because they are specific.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the forgiveness verse is actually more specific in restricting pardon, and promise is prioritized over warning.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi say the forgiveness verse is more specific than the warning verses?
A) Because it contains the word 'all'
B) Because it indicates pardon for some people
C) Because it was revealed later
D) Because the Mu'tazila said so

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi resolves the conflict by arguing that the forgiveness verse is more specific and that promise takes precedence over warning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that Allah's promise of forgiveness is more decisive than the warning of punishment? How does this belief affect the urgency and sincerity of your repentance right now?
?
If you honestly examined your heart, do you rely more on fear of Allah's warning or hope in His promise? What would change if you shifted that balance today?
?
Consider a major sin you have committed. Are you using the generality of Allah's forgiveness as an excuse to delay repentance, or does the specificity of this verse push you to seek His pardon immediately? What step will you take today to act on this?

The Objection Against the Scope of Forgiveness
Razi presents an objection with four aspects about the scope of forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four aspects of the objection that divine forgiveness cannot extend to repentant sinners and minor sinners.

Core Idea
The objection tries to limit forgiveness using logic and linguistic analysis.

Imam Razi presents an objection against the view that forgiveness can cover all sins. The objector concedes the definition of forgiveness but argues that it cannot apply to the repentant or minor sinners. Four aspects are given, the first three relying on Mu'tazilite principles, while the fourth is a linguistic argument about the scope of a Quranic phrase.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection text
Read the Arabic passage of the objection once, then the translation. Note the concession and the four aspects.
The objector starts by conceding the definition of forgiveness.
2
Identify the four aspects
Find the first three aspects (based on Mu'tazilite principles) and the fourth (linguistic argument about generality). Understand how each supports the objection.
The first three are not detailed here, but they rely on principles you reject.
3
Analyze the linguistic argument
Focus on the fourth aspect: the phrase 'and what is less than that' is argued not to indicate generality. Note the substitution test with 'some' and 'all'.
The objector also concedes generality but then specifies it using threat verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن الغفران عبارة عن الإسقاط بالكلية، فلم قلتم : إنه لا يمكن حمله على مغفرة التائب ومغفرة صاحب الصغيرة؟ العموم. أما الوجوه الثلاثة الأولُ : فهي مبنية على أصول المعتزلة، وأنتم لا تقولون بها. وأما الوجه الرابع : فلا نسلم أن قوله : وما دُونَ ذَلِكَ) [النساء: ٤٨] يفيد العموم. والدليل عليه : أنه يصح إدخال لفظي الكل والبعض عليه على البدل، مثل أن يقال : ويغفر بعض ما دون ذلك، ويغفر كل ما دون ذلك، وذلك يدل على أن هذه الصيغة لا تفيد العموم، وإلا لكان الأول نقضا، والثاني تكريرا. سلمنا : أنه للعموم، ولكنا نخصصه بصاحب الصغيرة والتائب ؛ لأن الآيات الواردة في الوعيد كل واحد منها مختص بنوع واحد من الكبائر مثل القتل والزنا والربا، وهذه الآية متناولة لجميع المعاصي والخاص مقدم على العام، فآيات الوعيد يجب أن تكون مقدمة علة هذه الآية. والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أنه لو كان المراد ما ذكرتم لزم أن يكون عقاب المشركين في الدنيا أكثر من عقاب غيرهم، وإلا لم يبق البتة في التقسيم والتفصيل فائدة، ومعلوم أنه ليس كذلك بدليل الاستقراء والمشاهدة.
We concede that forgiveness is the complete cancellation, so why do you say that it cannot be applied to the forgiveness of the repentant and the forgiveness of the minor sinner? Generality. As for the first three aspects: they are based on the principles of the Mu'tazila, and you do not affirm them. As for the fourth aspect: we do not concede that His statement 'and what is less than that' [al-Nisa' 4:48] indicates generality. The proof of that is that it is valid to insert the words 'all' and 'some' into it as alternatives, such as saying 'and He forgives some of what is less than that' and 'and He forgives all of what is less than that', and that shows that this form does not indicate generality; otherwise the first would be a contradiction and the second a repetition. We concede that it is for generality, but we specify it to the minor sinner and the repentant, because the verses that concern threat each are specific to one type of major sin such as murder, adultery, and usury, while this verse encompasses all sins, and the specific is given precedence over the general, so the threat verses must take precedence over this verse. The answer to the first question: if what you mentioned were intended, then the punishment of the polytheists in this world would necessarily be greater than the punishment of others; otherwise there would be no benefit at all in the division and detail, and it is known that this is not the case by the evidence of induction and observation.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the author's answer regarding the scope of divine forgiveness. The objector begins by conceding that forgiveness means complete cancellation, then asks why it cannot be applied to the forgiveness of the repentant and the minor sinner. The objector notes that the first three of four aspects (not detailed here) are based on Mu'tazilite principles which the author rejects. The fourth aspect is that the Quranic phrase 'and what is less than that' (from verse 4:48) does not indicate generality. The objector supports this by showing that one can substitute 'some' or 'all' into the phrase, which would be contradictory if it were general. Then the objector concedes generality but says it is specified by threat verses that are specific to major sins, so the specific should prevail. The author answers the first question directly: if forgiveness applied only to repentant and minor sinners, then polytheists would suffer more punishment in this world than others, which is not observed. This refutes the objection by showing its consequence is false.

Key Terms
غفران
إسقاط بالكلية
عموم
خصوص
وعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector's concession of the definition of forgiveness implies agreement with the overall position.
Correction: The concession is only a starting point for the objection; the objector proceeds to argue that forgiveness cannot apply to repentant and minor sinners.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the fourth aspect of the objection?
A) The phrase 'and what is less than that' is general because it accepts substitution of 'all' and 'some'.
B) The phrase 'and what is less than that' does not indicate generality because substituting 'some' or 'all' leads to contradiction or redundancy.
C) The first three aspects are sufficient to refute Razi's position.
D) The threat verses are not specific.

Key Takeaway
The objection uses both theological principles and linguistic analysis to argue that forgiveness is limited to repentant and minor sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector's linguistic argument shows that the phrase 'and what is less than that' does not automatically indicate generality. Have you ever assumed a verse's meaning without careful linguistic analysis? How does this lesson challenge you to be more rigorous in your understanding of the Quran?
?
The objector conceded the generality but specified it using threat verses. Do you tend to focus on verses of hope or verses of fear? Which one has a greater impact on your daily spiritual state? How can you balance both?
?
The objection relies on the principle that the specific takes precedence over the general. When you face a situation where a general command seems to conflict with a specific one, which do you prioritize? Does your life reflect that principle?

Razi's Answer: A Refutation by Consequence
Razi gives a concise answer refuting the objection by an argument from consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the objection by pointing out its absurd consequence regarding the punishment of polytheists.

Core Idea
Razi turns the objection on its head by showing it leads to a false conclusion.

Having presented the objection, Imam Razi now answers it. He focuses on the first question and argues that if forgiveness were limited as claimed, then polytheists would suffer more punishment in this world than others, which is not observed. This refutes the objection by showing its logical consequence is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer text
Read the Arabic passage of the answer once, then the translation. Note that it is a direct response to the first question of the objection.
The answer is concise and uses a conditional argument.
2
Understand the conditional argument
Razi argues: If the objection were true (forgiveness only for repentant/minor sinners), then polytheists would suffer more punishment in this world than others. But this is not the case.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the use of observation
Razi uses 'induction and observation' (al-istiqra' wa al-mushahada) to show the consequence is false. Consider how empirical evidence is used in theological argument.
Observation of the world shows that polytheists do not necessarily suffer more.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن الغفران عبارة عن الإسقاط بالكلية، فلم قلتم : إنه لا يمكن حمله على مغفرة التائب ومغفرة صاحب الصغيرة؟ العموم. أما الوجوه الثلاثة الأولُ : فهي مبنية على أصول المعتزلة، وأنتم لا تقولون بها. وأما الوجه الرابع : فلا نسلم أن قوله : وما دُونَ ذَلِكَ) [النساء: ٤٨] يفيد العموم. والدليل عليه : أنه يصح إدخال لفظي الكل والبعض عليه على البدل، مثل أن يقال : ويغفر بعض ما دون ذلك، ويغفر كل ما دون ذلك، وذلك يدل على أن هذه الصيغة لا تفيد العموم، وإلا لكان الأول نقضا، والثاني تكريرا. سلمنا : أنه للعموم، ولكنا نخصصه بصاحب الصغيرة والتائب ؛ لأن الآيات الواردة في الوعيد كل واحد منها مختص بنوع واحد من الكبائر مثل القتل والزنا والربا، وهذه الآية متناولة لجميع المعاصي والخاص مقدم على العام، فآيات الوعيد يجب أن تكون مقدمة علة هذه الآية. والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أنه لو كان المراد ما ذكرتم لزم أن يكون عقاب المشركين في الدنيا أكثر من عقاب غيرهم، وإلا لم يبق البتة في التقسيم والتفصيل فائدة، ومعلوم أنه ليس كذلك بدليل الاستقراء والمشاهدة.
We concede that forgiveness is the complete cancellation, so why do you say that it cannot be applied to the forgiveness of the repentant and the forgiveness of the minor sinner? Generality. As for the first three aspects: they are based on the principles of the Mu'tazila, and you do not affirm them. The proof of that is that it is valid to insert the words 'all' and 'some' into it as alternatives, such as saying 'and He forgives some of what is less than that' and 'and He forgives all of what is less than that', and that shows that this form does not indicate generality; otherwise the first would be a contradiction and the second a repetition. The answer to the first question: if what you mentioned were intended, then the punishment of the polytheists in this world would necessarily be greater than the punishment of others; otherwise there would be no benefit at all in the division and detail, and it is known that this is not the case by the evidence of induction and observation.

?

What would it take for you to embrace Razi's approach of letting observation correct your theological assumptions?

Razi's Argument for Generality of 'What is Less Than That'
Razi argues that the phrase 'what is less than that' indicates generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three reasons Razi gives to prove that the Quranic phrase 'what is less than that' indicates generality.

Core Idea
Razi uses essence, exception, and default clarity to argue generality.

In this passage, Razi responds to an objection about the interpretation of Quran 4:48. He provides three distinct arguments to show that the phrase 'what is less than that' should be understood as general. Each argument relies on a different principle of usul al-fiqh and kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure of objection and response.
2
Identify the three arguments
As you re-read, identify the three distinct arguments: the essence (mahiyya) argument, the exception (istithna') argument, and the default clarity argument.
3
Understand each argument
For each argument, ask: How does this prove generality? The essence argument uses the nature of the quality; the exception argument uses logical implication; the clarity argument uses the principle of avoiding ambiguity.
4
Reflect on the reasoning
Consider why these arguments are compelling in a kalam context. How do they strengthen the interpretation of the verse?
This reasoning is typical of Razi's method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : لم قلتم بأن قوله : (ما دون ذلك يفيد العموم) ؟ قلنا : لأن قوله : ما دُونَ ذَلِكَ [النساء: ٤٨] يفيد الإشارة إلى الماهية الموصوفة بأنها دون الشرك، وهذه الماهية من حيث هي ماهية واحدة نوعية، وقد حكم بأنه يغفرها، ففي كل صورة تتحقق فيها هذه الماهية وجب تحقق الغفران، فثبت أنه للعموم، وأيضا يصح استثناء كل واحد من المعاصي عنها عند الوعيدية، وصحة الاستثناء تدل على العموم. وأيضًا: فلأنه لو كان المراد بعض الأنواع مع أنه لم يحصل ما يدل على ذلك المعنى صارت الآية مجملة، والأصل عدم الإجمال.
He said: 'Why do you say that His statement “what is less than that” indicates generality?' We said: because His statement 'what is less than that' [al-Nisa' 4:48] indicates reference to the essence described as being less than shirk, and this essence, inasmuch as it is a single specific essence, and the ruling that He forgives it has been made, so in every instance where this essence is realized, the realization of forgiveness is necessary; thus it is established that it is for generality. Also, it is valid to exclude every one of the sins from it according to the threat-based [interpretation]? [unclear], and the validity of exception indicates generality. Also, because if only some types were intended, without any indication of that meaning, the verse would become ambiguous, and the principle is [that there is] no ambiguity.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage provides the author's reasoning for why the Quranic phrase 'what is less than that' (from 4:48) indicates generality. The objector asks why the author claims it is general. The author gives three reasons: First, the phrase refers to the essence (mahiyya) of being 'less than shirk', and since the ruling of forgiveness is attached to that essence, wherever that essence is found, forgiveness must apply, making it general. Second, it is valid to make exceptions for any sin from this verse, and the validity of exception proves generality. Third, if only some types of sin were intended without any indicator, the verse would be ambiguous, but the default is lack of ambiguity. The author thus establishes that the verse is general in scope.

Key Terms
ماهية
عموم
استثناء
إجمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might confuse 'essence' with a concrete thing.
Correction: Here 'essence' refers to the abstract quality of being less than shirk, not a physical object.

Checkpoint
How does the author prove that 'what is less than that' indicates generality?
A) By citing consensus.
B) By showing that exception is valid, which implies generality.
C) By saying the verse is ambiguous.
D) By referring to threat verses.

Key Takeaway
Razi establishes that the phrase 'what is less than that' is general using three proofs: essence, exception, and default clarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin that is less than shirk, do you truly trust that God's forgiveness covers it, or do you secretly think your sin is too big or too specific? How does Razi's argument that the verse is general by essence and exception challenge your doubt?
?
The principle 'asl 'adam al-ijmal' means God's speech is not ambiguous. Do you approach the Quran with that confidence, or do you sometimes read your own uncertainties into it? What would change in your life if you fully believed every verse is clear in its intended message?
?
Razi uses the validity of exception to prove the generality of forgiveness. In your own heart, do you ever 'except' yourself from God's mercy—thinking He might forgive others but not you? What would it take for you to stop making that exception and accept the full generality of His forgiveness?

The Priority of the Forgiveness Verse Over Threat Verses
Razi argues for the specificity of the forgiveness verse over threat verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi considers the forgiveness verse more specific than the threat verses and the principle that promise takes precedence over threat in cases of conflict.

Core Idea
When promise and threat conflict, pardon takes precedence.

Imam Razi responds to an objection that threat verses are more specific than the general forgiveness verse. He argues that the forgiveness verse is actually more specific because it indicates pardon for some, while threat verses threaten everyone who commits the act. Additionally, he invokes a general principle: when a promise and a threat conflict, the side of pardon and promise must be preponderant.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice that this is a classic objection-answer structure.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector claims that the threat verses are more specific than the forgiveness verse. Consider what 'more specific' might mean here.
The objector likely means the threat verses apply to a narrower set of people or sins.
3
Understand Razi's two responses
First, Razi argues the forgiveness verse is actually more specific because it applies only to some, while threat verses apply to all who commit the act. Second, he states the general principle that promise is preponderant over threat.
The first response is about scope; the second is about a rule of weighting.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why promise (wa'd) might be given precedence over threat (wa'id) in Islamic theology. How does this affect hope for forgiveness?
This principle reflects Allah's mercy being predominant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : آيات الوعيد أخص قلنا لكن هذه الآية أخص منها ؛ لأنها تفيد العفو في حق البعض دون البعض وآيات الوعيد تفيد حصول الوعيد في حق كل من فعل ذلك الفعل، وأيضًا قد بينا أن عند التعارض بين الوعد والوعيد وجب أن يكون جانب العفو والوعد راجحا والله أعلم.
He said: 'The threat verses are more specific.' We said: But this verse is more specific than them, because it indicates pardon for some without others, while the threat verses indicate the occurrence of the threat for everyone who does that act. Also, we have shown that when there is a conflict between a promise and a threat, the side of pardon and promise must be preponderant. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is an objection-answer pair concerning the specificity of verses. The objector claims that the threat verses (wa'id) are more specific than the general forgiveness verse. The author responds that actually the forgiveness verse is more specific because it indicates pardon for some people (i.e., those whom God wills), whereas threat verses are general in threatening everyone who commits the act. Moreover, the author states a general principle: when a promise and a threat conflict, the promise side should be given precedence. This supports the author's position that forgiveness can be hoped for even for major sins.

Key Terms
وعيد
وعد
تعارض
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'more specific' means 'more limited in applicability', and thus the threat verses are narrower. Actually, the forgiveness verse is more specific because it applies only to some people, while threat verses apply to all who commit the act.
Correction: In this context, 'more specific' (akhaṣṣ) means the verse applies to fewer individuals (those whom God chooses to forgive), not that it restricts the category of sin.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the forgiveness verse more specific than the threat verses?
A) It only applies to minor sins.
B) It indicates pardon for some, while threat verses threaten all who do the act.
C) It is from the same chapter.
D) It was revealed later.

Key Takeaway
The forgiveness verse is more specific than the threat verses, and when promise and threat conflict, the side of promise and pardon is preponderant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a conflict between fearing God's punishment and hoping for His mercy, which side do you instinctively lean toward? In light of this lesson, what would it mean for you to truly give 'the side of pardon and promise' preponderance in your daily life?
?
This lesson argues that the forgiveness verse is more specific—it applies to those God wills to forgive. Do you live as though you are among those singled out for His pardon? What specific sin or fear have you been holding onto that you need to surrender to His specific promise of forgiveness?
?
Razi's principle turns the common assumption on its head: hope outweighs fear when texts conflict. Where in your heart do you hold a 'threat' against yourself or others—believing you are beyond forgiveness or that someone else deserves punishment? How can you act on the preponderance of pardon today?

Responding to Threat Verses: Hope Without Certainty
Razi responds that prior arguments are preferential, so hope for forgiveness remains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi qualifies the epistemic status of arguments for forgiveness as speculative hope rather than categorical certainty.

Core Idea
Forgiveness arguments are speculative, so we hope but cannot be certain.

Imam Razi anticipates an objection: opponents will cite threat verses to argue that forgiveness is impossible. In this short passage, he responds by clarifying that the earlier arguments for forgiveness are not certain but merely preponderant. This means we can hope for divine pardon without claiming absolute knowledge. Let's read the passage closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's concise style come through.
Notice that the passage has two main movements: restating the preponderance and qualifying its epistemic status.
2
Identify the counter-argument
The author says 'If they counter with the threat verses.' Understand the objection: threat verses (verses warning of punishment) might be used to argue that forgiveness is impossible.
Consider why this is a powerful objection in Islamic theology.
3
Analyze Razi's response
Focus on two points: (1) the preponderance remains as before, meaning the earlier arguments still hold; (2) but those preponderations are speculative (ẓannī), not decisive.
The word 'ظنية' is key: it indicates probability, not certainty.
4
Grasp the epistemic distinction
Because the arguments are speculative, the author says 'we hope for pardon' (رجونا العفو) and 'we are not certain of it' (ما قطعنا به). This distinguishes hope from certainty.
This is a crucial nuance in Islamic theology: not all doctrinal claims are made with qaṭʿ (certainty).
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does Razi make this qualification? How does it affect the overall argument of the chapter? It shows that some theological conclusions are based on preponderant evidence, not absolute proof.
This prevents misunderstanding that the author is backtracking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة والثلاثون فإن عارضوا بآيات الوعيد قلنا : الترجيح ما تقدم ولما كانت تلك الترجيحات ظنية رجونا العفو، وما قطعنا به.
The thirty-fifth issue: If they counter with the threat verses, we say: the preponderance is as previously stated, and since those preponderations are speculative, we hope for pardon, and we are not certain of it.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This short passage introduces the thirty-fifth issue. The author anticipates an objection: opponents might use threat verses to argue against forgiveness. The author's response is that the earlier arguments (the preponderations) are already in favor of forgiveness. However, these arguments are speculative (zanni), not decisive, so the author concludes that we can hope for forgiveness but cannot be absolutely certain of it. This qualifies the earlier discussion: the claim is not a categorical certainty but a hope based on probable evidence.

Key Terms
ترجيح
ظني
عفو
قطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is uncertain and contradicts his earlier proof.
Correction: The author clarifies that the earlier arguments were never claimed as certain; they are preponderant speculative proofs that yield hope, not certainty. This is a nuanced epistemic position, not a contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the epistemic status of the arguments for forgiveness according to this passage?
A) Certain (qaṭ'ī)
B) Speculative (ẓannī)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi shows that arguments for divine forgiveness are speculative, leading to hope without absolute certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own spiritual life, do you base your hope for Allah's forgiveness on arguments that you consider certain, or do you acknowledge they are speculative? How does Razi's admission of speculativeness affect the strength of your hope?
?
When you encounter verses of warning (āyāt al-waʿīd), do you tend to dismiss them with generic hope, or do you engage in weighing evidence like Razi does? What does your response reveal about your understanding of divine justice and mercy?
?
Razi says we are not certain of pardon. If you truly internalized that uncertainty, would it lead you to increase in obedience or to become careless? Which reaction is more consistent with the attitude of hope (rajā') as presented in this lesson?
